The Classical Proof for the Temporal Origination of the Body
The author presents the mutakallimun proof that body is originated because it cannot be free from accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and premises of the classical kalam proof that the body (and therefore the physical world) is temporally originated (hadith).

Core Idea
The body cannot be free from accidents, and whatever is not free from accidents must itself be originated.

This lesson presents the foundational argument used by the mutakallimun to prove that the physical world is not eternal. The proof is a simple syllogism, but its justification requires careful analysis of the nature of bodies and accidents. The author explains each premise, showing why bodies necessarily possess accidents and why those accidents must be originated, leading to the conclusion that bodies themselves have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main syllogism
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the proof is presented as a conditional argument: if body is not free from accidents, then it is originated.
The syllogism is stated at the opening: 'The body is not free of accidents, and whatever is not free of accidents is originated, so the body is originated.'
2
Understand the first premise: body is not free from akwan
The author explains that 'akwan' (occupancy of space) are accidents additional to the body's essence, and the body always has some location. Therefore the body is never free from these accidents.
This establishes that the body is constantly associated with originated things.
3
Understand why akwan are originated
Each kaw in can cease to exist (e.g., by the body moving), so it is susceptible to non-existence, hence it cannot be eternal. Therefore every kaw in is originated.
4
Follow the reasoning for the second premise and conclusion
Since the body cannot be free from originated things, those originated things must have a beginning (infinite regress is rejected elsewhere). Therefore the body itself must have a beginning, i.e., it is originated.
The author references proofs against accidents without beginning, which is a separate discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير دلائل أخرى في اثبات حدوث العالم
الحجة الأولى : - وهي الحجة القديمة للمتكلمين - أن قالوا : الجسم لا يخلو عن الحوادث وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، فهو حادث ، فالجسم حادث .
أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : فهو أن نقول : الجسم لا يخلو عن الأكوان . والأكوان حادثة ، ينتج : أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحوادث ،
أما قولنا : « الجسم لا يخلو عن الأكوان ، فهو بناء على مقامين : الأول : إثبات الأكوان . والمراد من الكون : حصول الجسم في الحيز وقد دللنا : على أن حصول الجسم في الحيز أمر زائد على ذاته
والثاني : بيان أن الجسم لا ينفك عن الأكوان . وتقريره ظاهر . لأن الجسم ما دام يكون جسماً ، فإنه يجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين . فإذا دللنا على أن حصوله في الحيز المعين زائد عليه. كان ذلك دليلا على أن ذات الجسم لا تنفك عن الأكوان . وأما قولنا : ( إن كل كون محدث ، فتقريره : أن كل جسم ، فإنه يصح خروجه عن حيزه . وبتقدير خروجه عن حيزه ، فإنه يبطل حصوله في ذلك الحيز ، وكل ما يصح عليه العدم ، امتنع أن يكون قديماً . فيثبت : أن الجسم يمتنع خلوه عن الأكوان ، ويثبت : أن كل كون محدث ، فثبت أن كل جسم ، فإنه يمتنع خلوه عن الحوادث . وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما يمتنع خلوه عن الحوادث فهو حادث . لأن تلك الحوادث . إما أن يكون لها أول ، وإما أن لا يكون لها أول والثاني باطل ، للدلائل المذكورة في إبطال حوادث لا أول لها ، فبقي الأول . فيثبت : أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، وثبت : أن كل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث، فهو حادث ، فوجب : أن يكون الجسم حادثاً . وهو المطلوب
Presentation of other proofs for establishing the temporal origination of the world. The first proof – it is the ancient proof of the mutakallimun – that they said: The body is not free of accidents, and whatever is not free of accidents is originated, so the body is originated. As for the explanation of the first premise: it is that we say: The body is not free of akwan (states of occupying space). And the akwan are originated, which yields: that the body is not free of accidents. As for our statement: 'The body is not free of akwan', it is based on two points: first, establishing akwan. And the meaning of kaw in (state) is the body's obtaining in a location, and we have proven that the body's obtaining in a location is something additional to its essence. Second, explaining that the body is not separate from akwan. And its exposition is apparent, because as long as the body is a body, it must be obtaining in a specific location. So if we prove that its obtaining in the specific location is additional to it, that is a proof that the body's essence is not separate from akwan. And as for our statement: 'Indeed every kaw in is originated', its exposition: that every body, it is possible for it to depart from its location, and assuming its departure from its location, its obtaining in that location is invalidated, and whatever is susceptible to non-existence is impossible for it to be eternal. So it is established: that the body's being free of akwan is impossible, and it is established: that every kaw in is originated, so it is established that every body, its being free of accidents is impossible. And we only said: that whatever is impossible to be free of accidents is originated, because those accidents: either they have a beginning, or they do not have a beginning. The second is invalid, due to the proofs mentioned in the refutation of accidents without a beginning, so the first remains. So it is established: that the body is not free of accidents, and it is established: that whatever is not free of accidents is originated, so it is necessary: that the body is originated. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the classical mutakallimun proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies, which is foundational for proving the existence of a Creator. The proof is a syllogism: (1) The body cannot be free from accidents (hawadith). (2) Whatever cannot be free from accidents is itself originated (hadith). Therefore, the body is originated. The author explains the first premise in detail. He states that 'the body cannot be free from akwan' – where kaw in (plural akwan) means the body's occupancy of a specific location. He shows that this occupancy is an attribute additional to the body's essence, and that the body always necessarily has some location. Thus the body is never free from akwan, and since akwan are originated (because they can cease to exist), the body is never free from originated things. Then the author explains the second premise: whatever is never free from originated things must itself be originated, because those originated things either have a beginning or not; the possibility of an infinite regress of accidents is refuted elsewhere (as in the proof against accidents without beginning). Therefore, the body must have a beginning. This proof is a standard kalam argument for the temporality of the physical world.

Key Terms
جسم
حادث
كون
الأكوان
حوادث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes that accidents are originated, which is what it tries to prove about bodies.
Correction: The proof does not assume accidents are originated without reason; it proves their originatedness separately by noting that each accident can cease (e.g., by movement), so it is temporal. The body's dependency on accidents then forces the body itself to be temporal.

Checkpoint
What are the two premises of the mutakallimun proof for the temporal origination of the body as explicitly stated in the passage?
A) The body is not free from akwan, and every kaw in is originated.
B) The body is not free from accidents, and every accident is originated.
C) The body is not free from akwan, and whatever is not free from akwan is originated.
D) The body is originated, and whatever is originated is not free from accidents.

Key Takeaway
The body must be temporally originated because it is never free from accidents that are themselves temporally originated, and an infinite chain of accidents is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every physical thing, including your body, is temporally originated and dependent on a Creator, why do you still live as if this world is permanent? What would change if you truly internalized that your very existence is a recent act of creation?
?
This proof shows that the body cannot be free from accidents—change is constant. How does your attachment to changing things (looks, health, wealth) reveal a denial of their originating nature? Will you today surrender one of those attachments to the One who originated them?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of an infinite chain. Your life is a finite chain of moments—each one a gift. If you knew that this moment could be your last, what would you say to your Creator that you have been avoiding?

The Second Proof: Bodies Are Receptive to Accidents
Author presents the second proof and its dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam proof for the creation of bodies, which relies on their universal receptivity to accidents, and the dilemma regarding the nature of that receptivity.

Core Idea
Bodies are never free from accidents, so they must be originated.

After establishing the first proof based on movement and rest, the author now presents a more general proof: bodies are receptive to all accidents. By examining whether this receptivity is identical to the body's essence or additional to it, we arrive at the same conclusion: bodies are created. This passage introduces the syllogism and begins the analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism carefully
Identify the three premises and the conclusion: bodies are receptive to accidents; everything receptive to accidents is not free from them; everything not free from accidents is originated; therefore bodies are originated.
This is a standard kalam syllogism.
2
Compare with the first proof
The first proof focused only on movement and rest (al-akwan). This proof covers all accidents like light, darkness, heat, cold, and shapes. Recognize why this is more powerful.
3
Examine the dilemma
The author asks: is the receptivity (qabiliyya) of the body to accidents identical to its essence or additional to it? Note that this dilemma is crucial for the argument's validity.
If identical, proving receptivity is originated directly proves the body's originatedness. If additional, we need two separate proofs.
4
Analyze each branch
Understand what each option implies. For the 'additional' branch, the author states two requirements: prove receptivity is originated, and prove the body is never without it.
The passage begins demonstrating the first requirement but is cut off.
5
Reflect on the incomplete proof
The text ends mid-sentence. Consider how you would complete the proof: why must the receptivity itself be originated? Hint: it depends on the possibility of the accidents it receives.
This encourages independent reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: الأجسام قابلة للحوادث، وكل ما كان قابلاً للحوادث، فإنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث، ينتج: أن الأجسام حادثة. واعلم أن الفرق بين هذه الطريقة وبين ما قبلها: أن الطريقة المتقدمة مختصة بالأكوان - أعني الحركة والسكون - وأما هذه الطريقة فإنها عامة في جميع الأعراض. وتقريره أن نقول: لا شك أن الأجسام قابلة للضوء والظلمة، والحرارة والبرودة، وللأشكال المختلفة، وللحركة والسكون، فنقول: قابلية الجسم لهذه الصفات، إما أن يكون عين ذات الجسم، وإما أن يكون زائداً عليها. فإن كان عين ذات الجسم، فإذا دللنا على أن هذه القابلية حادثة، لزم الجزم بكون الجسم حادثاً. وأما إن قلنا: أن هذه القابلية زائدة على ذات الجسم، فإذا دللنا على أنها حادثة، ودللنا على أن ذات الجسم لا يخلو عن هذه القابلية، فحينئذ يحصل لنا: أن ذات الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث. أما بيان أن هذه القابلية حادثة: فهو أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفة، فرع على كون تلك الصفة في نفسها ممكنة الوجود. لأن إمكان...
The second proof: that we say: Bodies are receptive to accidents, and everything that is receptive to accidents is not free from accidents, and everything that is not free from accidents is originated. It yields: that bodies are originated. And know that the difference between this method and what preceded it is that the preceding method was specific to the states (al-akwan) – I mean movement and rest – while this method is general to all accidents. And its explanation is that we say: There is no doubt that bodies are receptive to light and darkness, heat and cold, various shapes, movement and rest. So we say: The receptivity (qabiliyya) of the body to these attributes is either identical to the essence of the body or additional to it. If it is identical to the essence of the body, then if we demonstrate that this receptivity is originated, it necessitates the certainty that the body is originated. And if we say that this receptivity is additional to the essence of the body, then if we demonstrate that it is originated and we demonstrate that the essence of the body is not free from this receptivity, then at that point we obtain that the essence of the body is not separate from accidents. As for the demonstration that this receptivity is originated: it is that the possibility (imkan) of the essence being qualified by an attribute is dependent on that attribute in itself being possible of existence. Because possibility...
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniyya) for the createdness of bodies, which is more general than the first proof that focused on movement and rest (al-akwan). The argument is structured as a syllogism: Bodies are receptive to accidents (qabilah lil-hawadith). Everything receptive to accidents is not free from accidents. Everything not free from accidents is originated. Therefore, bodies are originated. The author then explains the difference from the previous method: the first was specific to certain accidents (movement and rest), while this is general to all accidents like light, darkness, heat, cold, shapes, etc. Then the author examines the nature of this receptivity (qabiliyya). He poses a dilemma: either the receptivity of the body to these attributes is identical to the essence of the body (dhāt al-jism) or it is additional (zā'id) to it. If it is identical, then proving that the receptivity is originated proves directly that the body is originated. If it is additional, then one must prove that the receptivity is originated and also that the body is never free from it, so the body is never separated from accidental things and therefore is originated. The author then begins to demonstrate that this receptivity is originated by analyzing the possibility (imkan) of the essence being qualified by an attribute, but this part is cut off. The key point is that the receptivity itself must be originated because it depends on the possibility of the attribute which is itself contingent.

Key Terms
al-hujja al-thaniyya (the second proof)
al-qabiliyya (receptivity)
al-a'rad (accidents)
al-dhat (essence)
al-imkan (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Receptivity is an accident like any other.
Correction: Receptivity is not an accident but a property of the body that enables accidents; the passage analyzes its relation to the essence, treating it as either identical or additional, not as an accident itself.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the receptivity of the body to accidents is additional to its essence, what must be demonstrated to prove the body is originated?
A) That the receptivity is eternal.
B) That the receptivity is originated and the body is always accompanied by it.
C) That the body is free from accidents.
D) That the accidents have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
The universality of this proof makes it a cornerstone for establishing the creation of all bodies, regardless of their specific accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, constantly changing through growth, aging, and sensations, this proof forces you to admit it must have been created. Does that intellectual certainty translate into a heartfelt awareness that your body is not your own, but a gift from Allah? How would you live differently today if you truly felt that every breath is borrowed?
?
The author argues that whether receptivity is part of your essence or an extra attribute, your body is never free from change and thus cannot be eternal. If you truly believe this, what does it mean for your attachment to worldly pleasures that rely on these very changes? Can you still chase comfort and safety while knowing they are signs of your contingency?
?
This proof is logical and universal, covering all accidents. But if you accept it with your mind, does it move your heart to thank the One who brought you into existence from nothing? Or is your faith still based on repetition rather than conviction? What would it take for you to live as if this proof were the most certain thing in your life?

The Second Proof: Generality of Accidents
Sanusi states the second proof for the creation of bodies and distinguishes it from the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the creation of bodies based on receptivity to accidents and how it differs from the first proof in scope.

Core Idea
The second proof applies to all accidents, not just motion and rest.

Sanusi now presents a second, more comprehensive argument for the createdness of bodies. While the first proof focused on motion and rest, this one extends to all accidents. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the depth of kalam reasoning about the contingency of the material world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully. Notice its concise, syllogistic structure. Then compare with the literal translation to ensure comprehension.
2
Identify the three premises
The proof consists of three linked statements. Identify each: (1) bodies are receptive to accidents, (2) whatever is receptive is never free from them, (3) whatever is never free is created. See how the conclusion follows.
This is a classic kalam syllogism.
3
Compare with the first proof
Reflect on the difference Sanusi highlights: the first proof was specific to motion and rest (akwān), while this proof covers all accidents. Why might this be important?
Generality strengthens the argument.
4
Consider the scope
Think about what 'all accidents' includes — e.g., colors, shapes, temperatures, tastes. This makes the proof applicable to any observable change in bodies.
The proof becomes universally applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : الأجسام قابلة للحوادث ، وكل ما كان قابلا للحوادث ، فإنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ينتج : أن الأجسام حادثة واعلم أن الفرق بين هذه الطريقة وبين وما قبلها : أن الطريقة المتقدمة مختصة بالأكوان - أعني الحركة والسكون - وأما هذه الطريقة فإنها عامة في جميع الأعراض.
The second proof: we say: bodies are receptive to accidents, and everything that is receptive to accidents is not free of accidents, and everything not free of accidents is created. This yields: that bodies are created. And know that the difference between this method and the one before it is that the previous method is specific to the states (akwān) – I mean motion and rest – while this method is general to all accidents.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thāniya) for the creation of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajsām). It begins with a concise syllogism: bodies are receptive to accidents (qābila li-l-ḥawādith); whatever is receptive to accidents is not free of them; and whatever is not free of accidents is created; therefore bodies are created. The author then clarifies the difference between this proof and the first one mentioned earlier. The first proof was restricted to the 'states' (akwān), i.e., motion and rest, whereas this proof applies to all accidents (a‘rāḍ) such as light, darkness, heat, cold, and shapes. This generalization makes the proof more comprehensive. The passage serves as an introduction to a more detailed explication that follows.

Key Terms
حجة
جسم
حادث
قابل
كون
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the second proof and the first proof?
A) The first proof uses the concept of being receptive to accidents, while the second uses the concept of not being free of accidents.
B) The second proof is general to all accidents, while the first was specific to motion and rest.
C) The second proof depends on the existence of a first event, while the first does not.
D) The second proof is only for bodies, while the first is for all substances.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates the createdness of bodies by arguing that their receptivity to all accidents entails they are not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is proven to be created because it is never free from accidents, what does that say about your constant need for the Creator who sustains every accident you experience each moment? How will you remind yourself of this reliance today?
?
The proof is general to all accidents, meaning every change you undergo—from your heartbeat to your thoughts—points to your createdness. Take one accident you experienced today (a feeling, a thought, a movement). Can you trace its existence back to a cause, and ultimately to the Creator? If so, how should that affect your gratitude and humility?
?
The first proof relied on motion and rest, but the second proof covers all accidents. What if you applied this reasoning to your own life: your existence is a series of accidents from birth to death. If you are not the author of these accidents, who is? Does accepting this truth change how you live the next hour? Commit to a concrete action that acknowledges your dependence on Allah.

The Two Possibilities for Receptivity
Sanusi presents two possibilities for the body's receptivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible relationships between a body's receptivity to accidents and its essence, and how this sets up the proof for the body's creation.

Core Idea
The body's receptivity is either its essence or additional — both roads lead to creation.

Sanusi begins his detailed proof with a simple observation: bodies are receptacles for accidents like light, heat, and motion. He then instantly sharpens the question: is this receptivity the very essence of the body, or is it something added to the essence? The entire argument hangs on this fork.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening observation
Notice that Sanusi takes the body's receptivity to accidents as a given. He does not prove it — it is self-evident.
The accidents listed are examples only.
2
Identify the two possibilities
He states two options: receptivity is either identical to the essence (ʿayn al-dhāt) or additional (zāʾid ʿalayhā). Understand the difference.
This dichotomy is exhaustive.
3
Follow the first path
If receptivity is the essence, then proving it is created directly proves the body is created.
No further steps needed in that case.
4
Follow the second path
If receptivity is additional, we need two separate proofs: that the receptivity is created, and that the body cannot exist without it.
This sets up the rest of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقريره أن نقول : لا شك أن الأجسام قابلة للضوء والظلمة ، والحرارة والبرودة ، وللأشكال المختلفة ، وللحركه والسكون ، فنقول : قابلية الجسم لهذه الصفات، إما أن يكون عين ذات الجسم ، وإما أن يكون زائداً عليها . فإن كان عين ذات الجسم ، فإذا دللنا على أن هذه القابلية حادثة ، لزم الجزم بكون الجسم حادثاً . وأما إن قلنا : أن هذه القابلية زائدة على ذات الجسم ، فإذا دللنا على أنها حادثة ، ودللنا على أن ذات الجسم لا يخلو عن هذه القابلية ، فحينئذ يحصل لنا : أن ذات الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث . أما بيان أن هذه القابلية حادثة : فهو أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفة، فرع على كون تلك الصفة في نفسها ممكنة الوجود . لأن إمكان اتصاف غيره به صفة عارضة له، من حيث إنه هو . وإمكان وجوده في نفسه ، اعتبار حال ذاته من حيث إنها هي . ومن المعلوم : أن اعتبار حال الشيء في نفسه، سابق على اعتبار حاله مع غيره . فيثبت : أن إمكان اتصاف غیره به، فرع على إمكان وجوده في نفسه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الحادث ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان إمكان وجوده في نفسه فائتاً في الأزل، كان إمكان اتصاف غيره به أيضاً فائتاً في الأزل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفات الحادثة: أمر حادث، ممتنع الحصول في الأزل. وأما بيان أن هذه القابلية من لوازم الذات: فلانا نقول: هذه القابلية، إما أن تكون من الأمور اللازمة لماهية الجسم، وإما أن تكون من العوارض المفارقة. فإن كان الأول فهو المطلوب، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: لما كانت هذه القابلية ممكنة الحصول لتلك الذات، كانت الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية. فقابلية تلك القابلية، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المطلوب، وإن كانت من العوارض المفارقة، كان الكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول. فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قابلية الجسم للصفات الحادثة: أمر حادث. وعند هذا نقول: إن كانت القابلية عين ذات الجسم، لزم من حدوثها، حدوث ذات الجسم، وإن كانت مغايرة لذات الجسم، وجب كونها من لوازم الذات، وحينئذ يحصل لنا الجزم بأن الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث، وقد سبق بيان أن ما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث. وحينئذ يلزم كون الجسم حادثاً. وهو المطلوب. وحينئذ يحصل لنا من هذا الدليل مطلوبان شريفان :
And its explication: we say: There is no doubt that bodies are receptive to light and darkness, heat and cold, different shapes, and motion and rest. So we say: the receptivity of the body to these attributes—either it is the very essence of the body, or it is additional to it. If it is the very essence, then when we prove that this receptivity is created, we necessarily judge that the body is created. And if we say that this receptivity is additional to the essence, then when we prove that it is created, and we prove that the essence of the body is not free of this receptivity, then at that point we obtain: that the essence of the body does not separate from accidents. As for the demonstration that this receptivity is created: it is that the possibility of the essence being described by an attribute is subsidiary to that attribute itself being possible in existence. Because the possibility of something else being described by it is an accidental property of it, insofar as it is itself. And its possibility of existence in itself is a consideration of the state of its essence insofar as it is itself. And it is known that the consideration of the state of a thing in itself precedes the consideration of its state with another. So it is established: that the possibility of something else being described by it is subsidiary to the possibility of its existence in itself. And when this is established, we say: the created thing is impossible of existence in pre-eternity (al-azal). And when the possibility of its existence in itself is absent in pre-eternity, then the possibility of something else being described by it is also absent in pre-eternity. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the possibility of the essence being described by created attributes is a created matter, impossible of occurrence in pre-eternity. And as for the demonstration that this receptivity is among the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence: we say: this receptivity—either it is among the necessary things for the quiddity (māhiyya) of the body, or it is among separable accidents. If it is the first, then it is what is sought. And if it is the second, then we say: since this receptivity is possible of occurrence to that essence, then the essence is receptive to that receptivity. So the receptivity of that receptivity—if it is among the necessary concomitants, then it is what is sought; and if it is among separable accidents, then the discussion regarding it is like the discussion regarding the first. So an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the receptivity of the body to created attributes is a created matter. And at this point we say: if the receptivity is the very essence of the body, then from its creation follows the creation of the essence of the body; and if it is distinct from the essence of the body, then it must be among the necessary concomitants of the essence, and then we obtain the certainty that the body is not free from accidents. And it has already been demonstrated that whatever is not free from accidents is created. And then it follows that the body is created. And that is what was sought. And then we obtain from this proof two noble objectives:
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is the detailed explication (taqrīr) of the second proof. The author builds the argument in several steps. First, he notes that bodies are receptive to various accidents (light, darkness, heat, cold, shapes, motion, rest). Then he distinguishes two possibilities: either this receptivity (qābiliyya) is identical to the body's essence, or it is additional to it. If identical, then proving the receptivity is created directly proves the body is created. If additional, then one must prove both that the receptivity is created and that the body cannot exist without it. The proof that receptivity is created is subtle: the possibility (imkān) of the essence being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Since a created accident is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), its possibility is also absent in pre-eternity. Therefore, the attribute's possibility of describing something else is also absent in pre-eternity, so the possibility of the essence being described by it is a created thing. Next, he proves that this receptivity is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the essence, using an argument against infinite regress (tasalsul). If receptivity were accidental, it would itself require an underlying receptivity, leading to regress. Therefore, it must be necessary. Finally, he concludes: if receptivity is the essence, the body is created directly; if not, the body is inseparable from it, and since it is created, the body must be created as well. The passage ends by noting that this proof yields two noble objectives (maṭlūbān sharīfān), though the details are cut off.

Key Terms

ذات
عارض
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what are the two possibilities for the relationship between the body's receptivity and its essence?
A) Either it is inseparable or separable.
B) Either it is identical or additional.
C) Either it is created or eternal.
D) Either it is necessary or contingent.

Key Takeaway
The body's receptivity to accidents is either identical to its essence or additional, and both paths ultimately lead to the body being created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your body is permanent, even though this lesson proves it is created and constantly changing? What would change if you truly internalized that every moment you are dependent on the Creator?
?
Sanusi shows that either way, the body is created. Can you point to a specific area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient or eternal? How can you remind yourself daily of your contingency?
?
The argument uses the body's receptivity to light, darkness, heat, cold, etc. Are you more attached to these temporary sensations than to the One who creates them? What practical step can you take today to prioritize your relationship with Allah over your comfort?

Allah's Existence
advanced
8 min
Proof that Receptivity is Created
Sanusi proves that the receptivity to created accidents is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle argument that the possibility of being described by created attributes is itself created, based on the priority of self-existence.

Core Idea
A thing's possibility to describe another depends on its own possibility — and created things have no possibility in pre-eternity.

Sanusi now delivers the first of the two promised proofs: that the receptivity to created accidents cannot be eternal. His reasoning is delicate: the possibility of an essence acquiring an accident depends on the accident's own possibility of existence. Since an accident cannot exist in pre-eternity, its possibility to describe another also vanishes in pre-eternity. Therefore, the receptivity is a created thing.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the dependency claim
Sanusi states that the possibility of an essence being described by an attribute is subsidiary (farʿ) to that attribute's own possibility of existence.
This is a logical priority: self before relation.
2
Understand the priority argument
He argues that considering a thing in itself is prior to considering it in relation to another. So the attribute's own possibility is before its ability to describe something else.
This is intuitive: what can exist alone is a precondition.
3
Apply to pre-eternity
A created accident (like a specific heat) cannot exist in pre-eternity (al-azal). Therefore its possibility of existence is absent in pre-eternity, and consequently its possibility to describe another is also absent.
Thus the receptivity to such an accident must be created.
4
Conclusion
So the possibility of the essence being described by created attributes is itself a created matter and impossible in pre-eternity.
This satisfies the first required proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is the detailed explication (taqrīr) of the second proof. The author builds the argument in several steps. First, he notes that bodies are receptive to various accidents (light, darkness, heat, cold, shapes, motion, rest). Then he distinguishes two possibilities: either this receptivity (qābiliyya) is identical to the body's essence, or it is additional to it. If identical, then proving the receptivity is created directly proves the body is created. If additional, then one must prove both that the receptivity is created and that the body cannot exist without it. The proof that receptivity is created is subtle: the possibility (imkān) of the essence being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Since a created accident is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), its possibility is also absent in pre-eternity. Next, he proves that this receptivity is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the essence, using an argument against infinite regress (tasalsul). Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the receptivity is eternal because bodies themselves are eternal.
Correction: Sanusi shows that receptivity to created accidents cannot be eternal because the accidents themselves have no possibility of existence in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi claim that the possibility of a body being described by a created accident cannot be eternal?
A) Because the body itself is created.
B) Because the accident's own possibility of existence is absent in pre-eternity.
C) Because the body's essence is simple.
D) Because the accident is inseparable.

Key Takeaway
The receptivity to created accidents is itself created because the possibility of a created accident describing something cannot exist in pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every capacity you possess—every skill, every potential for good or evil—is created by Allah in each moment? If so, why do you often act as if your abilities are self-sustained and independent of Him?
?
Since even the 'possibility' of your actions is not eternal but created anew, what does that mean for your pride in past achievements or your anxiety about future failures? Are you acknowledging your complete dependence on Allah for even the potential to change?
?
Sanusi proves that the very receptivity to change in the universe is created—nothing in creation carries its own possibility eternally. How does this truth confront your tendency to rely on created causes as if they were self-sufficient? Will you today repent from any hidden belief that anything besides Allah has intrinsic power or possibility?

Receptivity as a Necessary Concomitant and Final Conclusion
Sanusi proves reception is necessary and concludes the body is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the receptivity must be a necessary concomitant of the body's essence and how this leads to the body's creation.

Core Idea
Receptivity cannot be accidental — that would cause infinite regress; it must be a necessary property, making the body inseparable from createdness.

Sanusi now provides the second required proof: that the receptivity is not a separable accident but a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the body's essence. He argues by contradiction: if it were separable, an infinite regress would follow. Together with the earlier proof that receptivity is created, this forces the conclusion that the body itself is created — either directly or because it cannot escape what is created.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Sanusi asks: is the receptivity a necessary part of the body's quiddity, or a separable accident? Two options.
Necessary = part of what the body is.
2
Follow the first option
If necessary, then the goal is reached: receptivity is a necessary concomitant.
No further steps needed.
3
Examine the second option: regress argument
If separable, then since the receptivity itself is possible, the essence would need another receptivity to receive it, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible.
Regress is a standard refutation in kalam.
4
Conclusion of the second proof
Thus receptivity is a necessary concomitant. Now combine with the first proof: receptivity is created. So the body is either identical to a created receptivity (first path) or inseparable from it (second path). In both cases, the body is created.
Sanusi invokes the principle: whatever is inseparable from accidents is itself created.
5
Note the two noble objectives
The proof yields two results: the creation of the body and perhaps another objective (cut off in text).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقريره أن نقول : لا شك أن الأجسام قابلة للضوء والظلمة ، والحرارة والبرودة ، وللأشكال المختلفة ، وللحركه والسكون ، فنقول : قابلية الجسم لهذه الصفات، إما أن يكون عين ذات الجسم ، وإما أن يكون زائداً عليها . فإن كان عين ذات الجسم ، فإذا دللنا على أن هذه القابلية حادثة ، لزم الجزم بكون الجسم حادثاً . وأما إن قلنا : أن هذه القابلية زائدة على ذات الجسم ، فإذا دللنا على أنها حادثة ، ودللنا على أن ذات الجسم لا يخلو عن هذه القابلية ، فحينئذ يحصل لنا : أن ذات الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث . أما بيان أن هذه القابلية حادثة : فهو أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفة، فرع على كون تلك الصفة في نفسها ممكنة الوجود . لأن إمكان اتصاف غيره به صفة عارضة له، من حيث إنه هو . وإمكان وجوده في نفسه ، اعتبار حال ذاته من حيث إنها هي . ومن المعلوم : أن اعتبار حال الشيء في نفسه، سابق على اعتبار حاله مع غيره . فيثبت : أن إمكان اتصاف غیره به، فرع على إمكان وجوده في نفسه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الحادث ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان إمكان وجوده في نفسه فائتاً في الأزل، كان إمكان اتصاف غيره به أيضاً فائتاً في الأزل . فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن إمكان اتصاف الذات بالصفات الحادثة: أمر حادث، ممتنع الحصول في الأزل. وأما بيان أن هذه القابلية من لوازم الذات: فلانا نقول: هذه القابلية، إما أن تكون من الأمور اللازمة لماهية الجسم، وإما أن تكون من العوارض المفارقة. فإن كان الأول فهو المطلوب، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: لما كانت هذه القابلية ممكنة الحصول لتلك الذات، كانت الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية. فقابلية تلك القابلية، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المطلوب، وإن كانت من العوارض المفارقة، كان الكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول. فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن قابلية الجسم للصفات الحادثة: أمر حادث. وعند هذا نقول: إن كانت القابلية عين ذات الجسم، لزم من حدوثها، حدوث ذات الجسم، وإن كانت مغايرة لذات الجسم، وجب كونها من لوازم الذات، وحينئذ يحصل لنا الجزم بأن الجسم لا ينفك عن الحوادث، وقد سبق بيان أن ما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث. وحينئذ يلزم كون الجسم حادثاً. وهو المطلوب. وحينئذ يحصل لنا من هذا الدليل مطلوبان شريفان :
And its explication: we say: There is no doubt that bodies are receptive to light and darkness, heat and cold, different shapes, and motion and rest. So we say: the receptivity of the body to these attributes—either it is the very essence of the body, or it is additional to it. If it is the very essence, then when we prove that this receptivity is created, we necessarily judge that the body is created. And if we say that this receptivity is additional to the essence, then when we prove that it is created, and we prove that the essence of the body is not free of this receptivity, then at that point we obtain: that the essence of the body does not separate from accidents. As for the demonstration that this receptivity is created: it is that the possibility of the essence being described by an attribute is subsidiary to that attribute itself being possible in existence. Because the possibility of something else being described by it is an accidental property of it, insofar as it is itself. And its possibility of existence in itself is a consideration of the state of its essence insofar as it is itself. And it is known that the consideration of the state of a thing in itself precedes the consideration of its state with another. So it is established: that the possibility of something else being described by it is subsidiary to the possibility of its existence in itself. And when this is established, we say: the created thing is impossible of existence in pre-eternity (al-azal). And when the possibility of its existence in itself is absent in pre-eternity, then the possibility of something else being described by it is also absent in pre-eternity. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the possibility of the essence being described by created attributes is a created matter, impossible of occurrence in pre-eternity. And as for the demonstration that this receptivity is among the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence: we say: this receptivity—either it is among the necessary things for the quiddity (māhiyya) of the body, or it is among separable accidents. If it is the first, then it is what is sought. And if it is the second, then we say: since this receptivity is possible of occurrence to that essence, then the essence is receptive to that receptivity. So the receptivity of that receptivity—if it is among the necessary concomitants, then it is what is sought; and if it is among separable accidents, then the discussion regarding it is like the discussion regarding the first. So an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the receptivity of the body to created attributes is a created matter. And at this point we say: if the receptivity is the very essence of the body, then from its creation follows the creation of the essence of the body; and if it is distinct from the essence of the body, then it must be among the necessary concomitants of the essence, and then we obtain the certainty that the body is not free from accidents. And it has already been demonstrated that whatever is not free from accidents is created. And then it follows that the body is created. And that is what was sought. And then we obtain from this proof two noble objectives:
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is the detailed explication (taqrīr) of the second proof. The author builds the argument in several steps. First, he notes that bodies are receptive to various accidents (light, darkness, heat, cold, shapes, motion, rest). Then he distinguishes two possibilities: either this receptivity (qābiliyya) is identical to the body's essence, or it is additional to it. If identical, then proving the receptivity is created directly proves the body is created. If additional, then one must prove both that the receptivity is created and that the body cannot exist without it. The proof that receptivity is created is subtle: the possibility (imkān) of the essence being described by an attribute depends on the possibility of that attribute existing in itself. Since a created accident is impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), its possibility is also absent in pre-eternity. Therefore, the attribute's possibility of describing something else is also absent in pre-eternity, so the possibility of the essence being described by it is a created thing. Next, he proves that this receptivity is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the essence, using an argument against infinite regress (tasalsul). If receptivity were accidental, it would itself require an underlying receptivity, leading to regress. Therefore, it must be necessary. Finally, he concludes: if receptivity is the essence, the body is created directly; if not, the body is inseparable from it, and since it is created, the body must be created as well. The passage ends by noting that this proof yields two noble objectives (maṭlūbān sharīfān), though the details are cut off.

Key Terms
لازم
تسلسل
ماهية
عارض مفارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the receptivity could be a separable accident without leading to regress.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if separable, the very fact that the essence 'receives' it implies another receptivity, leading to an infinite chain.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi prove that the body's receptivity to accidents is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of its essence?
A) By citing a scriptural text.
B) By arguing that if it were separable, an infinite regress would follow.
C) By noting that all bodies have it.
D) By showing it is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The body's receptivity is a necessary concomitant, and since it is created, the body itself must be created — either directly or by inseparability from accidents.

?
Sanusi uses rigorous logic to reach a conclusion about Allah's existence. Are you willing to submit to such rational arguments even when they challenge your assumptions? What are you holding onto that might not withstand careful examination?
?
If the receptivity (qābilīyya) is necessary and inseparable from you, then your entire being testifies to your createdness. How does that testimony shape your relationship with your Creator? What would change if you lived every moment aware that your very existence proves you are not self-sufficient?

Third Proof: Impossibility of Change in the Eternal
Al-Razi classifies and refutes four eternal state possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third proof for the creation of bodies, which systematically refutes all possible eternal states using the principle that the eternal does not change.

Core Idea
If bodies were eternal, they could never change—but we observe change.

Al-Razi continues his arguments for the creation of bodies by examining what would be required if bodies were eternal. He presents a classification of all possible states in pre-eternity and shows each leads to a contradiction. This lesson walks through his reasoning step-by-step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the four possibilities listed.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the four possibilities
Al-Razi lists four mutually exclusive states bodies could have been in if eternal: solely aggregated, solely separated, sometimes each, or some each. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why the first two are impossible
If bodies were always aggregated, they could never separate, but we see separation. Similarly for always separated. This contradicts the principle 'the eternal does not cease'.
Key principle: الأزلي لا يزول
4
Refutations of the third and fourth
The third (sometimes aggregated, sometimes separated) implies infinite temporal events, already refuted. The fourth (some aggregated, some separated) fails because each part would be eternally fixed in its state, yet we observe change. See the commentary for details.
This proof returns to an earlier argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أجسام العالم ، لو كانت أزلية ، لكانت في الأزل ، إما أن تكون مجتمعة فقط، أو متفرقة فقط، أو تارة مجتمعة، وتارة متفرقة. أو بعضها مجتمعة، وبعضها متفرقة . والأولان باطلان، وإلا لزم أن لا يصير المجتمع متفرقاً، ولا بالعكس . لما ثبت : أن الأزلي لا يزول ، والثالث باطل . لأنه يلزم منه وجود حوادث لا أول لها . وقد أبطلناه . والرابع باطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك الذي كان مجتمعاً ، وجب أن لا يفترق ، وذلك الذي كان مفترقاً ، وجب أن لا يجتمع . لما ثبت أن الأزلي لا يزول . واعلم أن هذا الدليل بعينه عائد إلى ما تقدم ذكره
The third proof: The bodies of the world, if they were eternal, would be in pre-eternity either only aggregated, or only separated, or sometimes aggregated and sometimes separated, or some of them aggregated and some of them separated. The first two are false, otherwise it would necessitate that the aggregated never becomes separated, nor vice versa, because it has been established that the eternal does not cease. The third is false because it entails the existence of temporal events with no beginning, and we have already refuted that. The fourth is also false because that which was aggregated must not separate, and that which was separated must not come together, because it has been established that the eternal does not cease. And know that this very argument returns to what was mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the creation of bodies (أجسام). The argument is a classification and refutation of possibilities. Al-Razi first posits that if bodies were eternal (أزلية), in pre-eternity they must have been in one of four states: (1) purely aggregated, (2) purely separated, (3) sometimes aggregated and sometimes separated, or (4) partly aggregated and partly separated. The first two are invalid because if they were always in one state, they could never change to the other, but we observe change (aggregation and separation). The third is invalid because it would require an infinite sequence of temporal events, which has been rejected in earlier arguments. The fourth is invalid because each part, being eternally in its state, could not change, yet we see change. Thus, no state is possible, so bodies cannot be eternal. Al-Razi notes that this proof essentially returns to an earlier argument, meaning it relies on the same principle that the eternal cannot change.

Key Terms
أزلي
محدث
جسم
اجتماع
افتراق
الأزلي لا يزول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof fails because it assumes bodies must be in one of the four states in pre-eternity, but the argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the hypothesis of eternity.
Correction: The proof does not assume actual states; it is a logical breakdown of all possible states under the hypothesis of eternity, showing each leads to a contradiction. The hypothesis is therefore impossible.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, why is the third possibility (sometimes aggregated, sometimes separated) rejected?
A) Because it contradicts the observation of change.
B) Because it would require an infinite sequence of temporal events.
C) Because the eternal cannot change state.
D) Because it leads to a contradiction with the first two possibilities.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third proof uses a case analysis to show that if bodies were eternal, each possible state leads to a contradiction, so bodies must be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your own existence is self-sufficient, ignoring the obvious changes in your body and circumstances? How does Al-Razi's proof about the impossibility of change in the eternal force you to acknowledge that you are created, not self-existing? This week, when you notice change (aging, mood shifts, events), let it be a reminder that you are not eternal—only Allah is.
?
The proof shows that no possible state of eternal bodies can account for the change we see. If you are honest, do you sometimes resist the idea that you are a created being because you want to feel independent or in control? What is one belief or attachment you hold onto as if it will never end? How can embracing your own createdness free you from false security and bring you closer to the Eternal?
?
Al-Razi says 'the eternal does not cease.' Reflect on something in your life that you treat as permanent—a relationship, a role, a possession—even though everything in creation changes. Is there a part of you that fears losing it? How can trusting in the Creator who alone is eternal give you peace with the temporality of everything else?

The Fourth Proof: Motion Proves Bodies Are Created
Al-Razi argues that if bodies were eternal, they would be fixed in a specific place, but motion proves they are not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's fourth proof for the creation of bodies, which argues that motion in bodies proves they cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
An eternal body would be motionless; motion proves bodies are created.

In this lesson, we examine al-Razi's concise argument that bodies cannot be eternal. The proof hinges on the idea that an eternal body would occupy a specific place forever, making motion impossible. Since we observe motion in bodies, they must be temporal. This argument is a streamlined version of the classic argument from motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Note the logical flow: the argument starts with a conditional and ends with a conclusion.
The text includes a self-critique at the end.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the three main premises: (1) an eternal body would be in a specific place, (2) from a specific place it cannot move, (3) motion is observed in bodies.
Each premise has its own justification.
3
Understand the problem of an ambiguous place
Al-Razi argues that a determined body cannot be in an ambiguous place, so it must be in a specific place. Grasp why this matters for the proof.
This is a subtle point about individuation.
4
Notice the reliance on observation
The proof ultimately depends on sensory evidence of motion. Al-Razi acknowledges that the proof is not fully independent; it reduces to the argument from motion and rest.
This shows the empirical basis of the kalam argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that bodies cannot be eternal. Ask yourself: what would be the implication if bodies were not eternal? They must be created (hadith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان الجسم أزلياً ، لكان في الأزل حاصلاً في حيز معين . ولو كان كذلك ، لامتنع خروجه عن ذلك الحيز ، ولا متنـع كـونـه متحركاً . وحيث لم يمتنع ذلك ، علمنا : أن الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون أزلياً وإنما قلنا : إنه لو كان أزلياً ، لكان في الأزل حاصلاً في حيز معين : لأن الجسم هو الذي يصدق عليه أنه مقدار وحجم . وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حاصلا في حيز مبهم . لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه ، فهو معين في نفسه ، فحصول الجسم المعين في حيز مبهم ) في نفس الأمر : محال في العقل . فيثبت : أنه لو كان الجسم موجوداً في الأزل ، لكان حاصلا في حيز معين . ولو كان كذلك ، لكان حصوله في ذلك الحيز : أزلياً . ولو كان كذلك ، لا يمتنع العدم على تلك الصفة . لما ثبت : أن الأزلي يمتنع عليه العدم . ولما دل الحس على جواز الحركة على الأجسام، علمنا : امتناع كون الجسم أزلياً . وهذا الدليل لا يتم أيضاً في الحقيقة ، إلا عند الرجوع إلى دليل الحركة والسكون ، إلا أنه [ أقل ] مقدمات من ذلك الدليل
The fourth proof: If a body were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be in a specific place. And if it were so, its exit from that place would be impossible, and its being in motion would be impossible. And since that is not impossible, we know that the body cannot be eternal. And we say: If it were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be in a specific place because the body is that of which it is true that it is an amount and a volume. And everything that is so, it must be in an ambiguous place. Because everything that exists in itself is determined in itself, so the occurrence of a determined body in an ambiguous place is rationally impossible. So it is established that if the body existed in pre-eternity, it would be in a specific place. And if so, its occurrence in that place would be eternal. And if so, non-existence would be impossible for that attribute, because it is established that the eternal cannot cease to exist. And since sense indicates the permissibility of motion in bodies, we know that it is impossible for the body to be eternal. And this argument is not complete in reality except by recourse to the argument of motion and rest, except that it has fewer premises than that argument.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the creation of bodies. The argument proceeds as follows: If a body were eternal, then in pre-eternity it would have to occupy a specific place (حيز معين). Why? Because a body is defined as an amount and a volume; such a thing must exist in some place. A determined body in an ambiguous place is rationally impossible, so it must be in a specific place. If it is in a specific place eternally, then it cannot leave that place and thus cannot move. But we observe motion in bodies, so they cannot be eternal. Al-Razi notes, however, that this proof is not fully independent; it ultimately relies on the argument from motion and rest (i.e., the fact that bodies move and rest proves they are temporal). It is just shorter in premises. The key point is that the necessity of a specific place for an eternal body leads to immobility, which contradicts sensible evidence.

Key Terms
أزلي
جسم
حيز معين
حيز مبهم
متحرك
دليل الحركة والسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that bodies cannot be in a specific place.
Correction: The proof does not claim that bodies cannot be in a specific place; rather, it argues that an eternal body would be forever fixed in a specific place, which contradicts observed motion.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must an eternal body be in a specific place?
A) Because it is an amount and a volume.
B) Because it is determined in itself.
C) Because it cannot be in an ambiguous place.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that bodies cannot be eternal because their motion contradicts the impossibility of motion in an eternal fixed body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You are a body that moves. If every moving body is created and not eternal, then your own existence is contingent. Does your daily life reflect the urgency of this temporality, or do you live as if you are permanent? What one change today can remind you that you are a created being?

Al-Razi argues that an eternal body would be fixed and motionless. You are not eternal. Look at your hands—they move. Will you let that movement become a sign that you are created and accountable? How can you turn every physical motion into a moment of gratitude and awareness of your Creator?
?
The proof ends with the conclusion that bodies cannot be eternal. You are a body. What does it mean for you that your existence has a beginning? If your life has a starting point, it also has an end. Are you prepared for that end? What would you do differently if you knew you had only one day left to move?

Fifth Proof: Finitude of Magnitude Implies a Free Creator
Al-Razi lays out the fifth proof for creation via finitude of magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's fifth proof that bodies are created, which argues that their finite magnitude implies a free creator because the specific size is contingent and cannot be explained by a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Finitude of size points to a free agent, not a necessary cause.

Al-Razi presents the fifth proof for the creation of bodies, building on the finitude of their magnitude. This argument moves from the fact that bodies have finite size to the conclusion that they must be created by a free agent. The key insight is that a specific size could have been otherwise, so it needs a chooser, not just a cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two premises
The proof has a minor premise (bodies are finite in magnitude) and a major premise (everything finite in magnitude is created). Note that the minor premise is proved elsewhere in the discussion on finitude of dimensions.
Al-Razi references 'mas'alat tanahi al-ab'ad' for the proof of finitude.
2
Analyze the major premise
Al-Razi explains: finitude means the object could have been larger or smaller; its actual size is therefore contingent and requires a preponderant (murajjih) to choose that size. This preponderant cannot be a necessitating cause (mujib) because such a cause would relate uniformly to all magnitudes and could not prefer one.
The uniformity argument is crucial: a necessary cause cannot explain why this particular size rather than another.
3
Trace the conclusion
Therefore, the preponderant must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). And everything that is an act of a free agent is created (muhdath). Thus all bodies are created, establishing the desired conclusion.
The proof ends with certainty ('jazm') that every body is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الأجسام متناهية في المقدار، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو محدث . أما بيان الصغرى فهو مذكور في مسألة تناهي الأبعاد . وأما بيان الكبرى فهو أن نقول : كل ما كان متناهياً في المقدار ، فإنه يصح في العقل وجود ما هو أزيد مقداراً منه ، ووجود ما هو أنقص مقداراً منه . وإذا كان كذلك ، كان اختصاصه بذلك القدر المعين ، دون الزائد ودون الناقص ممكناً . وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مرجح ، وذلك المرجح يمتنع أن يكون موجباً . لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع المقادير على السوية . فلم يكن كونه موجباً المقدار معين ، أولى من كونه موجباً (۲) لغير ذلك المقدار ، فوجب أن يكون المؤثر فيه ، فاعلاً مختاراً . وكل ما كان فعلا لفاعل مختار ، فهو محـدث فيثبت : أن كل جسم ، فإنه متناهي [ المقدار، وكل متناهي المقدار (۳) ] فإن اختصاصه بذلك القدر يكون من الجائزات. وكل ما كان كذلك ، فإنه يمتنع رجحانه على غيره ، إلا بفعل الفاعل المختار . وكل ما كان فعلا للفاعل المختار ، فهو محدث . وعند ظهور هذه المقدمات يحصل الجزم بأن كل جسم فهو محدث . والله أعلم
The fifth proof: Bodies are finite in magnitude, and everything that is such is created. As for the demonstration of the minor premise, it is mentioned in the issue of the finitude of dimensions. As for the demonstration of the major premise, we say: Everything that is finite in magnitude, it is rationally valid for there to exist something greater in magnitude than it and something lesser. If that is so, its specification with that particular magnitude, to the exclusion of the greater and the lesser, is possible. And every possible thing must have a preponderant, and that preponderant cannot be a necessitating cause, because the relation of the necessitating cause to all magnitudes is uniform. Thus its being necessitating for a particular magnitude is not more likely than its being necessitating for another magnitude. Therefore, the effecting agent must be a free agent. And everything that is an act of a free agent is created. So it is established that every body is finite in magnitude, and every finite magnitude, its specification with that magnitude is among contingent things. And everything that is such, its preponderance over others is impossible except by the act of the free agent. And everything that is an act of the free agent is created. And upon the appearance of these premises, certainty is obtained that every body is created. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the creation of bodies, based on the finitude of their magnitude. The argument has two premises: a minor premise that bodies are finite in magnitude (proved elsewhere), and a major premise that everything finite in magnitude is created. Al-Razi explains the major premise: a finite body could have been larger or smaller; its specific size is therefore contingent, requiring a preponderant (مرجح) to choose that size. This preponderant cannot be a necessitating cause (موجب) because a necessitating cause would have a uniform relation to all magnitudes and could not prefer one. Thus, the preponderant must be a free agent (فاعل مختار). And an act of a free agent is created (محدث). So bodies are created. The proof relies on the principle of sufficient reason and the impossibility of a necessary cause producing a contingent specificity.

Key Terms
متناهي المقدار
ممكن
مرجح
موجب
فاعل مختار
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that finitude itself directly causes creation.
Correction: Al-Razi does not claim finitude causes creation; rather, finitude makes the specific size contingent, which points to a free agent who chose that size.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, why cannot the preponderant be a necessitating cause (موجب)?
A) Because a necessitating cause cannot act freely.
B) Because it would have a uniform relation to all magnitudes and could not prefer one.
C) Because it would be eternal.
D) Because it would require an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fifth proof shows that the finitude of bodies implies a free creator because their specific size is contingent and cannot be explained by a necessitating cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change if you truly believed that every particular of your size, your lifespan, your abilities was deliberately selected by a free Creator?
?
Al-Razi argues that a necessitating cause cannot explain why things are one way rather than another, so a free agent is required. In your own life, do you rely on explanations that reduce events to necessity—'just biology,' 'just society'—without acknowledging the free choice of the Creator? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that every detail is a free gift, not a mechanical outcome?
?
This proof shows that even the magnitude of bodies is contingent, chosen from among possibilities. Do you live as if your own existence is a necessary consequence of causes, or as a free act of God's choice? When you look at your body's limitations (height, health, etc.), do you see necessity or divine selection? How can you respond in gratitude to a God who did not have to create you with these specifics, but freely chose to?

The Fifth Argument: Finite Bodies Require a Free Agent
Presents the fifth argument that finite bodies are temporally produced due to requiring a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why finite bodies must be temporally produced because their specific magnitude requires a free agent rather than a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
A finite body's specific magnitude is contingent, so it must be chosen by a free agent.

This lesson presents a classical kalam argument for the temporal creation of bodies. It focuses on the finitude of bodies and the necessity of a free agent to account for their particular magnitudes. Follow the logical steps from minor premise to the conclusion that every body is temporally produced.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation carefully. Notice how the argument builds from the finitude of bodies to the necessity of a free agent.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the minor and major premises
Find the statement of the minor premise (bodies are finite) and the major premise (everything finite is temporally produced). Note how the major premise is explained in detail.
The minor premise is referenced to another discussion; focus on the major premise reasoning.
3
Understand the contingency of magnitude
The argument says: for any finite magnitude, a larger and smaller are conceivable, so the actual magnitude is not necessary but possible. Grasp why this leads to the need for a preponderating factor.
4
See why a necessitating cause fails
The text argues that a necessitating cause (mujib) relates equally to all magnitudes, so it cannot specify one. Recognize that this forces the cause to be a free agent.
This is the crucial step: the preponderating factor must be a free agent.
5
Conclude the argument
Follow the chain: free agent's act is temporally produced, so the body is temporally produced. Confirm that the premises yield certainty.
The argument ends with 'upon the appearance of these premises, certainty is obtained'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الأجسام متناهية في المقدار، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو محدث . أما بيان الصغرى فهو مذكور في مسألة تناهي الأبعاد . وأما بيان الكبرى فهو أن نقول : كل ما كان متناهياً في المقدار، فإنه يصح في العقل وجود ما هو أزيد مقداراً منه، ووجود ما هو أنقص مقداراً منه . وإذا كان كذلك، كان اختصاصه بذلك القدر المعين، دون الزائد ودون الناقص ممكناً . وكل ممكن فلا بد له من مرجح، وذلك المرجح يمتنع أن يكون موجباً . لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع المقادير على السوية . فلم يكن كونه موجباً المقدار معين، أولى من كونه موجباً لغير ذلك المقدار، فوجب أن يكون المؤثر فيه، فاعلاً مختاراً . وكل ما كان فعلا لفاعل مختار، فهو محدث فيثبت : أن كل جسم، فإنه متناهي المقدار، وكل متناهي المقدار فإن اختصاصه بذلك القدر يكون من الجائزات. وكل ما كان كذلك، فإنه يمتنع رجحانه على غيره، إلا بفعل الفاعل المختار . وكل ما كان فعلا للفاعل المختار، فهو محدث . وعند ظهور هذه المقدمات يحصل الجزم بأن كل جسم فهو محدث . والله أعلم
The fifth proof: Bodies are finite in magnitude, and everything that is finite is temporally produced. As for the explanation of the minor premise, it is mentioned in the issue of the finitude of dimensions. And as for the explanation of the major premise, we say: Everything that is finite in magnitude, it is rationally valid for there to exist something greater in magnitude than it, and for there to exist something lesser in magnitude than it. And if that is the case, then its being specifically restricted to that particular magnitude, to the exclusion of the greater and the lesser, is possible. And everything possible requires a preponderating factor. And that preponderating factor cannot be a necessitating cause (mujib), because the relation of the necessitating cause to all magnitudes is equal. Thus it would not be more likely for it to be a necessitating cause for a specific magnitude than for it to be a necessitating cause for another magnitude. Therefore, the effective agent (mu'aththir) must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). And everything that is an act of a free agent is temporally produced. Thus it is established that every body is finite in magnitude, and every finite in magnitude, its being specifically restricted to that magnitude is among contingent things (ja'izat). And everything that is such, its preponderance over others cannot occur except by the act of the free agent. And everything that is an act of the free agent is temporally produced. Upon the appearance of these premises, certainty is obtained that every body is temporally produced. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the temporal creation (huduth) of bodies. It is a kalam proof based on the finitude of bodies. The argument proceeds in several steps: First, it asserts the minor premise that all bodies are finite in magnitude. Second, it claims that everything finite is temporally produced. The explanation of the minor premise is referenced to another issue (finitude of dimensions). The major premise is explained in detail: For any finite magnitude, reason can conceive a larger or smaller magnitude; therefore the actual magnitude is not necessary but contingent (mumkin). A contingent thing requires a preponderating factor (murajjih) to specify it over alternatives. This preponderating factor cannot be a necessitating cause (mujib), because a necessitating cause relates equally to all magnitudes and cannot explain why one specific magnitude is chosen. Hence the cause must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) who acts by choice. Since an act of a free agent is temporally produced, the body—being an effect of such an act—is temporally produced. The argument concludes that these premises yield certainty that every body is temporally produced.

Key Terms
متناهي
محدث
مرجح
موجب
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preponderating factor could be a necessitating cause (mujib) that necessitates a specific magnitude directly.
Correction: The argument shows that a necessitating cause cannot specify one magnitude because its relation to all magnitudes is equal; it would necessitate all equally, which is impossible. Therefore, only a free agent can choose one magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, why can't the preponderating factor be a necessitating cause (mujib)?
A) Because it would require an infinite regress.
B) Because its relation to all magnitudes is equal.
C) Because a body cannot be a cause.
D) Because it would itself be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument proves that bodies are temporally produced because their specific magnitude is contingent and can only be explained by a free agent, not a necessitating cause.

?
If everything contingent requires a preponderating factor, what does the fact that you are reading this lesson right now—this specific moment—tell you about the One who preponderated it over infinite alternatives? How should that impact your response to the free Agent?
?
The argument shows that a necessitating cause cannot explain specificity because it relates equally to all options. In your life, do you sometimes feel like a machine driven by causes, or do you recognize the hand of a free Agent choosing your path? How does that change your attitude towards trials and blessings?
