The Sixth Argument: Similar Bodies and Specific Locations
Al-Razi presents the sixth argument for bodily temporality from similarity and spatial specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument from the similarity of bodies for the temporal creation of the world and why it necessitates a free agent.

Core Idea
Similar bodies cannot explain their diverse locations — only a free agent can.

Al-Razi presents a rigorous metaphysical argument that starts with the observed similarity of all bodies. By analyzing why each body occupies a specific space, he demonstrates that this cannot be due to the bodies themselves or a necessitating cause. This leads to the conclusion that a free agent must assign each body its location, implying the bodies are temporally produced.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of similarity
Read the opening lines where al-Razi asserts that bodies are similar in complete essence. Note the proof via equality in extension and spatial occupation.
2
Trace the spatial specification argument
Follow the reasoning that specific locations cannot be from bodies' own essences (or all would be same) and must come from an external cause.
3
Eliminate necessitating cause and affirm free agent
See why the cause cannot be a necessitating cause (either body or non-body) and must be a free agent, leading to temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : نقول : أجسام العالم متماثلة في تمام الماهية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، وجب كونها محدثة . بيان الأول : ما ذكرنا من أن الأجسام متساوية في الحجمية والتحيز، فلو خالف بعضها في أمر من الأمور المقوية للماهية، لكان ما به المخالفة، مغاير لما به المساواة التي هي عموم الجسمية . وذلك المغاير إما أن يكون صفة الجسم، أو موصوفاً به، أو لا صفة ولا موصوفاً، والكل باطل على ما سبق تقريره . فبطل القول باختلاف الأجسام. وإنما قلنا : إن الأجسام لما كانت متماثلة، كانت حادثة، لأن كل واحد من تلك المتحيزات، يجب أن يكون حاصلا في حيز معين، ويمتنع أن يكون حصوله فيه لذاته، وإلا لحصل الكل في ذلك الحيز . لما بينا : أنها بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية . والتساوي في العلة يوجب التساوي في المعلول. ولما بطل هذا القسم، ثبت أن اختصاص كل واحد منها بحيزه المعين : إنما كان لسبب منفصل . وذلك السبب لا يجوز أن يكون موجباً . لأنه لو كان موجباً، لكان إما أن يكون جسماً أو لا يكون والأول : باطل . وإلا لعاد الطلب في كيفية اختصاصه بحيزه المعين. والثاني أيضاً : باطل . لأن ذلك الموجب على هذا التقدير، امتنع أن يختلف حاله في القرب والبعد، بالنسبة إلى تلك الأجسام بل يجب أن يقال : إن نسبته إلى كل تلك الأجسام على السوية، فلم يكن بأن يجب لسببه حصول بعض الأجسام في بعض تلك الأحياز، أولى من العكس . فيثبت : أن السبب في اختصاص كل واحد منها بحيزه المعين، لا يجوز أن يكون هو موجباً بالذات . فوجب أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً. وكل ما كان فعلا لفاعل مختار، فهو محدث. فيثبت : أن حصول الجسم في الحيز المعين : محدث . وثبت : أن الجسم لا ينفك عنه، وما لا ينفك عن المحدث فهو محدث وهذا الدليل أيضاً لا يتم عند البحث التام، إلا بالرجوع إلى بعض مقدمات دليل الحركة والسكون
The sixth proof: We say: The bodies of the world are similar (mutamathila) in their complete essence. And when that is the case, it is necessary that they be temporally produced. Explanation of the first [claim]: What we mentioned, that bodies are equal in 'being extended' (hajmiyya) and 'occupying space' (tahayyuz). So if some of them differed in any of the matters that constitute the essence, then that by which the difference occurs would be distinct from that by which the equality occurs, which is the general corporeality. And that distinct [thing] must either be an attribute of the body, or something described by an attribute, or neither an attribute nor something described—all of which are invalid according to what was previously established. Thus the claim of difference among bodies is invalid. And we only say: Since bodies are similar, they are temporally produced, because each one of those spatial entities must be located in a specific space, and it is impossible for its being in that space to be due to itself; otherwise all would be in that space, since we have shown that they are all equal in complete essence. And equality in the cause necessitates equality in the effect. And when this division is invalid, it is established that the specific restriction of each one to its specific space is only due to a separate cause. And that cause cannot be a necessitating cause. For if it were a necessitating cause, it would either be a body or not. The first is invalid, otherwise the demand would recur regarding how it is specifically restricted to its specific space. The second is also invalid, because that necessitating cause on this assumption would have its state of nearness and farness impossible to vary with respect to those bodies; rather it must be said that its relation to all those bodies is equal. So it would not be more likely that because of it some bodies occur in some of those spaces rather than the reverse. Thus it is established that the cause of the specific restriction of each one to its specific space cannot be a necessitating cause by essence. So it must be a free agent. And everything that is an act of a free agent is temporally produced. Thus it is established that the occurrence of a body in a specific space is temporally produced. And it is established that the body is never free from it, and whatever is never free from the temporally produced is itself temporally produced. And this proof is also not complete in thorough investigation except by returning to some premises of the proof of motion and rest.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument for the temporal creation of bodies, based on the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. The argument begins by asserting that all bodies are similar in their complete essence (tamam al-mahiya). It proves this similarity by noting that bodies share the attributes of being extended (hajmiyya) and occupying space (tahayyuz); any difference would require an additional element that must be either an attribute, a subject, or neither, all of which are impossible based on earlier discussion. Hence, bodies are essentially identical. Now, each body occupies a specific space (hayyiz). This specific location cannot be due to the body's own essence, because if it were, all bodies (being essentially identical) would occupy the same space. So the specific location must come from an external cause. That cause cannot be a necessitating cause (mujib). If it were a body, the same problem of spatial specification would arise for that body. If it were not a body, its relation to all bodies would be equal, so it could not assign different locations to different bodies. Therefore, the cause must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). An act of a free agent is temporally produced, so the presence of a body in its specific space is temporally produced. Since a body cannot exist without being in some space, the body itself is temporally produced. The argument ends with a note that, in a full investigation, this proof ultimately relies on premises from the proof of motion and rest.

Key Terms
متماثلة
تمام الماهية
حجمية
تحيز
حيز
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the sixth argument is a version of the design argument appealing to purpose or order.
Correction: Actually, it is a purely a priori metaphysical argument relying on the principle of indiscernibles and the impossibility of a necessitating cause producing diverse effects without discrimination.

Checkpoint
Why does the sixth argument conclude that bodies are temporally produced?
A) Because they are all composed of atoms.
B) Because their specific spatial locations require a free agent.
C) Because they are subject to motion and rest.
D) Because they are similar in size.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument demonstrates that the diversity of spatial positions among similar bodies necessitates a free and choosy agent, proving the world's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence in this specific time and place is not due to your own choice or any necessary cause, but to a free agent who assigned you here, how does that reshape your sense of purpose today? Will you act as if you are placed here by a wise chooser?
?
The argument shows that similarity among bodies forces us to look outside them for an explanation of their differences. Where in your life do you see patterns of similarity (e.g., shared human nature) that demand you look beyond yourself for meaning? Are you seeking that meaning from the free agent or from mere causes?
?
Al-Razi proves that a free agent is necessary to account for diversity. Consider the diversity of your own talents and circumstances. Do you attribute them to random chance or to a deliberate giver? If you truly believe it is from a free agent, will you thank that agent today with a specific action?

The Eighth Argument: Finite Power Implies Contingency
Razi presents the eighth argument: finite power implies contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's eighth argument for the contingency of bodies—that finite power follows from finite magnitude—and his critique of the philosophers' premise.

Core Idea
Finite magnitude implies finite power, so bodies cannot be eternal.

In this passage, Razi presents a deductive argument that bodies, being finite in magnitude, must have finite power. Since an eternal being would require infinite power, bodies cannot be eternal. He then critiques the premise that finite magnitude guarantees finite power, noting that the philosophers' proof for this is weak. This lesson unpacks both the argument and Razi's critical stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: premises, conclusion, and critique.
The passage has two main parts: the argument and its critique.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Premise 1: bodies are finite in magnitude. Premise 2: finite magnitude entails finite power. Conclusion: bodies cannot be eternal.
3
Examine Razi's critique
Razi states that the philosophers' proof for premise 2 is weak. He says he has shown its weakness in many ways. Consider why this critique is important for the overall argument.
This shows that the argument is not decisive in Razi's view.
4
Address the philosophers' objection
Razi also responds to the philosophers who claim the world is eternal by the power of its cause. He notes that the cause is transcendent, not bodily, so the argument still supports contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قال بعضهم : : الأجسام متناهية في المقدار ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، كان متناهي القوة ، فوجب أن تكون قوة الأجسام على البقاء : متناهية . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فإنه يمتنع كونه أزلياً ، أما قولنا : إنها متناهية المقدار ، فلما ثبت في باب تناهي الأبعاد. وأما أن كل ما كان متناهي المقدار ، فإنه يكون متناهياً في القوة : فلما يذكره الحكماء من أن القوة الجسمانية يجب كونها متناهية . وأما النتيجة فظاهرة . واعلم أن دليل الحكماء على القوة الجسمانية يجب كونها متناهية : دليل ضعيف . قد بينا ضعفه بوجوه كثيرة . وأيضاً : فالحكماء يقولون : العالم أزلي أبدي ، لا لقوة ذاتية بل لقوة علّته ومبدئه . وهو منزه عن كونه جسماً أو جسمانياً [ والله أعلم ]
The eighth argument: Some said: Bodies are finite in magnitude, and everything that is so was finite in power; so it is necessary that the power of bodies to endure is finite. And everything that is so cannot be eternal. As for our saying that they are finite in magnitude, it is because it is established in the chapter on the finitude of dimensions. And as for that everything finite in magnitude is finite in power, it is because of what the philosophers mention: that bodily power must be finite. And the conclusion is apparent. Know that the philosophers' proof that bodily power must be finite is a weak proof. We have shown its weakness in many ways. Also, the philosophers say: the world is eternal and everlasting, not by its own power but by the power of its cause and principle. And He is transcendent beyond being a body or corporeal — and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument for the contingency of bodies. It is a deductive argument: bodies are finite in magnitude, therefore they have finite power; a being with finite power cannot be eternal; therefore bodies cannot be eternal. The author then notes that the philosophers' premise that finite magnitude implies finite power is weak, citing his own refutations elsewhere. He also critiques the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal through the power of its cause, noting that the cause is transcendent beyond body and corporeality, so the argument still supports contingency.

Misconception: That finite magnitude automatically means finite power is a universally accepted truth.
Correction: Razi argues that the philosophers' proof for this premise is weak and has been refuted.

Key Takeaway

Why Two Necessary Existents Are Impossible
Razi demonstrates that two necessary existents would entail composition and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two necessary existents is logically impossible according to Razi's first way.

Core Idea
Two necessary existents would each be composite, leading to an absurd dependency.

Razi presents a careful argument by contradiction. He assumes two necessary existents and shows that this leads to an impossible composition. By examining three possible relationships between their common necessity and individual distinctness, he demonstrates that only one Necessary Existent can exist. This lesson walks you through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption: two necessary existents
Imagine two beings that are both necessary in themselves. They share the attribute of necessity but must differ by something else — their individuation.
This sets up the problem.
2
Recognize the composition problem
Each supposed necessary being would be composed of necessity and its particular individuation, meaning it is not simple.
Composition implies dependence of parts.
3
Examine the three possibilities
Razi considers three ways the necessity and individuation might relate: necessity causing individuation, individuation causing necessity, or both caused by something external. Each leads to contradiction.
Focus on why each is impossible.
4
Conclude the impossibility of two
Since all possibilities are absurd, the initial assumption is false. Therefore, only one Necessary Existent exists.
This is a classic proof of divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : ما سوى الواحد ممكن ، وكل ممكن محدث ، فما سوى الواحد محدث [ بيان الأول من وجوه : الأول : إنا نقول ] لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكانا مشتركين في الوجوب بالذات ، ومختلفين في التعين ، فكان كل واحد منهما مركباً من الوجوب ومن ذلك التعين . فإما أن يكون الوجوب علة لذلك التعين ، فيلزم : أن يكون كل واجب الوجود لذاته ، فهو ذلك التعين ، فلا واجب وجود إلا ذلك التعين ، وإما أن يكون ذلك التعين ، علة لوجوب الوجود ، وهو محال . لأن وجوب الوجود بالذات ، لا يكون معللاً بالغير . وإما أن لا يكون واحد من هذين القيدين علة للثاني ، فحينئذ يجب أن يكون اجتماعهما معلول علة منفصلة . فيلزم : أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود لغيره . وهو محال . فيثبت : أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، ليس إلا الواحد
The ninth argument: Everything other than the One is possible, and every possible is originated, so everything other than the One is originated. [Explanation of the first of the aspects: The first: We say] If we suppose two existents that are necessary in existence, they would share in essential necessity and differ in individuation; so each of them would be composed of necessity and that individuation. Then either necessity is a cause of that individuation — then it follows that every necessary of existence in itself is that individuation, so there is no necessary existence except that individuation — or that individuation is a cause of the necessity of existence — which is impossible, because necessity of existence in itself cannot be caused by another. Or neither of these two determinations is a cause of the other; then their conjunction would have to be an effect of a separate cause, so it would follow that the necessary of existence in itself is necessary of existence through another — which is impossible. Thus it is established that the necessary of existence in itself is none other than the One.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents the first way within the ninth argument for the unity of the Necessary Existent. The author argues that if there were two necessary existents, each would be composed of the common attribute of necessity and a particular individuation. Three possibilities are examined: (1) necessity causes individuation — then every necessary would be identical to that individuation, so there could be only one; (2) individuation causes necessity — but necessity of existence in itself cannot be caused, so that is impossible; (3) neither is cause of the other — then their combination would require an external cause, making the necessary dependent on something else, which is absurd. Since all possibilities lead to contradiction, only one Necessary Existent is possible.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
علة
تعين
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God must be simple.
Correction: The argument actually shows that if there were two necessary existents, they would be composite in a way that leads to absurdity; it's about the impossibility of multiple necessary existents, not about simplicity per se.

Checkpoint
What does the first way conclude about the number of necessary existents?
A) There can be many
B) There is exactly one
C) There can be two
D) Nothing definite

Key Takeaway
Razi's first way proves that only one Necessary Existent can exist because any supposed second would entail an impossible composition and dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do you ever attribute independence to anything besides Allah in your heart? This proof shows that any supposed 'second necessary' leads to absurdity. What subtle 'second' have you unconsciously relied upon?
?
The argument demonstrates that if two necessary existents existed, each would require the other to explain their conjunction. Do you treat any created cause as if it were self-sufficient, forgetting that only Allah is necessary in Himself?
?
Reflect on your daily fears and hopes: do you act as if some creature has a measure of necessity independent of Allah? This logic proves that cannot be. What change must you make in your reliance?

The Second Way: Existence Distinct from Essence as Proof of Contingency
Razi presents a syllogism: bodies are contingent because existence is distinct from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between existence and essence in bodies leads to the conclusion that they are contingent.

Core Idea
If existence is not identical to essence, the thing is contingent.

This passage presents a concise syllogism from the ninth argument. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi draws on a prior discussion about existence and essence to establish a major premise: anything whose existence is distinct from its essence is possible in itself. Then he applies it to bodies, concluding that every body is contingent. This is a key example of how metaphysical distinctions ground proofs of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting its two-part structure: the premise and the conclusion.
Notice the phrase 'إنا بينا في باب أحكام الوجود' which references a prior discussion.
2
Identify the major premise
Identify the statement: 'وكل ما كان وجوده غير ماهيته، فهو ممكن لذاته' — this is the major premise linking distinction of existence to contingency.
3
Identify the minor premise
Identify the statement: 'أن الأجسام ، وجودها غير ماهياتها' — this is the minor premise applied to bodies.
4
Derive the conclusion
See how the conclusion 'أن كل جسم فإنه ممكن لذاته' follows logically from the two premises.
This is a classic syllogistic argument.
5
Reflect on the metaphysical background
Consider why the existence-essence distinction is important in Islamic thought. This argument relies on that distinction being already established.
The distinction is a cornerstone of Avicennan metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنا بينا في باب أحكام الوجود : أن الأجسام ، وجودها غير ماهياتها ، وكل ما كان وجوده غير ماهيته، فهو ممكن لذاته . ينتج : أن كل جسم فإنه ممكن لذاته
The second: We have shown in the chapter on the rulings of existence that bodies: their existence is other than their essences, and everything whose existence is other than its essence is possible in itself. The conclusion: that every body is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the second way within the ninth argument. It relies on a prior discussion (in the chapter on the rulings of existence) that for bodies, existence is distinct from essence. The principle is stated: if existence is not the same as essence, the thing is possible in itself (i.e., contingent). Applying this to bodies yields the conclusion that every body is contingent. This is a concise syllogism: major premise (any distinct-existence entity is contingent), minor premise (bodies have distinct existence), conclusion (bodies are contingent).

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this argument is about physical composition or spatial extension.
Correction: The argument is about metaphysical contingency based on the existence-essence distinction, not about physical composition.

Checkpoint

B) Because its existence differs from its essence

Ninth Argument, Third Way: Composition from Hyle and Form
Razi argues that bodies are contingent because they are composed of hyle and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third way of arguing for the contingency of bodies through their composition from hyle and form, and why mutual dependence without causation entails contingency.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because their matter and form depend on each other without one causing the other.

This lesson presents Razi's third proof for the possibility of bodies. He argues that bodies are composed of two distinct principles—hyle (matter) and form—which are inseparable yet not caused by one another. This mutual dependence without causal determination shows that bodies are contingent both in themselves and in their essential parts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument
Read the passage and identify the first argument: bodies are composed of hyle and form; every composite is possible; therefore bodies are possible.
This is a straightforward syllogism.
2
Examine the second argument
Notice that hyle and form cannot exist without each other, yet neither is the cause of the other. This mutual dependence without causation also implies contingency.
This argument focuses on the relationship between the parts.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that bodies are possible both in themselves and in the parts of their essences. Consider how both arguments lead to the same conclusion from different angles.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Understand that the contingency is not merely physical composition but metaphysical dependence: the parts themselves are contingent because they rely on each other yet are uncaused.
This is a subtle but important point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الأجسام مركبة من الهيولى والصورة . وكل مركب ممكن ، فالاجسام ممكنة . وأيضاً الهيولى والصورة يمتنع [ خلو ) ] كل واحد منهما عن الآخر ، مع أن واحداً منها ) ليس علة للآخر . وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ممكن . فالأجسام ممكنة بحسب ذواتها ، وبحسب أجزاء ماهياتها
The third: Bodies are composed of hyle and form. And every composite is possible, so bodies are possible. Also, hyle and form cannot be free of each other, while neither of them is a cause of the other. And everything that is like that is possible. So bodies are possible in themselves and in terms of the parts of their essences.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the third way. It gives two arguments in one. First, a general argument: bodies are composed of hyle (matter) and form, and anything composed is contingent. Second, a more specific argument: hyle and form are inseparable; neither can exist without the other, yet neither is the cause of the other. This mutual dependence without causality indicates that their coexistence is not necessary but contingent, hence the body itself is contingent in its essence and in its constituent parts.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
مركب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The contingency of bodies arises from their physical composition (being made of parts).
Correction: The passage emphasizes metaphysical dependence: hyle and form are mutually dependent yet uncaused, which is a deeper reason for contingency beyond mere compositeness.

Checkpoint
According to the third way, which of the following is a reason for bodies being possible?
A) They are perceived
B) They are composite
C) They are in motion
D) They are finite

Key Takeaway
Bodies are contingent because their essential composition from mutually dependent yet uncaused parts reveals their possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are composed of a body and a soul, neither of which can exist without the other in this world, yet neither causes the other. ?
Razi argues that even the parts of your essence are contingent. The Fourth Way: Dependence on Place and Conclusion
Fourth way and conclusion of Razi's ninth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a body's dependence on a specific place to prove contingency and then argues that the Creator must be a free agent.

Core Idea
A body's need for a place reveals its contingency and leads to a free Creator.

This lesson presents the final part of Razi's ninth argument for the existence and attributes of God. Building on the previous three ways, the fourth way focuses on the fact that every body occupies a particular location, yet its essence does not determine that location. This dependency proves the world's contingency and sets the stage to conclude that the influencer of all things is a free agent rather than a necessitating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the logical flow: premise, inference, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premises
Break down the argument: body cannot be free of a place, its essence is not the cause of that place, so body is contingent, world is contingent, needs an influencer, which is either necessitating or free.
The passage explicitly rejects the necessitarian option.
3
Understand the conclusion
The final sentence states that the influencer of everything other than the One Existent is a free agent. This establishes both God's existence and His will.
This connects the argument to divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why proving free will is important in Islamic theology: it distinguishes God from impersonal causes and affirms moral responsibility.
The common misunderstanding is that this argument only proves a Creator, but it also proves free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن الجسم يمتنع انفكاكه عن الحصول في الحيز المعين [ وليست ماهيته علة للحصول في الحيز المعين ) وإلا لامتنع خروجه عن الحيز المعين ، وكل ما يمتنع خلوه عن شيء ، ولا يكون ذلك الشيء معلولاً له ، فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى ذلك الشيء ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ممكن لذاته [ ينتج : أن الجسم ممكن لذاته ] فيثبت : أن العالم ممكن لذاته ، وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر ، وذلك [ المؤثر ] إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً . وبطل القول بالموجب للدلائل [ الكثيرة ] المذكورة في باب إثبات القادر ، ولسائر الوجوه التي نذكرها بعد ذلك في مقالة مفردة . فوجب أن يكون المؤثر في كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد : فاعلاً مختاراً.
The fourth: A body cannot be free of occurring in a particular place, and its essence is not a cause for occurring in that particular place, otherwise it would be impossible for it to leave that particular place. And everything that cannot be free of something, while that something is not an effect of it, is in need of that something for its actuality. And everything like that is possible in itself. [The conclusion: the body is possible in itself.] Thus it is established that the world is possible in itself, and everything possible in itself has an influencer, and that influencer is either necessitating or free. And the necessitarian view is invalid due to the many proofs mentioned in the chapter on establishing the Powerful, and other aspects we will mention afterwards in a separate discourse. So it is necessary that the influencer in everything other than the One Existent is a free agent.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and final way within the ninth argument, followed by the overall conclusion of the argument. The fourth way: a body is inseparable from occupying some specific location, but the body's essence does not cause that location; thus the body depends on an external factor for its location. This dependence indicates contingency. From the contingency of the world (established by all four ways), the argument moves to the need for an influencer. That influencer must be either necessitating (i.e., acting by necessity) or free (acting by choice). The necessitarian option is rejected based on other proofs (referenced to a previous chapter and a future separate discussion). Therefore, the influencer of everything other than the One must be a free agent, establishing both the existence and the attribute of will in the Creator.

Key Terms
حيز
مؤثر
موجب
مختار
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only proves a creator, not necessarily one with free will.
Correction: The argument explicitly concludes that the influencer must be a free agent because the necessitarian option is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth way conclude about the influencer of the world?
A) It must be a necessitating cause
B) It must be a free agent
C) It could be either
D) It is unknown

Key Takeaway
The world's dependence on specific places proves its contingency, and since a necessitating cause is impossible, the Creator must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that your very existence, occupying a specific place, is a sign of contingency. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge your dependence on the Creator every moment? What will you do today to remind yourself of that dependence?
?
Razi contrasts a necessitating cause with a free agent. Do you pray to God as if He is bound by your expectations, or do you truly believe He freely chooses to respond? How does this affect your trust in His wisdom when your prayers are not answered as you desire?
?
The conclusion of this argument is that the Creator is a free agent. Does your worship reflect that you serve a God who acts by choice, not compulsion? What change do you need to make in your attitude toward obeying His commands?

Proof of the Oneness of the Necessary Existent
Proof that necessary existence cannot be multiple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that there can only be one necessary existent by essence, refuting the possibility of multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
The necessary existent cannot be caused, so only one can exist.

This lesson presents a key argument from Islamic philosophical theology demonstrating that the Necessary Existent (God) must be one. Using logical reasoning, the author shows that multiplicity leads to contradiction, affirming tawhid (divine oneness) at the deepest ontological level.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the logical structure: a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The argument considers two possible ways two necessary existents could be distinct. Identify them: either one qualification causes the other, or neither is caused by the other.
3
See why both lead to impossibility
In the first scenario, one would be caused, contradicting necessity by essence. In the second, their combination would need an external cause, again making them caused.
4
Conclude the oneness
Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the only consistent conclusion is that there can be only one necessary existent by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن وجوب الوجود بالذات، لا يكون معللاً بالغير. وإما أن لا يكون واحد من هذين القيدين علة للثاني، فحينئذ يجب أن يكون اجتماعهما معلول علة منفصلة. فيلزم: أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لغيره. وهو محال. فيثبت: أن واجب الوجود لذاته، ليس إلا الواحد.
Because necessary existence by essence cannot be caused by another. And if neither of these two qualifications is a cause for the other, then their combination must be the effect of a separate cause. So it would follow that the necessary existent by essence is necessary existent through another, which is impossible. So it is established that the necessary existent by essence is only one.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God (the necessary existent by essence). It presents a reductio argument: if there were two necessary existents, they would have to be distinct by some qualification. If one qualification caused the other, that would make the caused necessary through another, contradicting necessity by essence. If neither causes the other, then their combination requires an external cause, again making each necessary through another. Since both options lead to impossibility, only one necessary existent by essence can exist.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود بالذات (necessary existent by essence)
معلل بالغير (caused by another)
قيد (qualification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There could be multiple necessary existents with different essences.
Correction: The argument shows that even if they differed by qualifications, multiplicity leads to contradiction because at least one would be caused.

Checkpoint
What is the impossible consequence the argument seeks to avoid?
A) Necessary existence by essence being caused by another
B) Two necessary existents having different essences
C) A third cause existing separately
D) The universe being eternal

Key Takeaway
The necessary existent by essence cannot be multiple because any multiplicity would entail causation, contradicting its essential necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the statement 'necessary existence by essence cannot be caused by another.' Do you live as if anything else in your life—your status, health, or relationships—is necessary for your well-being? What would it mean to truly internalize that only God is necessary by essence?
?
The proof shows that multiplicity entails contradiction. Yet, do you ever act as if there are multiple forces controlling your life—e.g., fearing people's opinions more than God's decree? How does this logical truth challenge you to purify your tawhid in daily decisions?
?
If the Necessary Existent is only one, then everything else is contingent and could cease to exist. Are you attached to anything—money, a dream, a relationship—as if it were indispensable? What step will you take today to release that attachment and rely solely on God?

Bodies are contingent because their existence is not identical to their essence.

The argument builds on a key distinction between existence and essence: for any being, if its existence is not the same as its essence, then that being is contingent. Bodies are shown to have such a distinction, and thus are contingent.

Third Proof: Bodies Contingent Due to Composition
Proof that bodies are contingent because they are composite and their parts are mutually dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold proof that bodies are contingent: their composition from matter and form, and the mutual dependence of these parts without causal relation.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because they depend on parts that need each other without cause.

This third proof presents two interconnected arguments demonstrating that bodies cannot be necessary beings. The first is based on the fact that bodies are composed of matter and form, and every composite is contingent. The second deepens this by showing that matter and form are mutually dependent yet neither causes the other—a clear sign of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: it contains two 'because' statements.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first argument
Look for the premise 'bodies are composed of matter and form' and the rule 'every composite is contingent'. Follow the deduction to the conclusion that bodies are contingent.
This is a simple syllogism.
3
Identify the second argument
Notice the word 'also'. The second argument states that matter and form cannot exist without each other, yet neither causes the other. This mutual dependence without causation also implies contingency.
This argument is more subtle than the first.
4
Connect to the final conclusion
The author concludes that bodies are contingent in themselves and in the parts of their essences. Understand that both arguments lead to the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن الأجسام مركبة من الهيولى والصورة. وكل مركب ممكن، فالاجسام ممكنة. وأيضاً الهيولى والصورة يمتنع خلو كل واحد منهما عن الآخر، مع أن واحداً منها ليس علة للآخر. وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو ممكن. فالأجسام ممكنة بحسب ذواتها، وبحسب أجزاء ماهياتها.
The third: Bodies are composed of matter (hayula) and form (sura). Every composite is contingent, so bodies are contingent. Also, matter and form cannot be free of each other, while neither is a cause for the other. Everything that is such is contingent. So bodies are contingent in themselves and in the parts of their essences.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the contingency of bodies based on composition. Bodies are made of matter and form, and every composite depends on its parts. Furthermore, matter and form always coexist, but neither is the cause of the other; they are mutually dependent. Such dependency indicates contingency, because necessary existence must be self-sufficient. Therefore, bodies are contingent in both their essence and their constituent parts.

Key Terms
هيولى (matter)
صورة (form)
مركب (composite)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Fourth Proof: Bodies Contingent Due to Dependence on Specific Location
Proof that bodies are contingent due to location dependence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that bodies are contingent because their essence does not necessitate a specific location, and that this leads to the conclusion that the world's influencer must be a free agent.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because their location is not self-determined.

In this proof, al-Sanusi argues that every body occupies some location, but its essence does not dictate which location. This dependency shows that bodies are contingent—they need an external cause. The proof then extends to show that the cause must be a free agent, not a blind necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the key premises
Focus on the two main premises: (a) a body cannot be free from being in a specific location, and (b) its essence is not the cause of that location.
If the essence were the cause, the body could never leave that location.
3
Understand the conclusion about contingency
See how the premises lead to the conclusion that bodies are contingent by themselves, because they depend on something external for their location.
Contingent means possible in itself but dependent on another.
4
Note the shift to the influencer
The proof then argues that the influencer of the contingent world must be either necessitating or free, and necessitation is invalid.
The author references other proofs against necessitation.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this proof contributes to the case for a free creator who acts by choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن الجسم يمتنع انفكاكه عن الحصول في الحيز المعين، وليست ماهيته علة للحصول في الحيز المعين، وإلا لامتنع خروجه عن الحيز المعين، وكل ما يمتنع خلوه عن شيء، ولا يكون ذلك الشيء معلولاً له، فهو مفتقر في تحققه إلى ذلك الشيء، وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو ممكن لذاته. ينتج: أن الجسم ممكن لذاته. فيثبت: أن العالم ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر، وذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً. وبطل القول بالموجب للدلائل الكثيرة المذكورة في باب إثبات القادر، ولسائر الوجوه التي نذكرها بعد ذلك في مقالة مفردة. فوجب أن يكون المؤثر في كل ما سوى الموجود الواحد: فاعلاً مختاراً.
The fourth: A body cannot be free from being in a specific location, and its essence is not the cause for being in that specific location, otherwise it would be impossible for it to leave that location. And everything that cannot be free from something, and that something is not its effect, then it is dependent on that thing for its realization. And everything that is such is contingent by itself. So it follows that a body is contingent by itself. Thus it is established that the world is contingent by itself, and every contingent by itself has an influencer. That influencer is either necessitating (mujib) or free (mukhtar). And the claim of necessitation is invalidated by the many proofs mentioned in the chapter on establishing the Powerful Agent, and by the other aspects we will mention later in a separate essay. So it is necessary that the influencer in everything other than the one existent is a free agent.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a proof that bodies are contingent because they are always found in a specific location (al-hayz al-mu'ayyan), but their essence does not necessitate that specific location. If it did, they could never leave it. Since they can leave, the location must be determined by an external cause. Anything that requires an external cause to actualize is contingent. The proof concludes that the world is contingent and therefore needs an influencer. It further argues that the influencer must be a free agent (mukhtar), not a necessitating cause (mujib), because necessitation has been refuted elsewhere.

Key Terms
حيز (location)
مفتقر (dependent)
مؤثر (influencer)
موجب (necessitating)
مختار (free agent)
فاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that bodies cannot change location.
Correction: No, the proof says bodies cannot be without any location, but they can change location. Their essence does not determine which location, so they are contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what must the influencer of the world be?
A) A necessitating cause
B) A free agent
C) Both necessitating and free
D) Unknown

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that bodies are contingent because they depend on an external cause for their specific location, and that cause must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is contingent—it depends on an external cause for its very location at every moment. Does this fact remind you that your existence is not self-sufficient? How does that change the way you view your dependence on Allah every second?
?
The proof concludes that the ultimate cause of everything is a free agent, not a blind force. If your existence is not accidental but willed by a free Creator, then what is the purpose of your life? Are you living as if your existence is a random accident, or as a purposeful creation accountable to that Free Agent?
?
The proof rejects a necessitating cause, meaning Allah is not bound by any system or external compulsion. Are there areas where you subconsciously expect Allah to act in a fixed, predictable way? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His absolute freedom and wisdom?

Conclusion: All Contingent Things Are Originated
Inference that all contingent things are originated from the need for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the need for a free agent leads to the conclusion that everything other than Allah is originated.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is contingent, and every contingent is originated.

This passage summarizes the grand conclusion of the preceding proofs. By carefully analyzing the need for a free agent, the author demonstrates that all contingent things are necessarily originated. Understanding this inference is key to grasping the Islamic theological worldview that distinguishes the Creator from creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the two main scenarios
Notice the division: the need for a free agent is either during existence or during non-existence. Then within existence, it splits into persistence or origination.
3
Understand why persistence is invalid
The text states that need during persistence would entail 'forming the already existent' which is logically impossible. This eliminates one branch.
4
Recognize the conclusion
With persistence eliminated, the remaining possibilities (need during origination or need during non-existence) both imply the thing is originated. Since everything besides the One is contingent, it follows that everything besides the One is originated.
This is the main takeaway of the entire section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: احتياج هذه الأشياء إلى الفاعل المختار، إما أن تكون في حال وجودها، أو في حال عدمها. فإن كان حال وجودها فإما أن يكون حال بقائها، أو حال حدوثها. والأول باطل لأنه يقتضي تكوين الكائن، وإنه محال. فبقي القسمان الآخران. وذلك يقتضي: أن كل ما كان محتاجاً إلى المؤثر، فهو محدث. فيثبت: أن ما سوى الواحد: ممكن. وثبت: أن كل ممكن محدث، فيلزم: أن كل ما سوى الواحد: محدث. وهو المطلوب. واعلم: أن الكلام في مقدمات هذا الدليل، قد تقدم في هذا الكتاب بالاستقصاء. فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.
And when this is established, we say: The need of these things for the free agent is either during their existence or during their non-existence. If during their existence, then either during their persistence or during their origination. The first is invalid because it would entail the formation of what already exists, which is impossible. So the other two possibilities remain. And that entails: everything that needs an influencer is originated. So it is established that what is other than the One is contingent. And it is established that every contingent is originated. Therefore, everything other than the One is originated. That is what was sought. And know: the discussion of the premises of this proof has preceded in this book in detail, so there is no need to repeat.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion drawn from the previous proofs. It argues that the need for a free agent (the influencer) cannot be for the continuation of existence, because that would entail creating something already existing, which is absurd. Therefore, the need must be for either origination (coming into existence) or for existence itself (if the thing is non-existent). In both cases, the thing is originated (hadith). Since everything other than God has been shown to be contingent, and every contingent is originated, it follows that everything other than God is originated. This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار (free agent)
حال الوجود (state of existence)
حال العدم (state of non-existence)
بقاء (persistence)
حدوث (origination)
تكوين الكائن (formation of the existent)
محدث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the free agent is needed only at the moment of origination, not for existence as such.
Correction: The argument shows that need for the agent is either during existence or non-existence, and both lead to origination. It does not restrict the need to the initial moment.

Checkpoint

A) Because persistence is not a real attribute
B) Because it would mean creating what already exists, which is impossible
C) Because the influencer only acts at the beginning
D) Because persistence does not require an agent

Key Takeaway
Everything other than Allah is originated because its existence requires a free agent that brings it from non-existence to existence.

The Identity and Regress Argument for the Origination of Bodies
Proof by elimination of two possible ways a body could be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that bodies cannot be eternal, based on the impossibility of eternity being either identical to bodiliness or an additional attribute.

Core Idea
A body's eternity cannot be itself or an extra—both lead to contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents the tenth proof for the temporal origination of bodies. The argument systematically eliminates the two possible ways a body could be eternal: either its eternity is the same as its being a body, or it is a separate property. Both options lead to logical absurdities, proving that bodies are not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the clear division into two parts: first and second possibilities.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument considers two ways a body could be eternal: either its eternity is identical to its being a body, or it is an additional attribute. Write them down.
3
Examine the first possibility: identity
If eternity is identical to bodiliness, then knowledge that something is a body would be the same as knowledge that it is eternal. Since we know bodies are bodies by necessity, we would have to know they are eternal by necessity, which is false.
This shows identity cannot hold.
4
Examine the second possibility: addition
If eternity is an additional attribute, then that attribute itself must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, an attribute of a contingent thing would be eternal—absurd. If originated, we get infinite regress.
Both options are impossible.
5
Connect to the previous chapter
The author notes that a similar discussion has already occurred. Reflect on how this proof builds on earlier reasoning.
This shows the interconnectedness of the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة: لو كان الجسم أزلياً، لكان كونه أزلياً. إما أن يكون نفس كونه جسمياً أو مغايراً له. والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونه أزلياً. أما بيان بطلان الأول: فلانه لو كان كذلك، لكان العلم بكونه جسماً، هو عين العلم بكونه أزلياً، فكما أن العلم بكونه جسماً ضرورياً، وجب أن يكون العلم بكونه أزلياً ضرورياً. وبيان بطلان الثاني: إن حدوثه لو كان زائداً، لكان ذلك الزائد إن كان قديماً، لزم أن تكون صفة المحدث قديمة، وإن كان حادثاً، لزم التسلسل وهو محال. واعلم: أنه سبق نظير هذا الكلام في الباب المتقدم.
The tenth proof: If a body were eternal, then its being eternal would either be identical to its being a body or different from it. Both divisions are false, so the statement that it is eternal is false. As for the proof of the falsity of the first: because if it were so, then knowledge that it is a body would be identical to knowledge that it is eternal. So just as knowledge that it is a body is necessary, knowledge that it is eternal would have to be necessary. And the proof of the falsity of the second: if its being originated were additional, then that additional [attribute] if it were eternal, would entail that an attribute of the originated is eternal; and if it were originated, it would entail infinite regress, which is impossible. And know: a similar discussion has preceded in the previous chapter.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies. It examines two possibilities for the eternity of a body: either its eternity is identical to its bodiliness (i.e., its essence), or it is an additional attribute. The first is false because if they were identical, then knowing that it is a body would necessarily imply knowing that it is eternal, but that is not the case; people know bodies are bodies without knowing they are eternal. The second is false because if eternity is an additional attribute, then it must itself be either eternal or originated. If eternal, then an attribute of a contingent thing would be eternal, which is absurd. If originated, then that attribute would require another attribute, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, bodies cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
أزلي (eternal)
جسم (body)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
زائد (additional)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that knowledge of bodies is necessary as an undeniable fact.
Correction: The argument does not assume necessary knowledge of bodies; it uses the premise that if eternity were identical to bodiliness, then knowledge of bodies would have to include necessary knowledge of eternity, which is not the case. The argument highlights the absurdity of the identity, not the truth of the premise.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if the eternity of a body were identical to its being a body?
A) The body would be eternal by essence
B) Knowledge of the body would be identical to knowledge of its eternity, implying eternity is necessarily known
C) The body would not be able to change location
D) The body would have no cause

Key Takeaway
Bodies cannot be eternal because any way to attribute eternity to them leads to either absurd necessary knowledge or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If eternal existence cannot be attributed to bodies without absurdity, what does that imply about the permanence of everything you see and rely on? Are you living as if the world around you is self-sufficient and eternal, ignoring its originated, dependent nature? What would change if you truly internalized that every body, including your own, is temporally originated and sustained by the Creator every moment?
?
The proof shows that if a body's eternity were identical to its essence, then we would have necessary knowledge of that eternity—but we don't. Have you ever assumed that something in your life (your health, relationships, career) is permanent? Where is your certainty rooted? Could this lack of necessary knowledge about worldly things free you from attachment to them?
?
The alternative of an additional eternal attribute leads to the impossibility of an eternal attribute of a contingent thing. Reflect on the simplicity of Allah’s essence—He is not composed of attributes added to His essence. Are you guilty of conceptualizing Allah with human-like qualities or as having parts? The Eleventh Proof: Bodies Cannot Be Eternal
Proof that bodies cannot be eternal because they would share in God's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if bodies were eternal they would share in God's attribute of eternity, and why eternity is considered a positive attribute.

Core Idea
If bodies were eternal, they would share in God's eternity — which is impossible.

This lesson introduces the eleventh proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah for the temporal origination of bodies. The argument is that if a body were eternal, it would participate in God's attribute of eternity. To forestall the objection that eternity is merely a negative concept, the author argues that eternity is a positive attribute. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then'.
2
Understand the premise
The proof begins with a conditional: if a body were eternal, it would share with God in eternity. Identify what would be wrong with sharing God's attribute.
Sharing in God's exclusive attribute is not permissible in Islamic theology.
3
Examine the defense of eternity as positive
The author argues that eternity is a positive attribute. He defines it as 'negation of prior non-existence' and says 'negation of negation is affirmation'. Understand why this makes eternity a real attribute.
4
Note the objection
The text ends with an objection: 'why is it not permissible to say...' The answer is missing. Consider what the objection might be about.
The objection likely challenges the idea that eternity is a positive attribute or that sharing it is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر: لو كان الجسم قديماً، لكان مشاركاً لذات الله تعالى في القدم. فنقول: القدم صفة ثابتة. لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق، ونفي النفي ثبوت، فالقديم أمر ثابت. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال:
The eleventh proof: If a body were eternal (qadim), it would share with the essence of God Almighty in eternity. We say: Eternity is a positive attribute. Because it is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is affirmation, so the eternal is a positive thing. If they say: why is it not permissible to say:
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage begins the eleventh proof for the origination of bodies. The premise is: if a body were eternal, it would participate with God in the attribute of eternity. To block the objection that eternity might be merely a negative concept (i.e., not a real attribute), the author argues that eternity is a positive attribute (sifa thabita) because it negates prior non-existence, and the negation of a negation yields an affirmation. Therefore, the eternal thing has a real positive attribute. The text then presents an objection (probably that eternity is not a real attribute or that sharing it does not entail a problem), but the answer is not included in this window.

Key Terms

قدم (eternity)
صفة ثابتة (positive attribute)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is merely a negative concept, i.e., just the absence of a beginning.
Correction: Eternity is a positive attribute; it is defined as the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of a negation yields an affirmation.

The Logical Proof from Contingency to Origination
A proof that everything contingent needs a creator and is temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that everything contingent requires a creator and is therefore temporally originated.

Core Idea
Everything contingent needs a cause, and that cause proves its origination.

In this passage, the author builds a concise logical argument to establish that everything other than God is contingent and temporally originated. By considering the different ways a thing can need an agent, he eliminates the possibility of a need for persistence, leaving only origination. This proof ties together contingency and temporal origination, foundational concepts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical divisions introduced by the author.
The author uses a conditional structure: 'If in existence, then either persistence or origination.'
2
Follow the divisions
The argument first divides the need for an agent into existence and non-existence. Then it subdivides existence into persistence and origination.
Understand why the first subdivision (need in persistence) is declared invalid.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating the invalid option, the remaining possibilities lead to the conclusion that everything needing an agent is originated, and since everything other than God is contingent, it is also originated.
The author references earlier discussions of the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: احتياج هذه الأشياء إلى الفاعل المختار، إما أن تكون في حال وجودها، أو في حال عدمها. فإن كان حال وجودها فإما أن يكون حال بقائها، أو حال حدوثها. والأول باطل لأنه يقتضي تكوين الكائن، وإنه محال. فبقي القسمان الآخران. وذلك يقتضي: أن كل ما كان محتاجاً إلى المؤثر، فهو محدث. فيثبت: أن ما سوى الواحد: ممكن. وثبت: أن كل ممكن محدث، فيلزم: أن كل ما سوى الواحد: محدث. وهو المطلوب. واعلم: أن الكلام في مقدمات هذا الدليل، قد تقدم في هذا الكتاب بالاستقصاء. فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.
And when this is established, we say: The need of these things for the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) is either in the state of their existence or in the state of their non-existence. If in the state of their existence, then either in the state of their persistence or in the state of their origination. The first is invalid because it would require the bringing-into-being of what already exists (takwīn al-kāʾin), and that is impossible. So the other two divisions remain. And this entails that everything that needs an effect (al-muʾaththir) is temporally originated (muḥdath). Thus it is established that everything other than the One is contingent (mumkin). And it is established that every contingent is temporally originated, so it follows that everything other than the One is temporally originated. That is what was sought. And know that the discussion of the premises of this proof has already been presented in this book exhaustively, so there is no need to repeat.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that all things other than God are contingent and therefore temporally originated. The author begins with a premise: everything that requires a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) does so either in its state of existence or non-existence. If in existence, the need is either for its persistence (baqāʾ) or its origination (ḥudūth). The first option—that something already existing needs an agent for its persistence—is invalid because it would entail bringing into being what already exists (takwīn al-kāʾin), which is absurd. Thus, the need must be either for origination or for non-existence. This proves that anything requiring an agent is originated. Since everything other than God is contingent (mumkin) and thus requires an agent, it follows that everything other than God is temporally originated. The author notes that the premises of this argument have been fully discussed earlier in the book, so no repetition is needed.

Key Terms
الفاعل المختار
محدث
ممكن
تكوين الكائن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only origination requires a creator, not persistence.
Correction: The argument shows that need for persistence is impossible, so the need must be for origination. However, this does not imply persistence doesn't need a creator; rather, the logical structure means that anything existing after non-existence needs a creator for its origination, and its persistence is dependent on that origination.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would require the agent to be temporal.
B) Because it would entail bringing into being what already exists (takwīn al-kāʾin), which is impossible.
C) Because persistence does not require an agent.
D) Because the need is only for non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Every contingent thing is temporally originated because its need for a creator cannot be for its persistence, only for its origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You are contingent—your existence is not necessary. Does your daily life reflect this total dependence on the Creator, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What one habit will you change today to live in constant awareness that you are brought into existence by Allah each moment?

The argument shows that your need for God is not just at the moment of your birth but in every state, including persistence and non-existence. How does this truth confront the way you think about your own control over your life? Will you surrender the illusion of independence and consciously rely on Allah for even your next breath?

If everything other than Allah is temporally originated, then every blessing you have is a fresh creation from Him. The Identity Argument: Why Bodies Cannot Be Eternal
A dilemma refuting the eternity of bodies through identity and addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the tenth proof uses a dilemma regarding identity versus addition to refute the eternity of bodies.

Core Idea
Eternity cannot be identical to corporeality nor a separate attribute — both lead to absurdity.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah against the possibility of an eternal body. The argument uses a logical dilemma: if a body were eternal, its eternality must either be the same as its being a body or an added attribute. Each option is shown to be impossible. As you read, trace how the author demonstrates that neither identity nor addition can hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and the English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument. Notice the structure of a dilemma.
The argument is concise but logically dense.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage states that if a body were eternal, its eternality would either be identical to its corporeality (being a body) or additional to it. Identify these two branches.
This is the core dilemma.
3
Analyze the refutation of identity
If eternality were identical to corporeality, then knowledge of one would be knowledge of the other. But we have necessary knowledge of corporeality, so we would have necessary knowledge of eternality — which we do not. This contradiction disproves the identity option.
The argument relies on the immediate necessity of knowledge about corporeality.
4
Analyze the refutation of addition
If temporality (ḥudūth) were an added attribute, that added attribute would have to be either eternal or temporal. If eternal, the attribute of a temporal thing would be eternal (contradiction). If temporal, an infinite regress of attributes follows (impossible). Thus both sub-options fail.
This uses the principle that an infinite regress is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة: لو كان الجسم أزلياً، لكان كونه أزلياً. إما أن يكون نفس كونه جسمياً أو مغايراً له. والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونه أزلياً. أما بيان بطلان الأول: فلانه لو كان كذلك، لكان العلم بكونه جسماً هو عين العلم بكونه أزلياً، فكما أن العلم بكونه جسماً ضرورياً، وجب أن يكون العلم بكونه أزلياً ضرورياً. وبيان بطلان الثاني: إن حدوثه لو كان زائداً، لكان ذلك الزائد إن كان قديماً، لزم أن تكون صفة المحدث قديمة، وإن كان حادثاً، لزم التسلسل وهو محال. واعلم: أنه سبق نظير هذا الكلام في الباب المتقدم.
The tenth proof: If the body were eternal (azalī), then its being eternal would either be identical to its being a body or additional to it. Both divisions are false, so the claim of its being eternal is false. As for the proof of the falsity of the first: because if it were so, then knowledge of its being a body would be identical to knowledge of its being eternal. So just as knowledge of its being a body is necessary, knowledge of its being eternal would have to be necessary. And as for the proof of the falsity of the second: if its temporariness (ḥudūth) were additional, then that additional [attribute] if it were eternal, would entail that the attribute of the temporally originated is eternal; and if it were temporally originated, an infinite regress (tasalsul) would be entailed, which is impossible. And know that a similar discussion preceded in the previous chapter.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof against the eternity of bodies. The argument is a dilemma: if a body were eternal (azalī), its eternality is either identical to its being a body or a separate attribute. The first option is false because if eternality were identical to corporeality, then knowledge of corporeality (which is necessary) would entail necessary knowledge of eternality—but we do not have such necessary knowledge. The second option is false because if temporality (ḥudūth) were an additional attribute, then that added attribute must either be eternal (making the attribute of a temporal thing eternal, a contradiction) or temporal (leading to an infinite regress of attributes). Both alternatives are impossible, so the original claim that a body is eternal is false. The author notes that a similar argument has appeared earlier in the book.

Key Terms
أزلي
جسم
ضروري
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if eternality is identical to corporeality, then all bodies would be known to be eternal necessarily.
Correction: The argument uses that impossibility as a reductio: since we do not have necessary knowledge of eternality, identity cannot hold.

Checkpoint
What does the argument claim would follow if a body's eternality were identical to its corporeality?
A) Knowledge of its corporeality would be identical to knowledge of its eternality, which is not the case.
B) The body would have two distinct attributes.
C) The body would be both temporal and eternal.
D) The attribute of temporality would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The identity argument shows that claiming a body is eternal leads to either a contradiction in knowledge or an impossible regress, so bodies cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a body cannot be eternal. Have you been living as if this world will never end, prioritizing it over your relationship with your Creator? What one change can you make today to remind yourself that everything besides Allah is temporal?
?
The argument relies on the impossibility of infinite regress. In your own life, do you chase endless desires or worries that have no end? How can you break that cycle by turning to Allah, the First and the Last?
?
If knowledge of a body’s eternality were necessary, you would know it immediately—but you don’t. Are there beliefs you hold that you think are certain but haven’t properly examined through revelation and reason? Will you commit to testing each core belief against the truth of the Qur’an and sound arguments?

Two Types of Demonstration for Divine Action: Lim vs. Anna
Author contrasts lim and anna demonstrations and ends with a prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the demonstration of 'lim (why) and the demonstration of 'anna (that) and why the author considers the former stronger.

Core Idea
The cause-based proof is nobler than the fact-based proof.

In this concluding passage, the author compares two approaches to proving God's creative action. Philosophers rely on a demonstrative proof from the completeness of the agent, while theologians use a proof from the changing nature of bodies. The author clarifies which is more excellent and then closes with a humble supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the shift from doctrinal reasoning to personal supplication.
The passage is the conclusion of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the two demonstrations
Find the descriptions of the philosophers' proof (lim) and the theologians' proof (anna). Compare their starting points and conclusions.
3
Note the condition for the eternity argument
The first statement 'لا قديم إلا الواحد' is said to be valid only on the view of negating attributes. Understand why this condition matters.
4
Reflect on the supplication
Read the closing supplication and consider how the author models humility after a profound theological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال : إنه لا قديم إلا الواحد. ولما كان البارى تعالى قديماً ، كان ما سواه محدثاً ، وهذه الدلالة إنما يصح التمسك بها على القول بنفي الصفات.
فهذا آخر الكلام في هذه المسألة الهائلة
•
وللفلاسفة ههنا مقام آخر . وهو أنهم قالوا : دلائلنا المأخوذة من باب برهان اللم . وذلك لأنا نظرنا في المؤثر. فقلنا : إنه مستجمع لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في المؤثرية ، وأنه متى يكون الأمر كذلك ، امتنع تخلف الأثر عنه . وأما أصحاب الحدوث فإنهم إنما نظروا في أحوال الجسم ، فعرفوا أنها متغيرة ، فيمتنع صدورها عن المؤثر في الأزل، فكان هذا الكلام من باب برهان الآن 1 ومعلوم : أن برهان الله أشرف وأقوى من برهان الآن (۱) ] وههنا آخر الكلام في هذا البحث المهيب ، والمطلوب الهائل .
ونختم الكلام فيه بهذا التضرع ، فأقول
يا من ذكره شرف للذاكرين ، ويا من طاعته فخر للمطيعين
إن أصبت فيها قلت ، فمنك الفضل والإحسان . وإن أخطأت فمن الجهل والخذلان ، وطاعة الشيطان. فيا حنان ويا منان ، وسبحان ، ويا برهان : الغفران ، الغفران ، الغفران
•
خلصنا من دركات النيران، وأوصلنا إلى درجات الجنان [ والحمد لله حتى حمده ، وصلواته على النبي محمد ، وصحبه وآله (۲) ]
It is said: There is no eternal except the One. And since the Creator, exalted, is eternal, what is other than Him is originated. And this indication can only be adhered to on the view of negating the attributes. So this is the end of the discourse on this tremendous issue. And the philosophers have another position here. That is, they said: Our proofs are taken from the chapter of demonstration of 'lim (why). Because we examined the effect. We said: It is complete in all aspects considered in effecting, and when the matter is such, it is impossible for the effect to be delayed from it. As for the proponents of temporal origination, they only examined the states of the body and knew that they are changing, so it is impossible for them to originate from the effect in eternity. So this discourse was from the chapter of demonstration of 'anna (that). And it is known that the proof of God is nobler and stronger than the demonstration of 'anna. And here is the end of the discourse on this awe-inspiring inquiry and tremendous objective. And we conclude the discourse with this supplication, so I say: O He whose remembrance is an honor for those who remember, and O He whose obedience is a pride for those who obey. If I am correct in what I say, then from You is grace and beneficence. And if I am mistaken, then from ignorance and abandonment, and obedience to Satan. So O Compassionate, O Beneficent, and Glorified, and O Proof: forgiveness, forgiveness, forgiveness. Deliver us from the depths of the Fire, and bring us to the degrees of Paradise. And praise be to God until His praise, and His blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, and his Companions and Family.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage contains the conclusion of a major discussion comparing two types of proofs for divine action. It begins by mentioning a statement: 'There is no eternal except the One,' which implies that since the Creator alone is eternal, everything else is originated. The author notes that this argument is only valid if one denies the attributes (i.e., the Ash'ari stance that the attributes are not additional to the essence). Then the author turns to the philosophers' approach. The philosophers use the demonstration of 'lim (cause), arguing that if the cause is fully complete in all relevant aspects, the effect must occur immediately—it cannot be delayed. In contrast, the proponents of temporal origination (al-mutakallimun) use the demonstration of 'anna (fact): they observe bodies and see that they change, so they cannot have existed eternally; thus they must have come into existence from an eternal cause. The author states that the demonstration of 'lim is nobler and stronger than that of 'anna. He then ends the discussion and offers a heartfelt supplication, seeking forgiveness and deliverance from Hell to Paradise, extolling God's honor and grace.

Key Terms
قديم
محدث
نفي الصفات
برهان اللم
برهان الآن
المؤثر
أصحاب الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author rejects the argument from temporal origination (anna) entirely.
Correction: The author does not reject it; he simply states that the cause-based (lim) argument is stronger and nobler.

Checkpoint
According to the text, under what condition is the argument 'لا قديم إلا الواحد' valid?
A) It is always valid
B) Only on the view of denying the attributes
C) Only for the philosophers
D) It is not valid at all

Key Takeaway
The demonstration of 'lim is nobler than that of 'anna, and all reasoning about God should end in humble supplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that knowing the 'why' of God's actions is nobler than merely knowing 'that' He acts. When you face trials in your life, do you seek only to know that God is in control (anna), or do you strive to understand the wisdom behind His decree (lim)? What would it take for you to genuinely trust His perfection even when you cannot see the reason?
?
The author ends his proof with a desperate plea for forgiveness, acknowledging that even his best reasoning is prone to error. When was the last time you sincerely doubted your own understanding of God and begged for guidance? Are you willing to admit that your certainty might be built on shaky grounds?
?
The philosophers' proof rests on the fullness of God's effecting power. But do you actually live as if God's power is complete and His action cannot be delayed? Or do you, by your impatience and worry, implicitly accuse God of delay? What concrete habit can you adopt this week to align your heart with the lim demonstration?
