Introduction to Proofs of God's Free Agency and the First Premise
Author divides proofs into rational and Quranic, and begins a premise on finite bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold division of proofs for God's free agency and the first premise that all sensible bodies are finite and shaped.

Core Idea
The manager of the universe must be a free agent, not a necessitating cause.

The author begins a new chapter dedicated to additional proofs that God is a free agent. He first divides the discussion into two categories: rational principles and Quranic evidence. Before delving into the rational proofs, he establishes a crucial premise about the nature of sensible bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice how the author introduces the structure of the chapter and the initial premise.
The passage sets the stage for a longer argument.
2
Identify the two sections
Find where the author divides the discourse into two sections. What are they? One deals with rational principles, the other with Quranic proofs.
This division shows the author's integration of reason and revelation.
3
Understand the premise
The author presents a premise: all sensible bodies are finite, and everything finite is shaped. Consider why this premise is necessary for the rational proof that God is a free agent.
The premise leads to the conclusion that bodies have shapes, which will be further analyzed.
4
Reflect on the connection to previous proofs
The author mentions that these new proofs, when combined with the six rational proofs from the earlier chapter, become very strong. Think about how this builds on prior knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنا قد ذكرنا وجوها كثيرة في باب إثبات كونه تعالى قادراً، ونريد أن نذكر ههنا وجوهاً أخرى. فنقول: الكلام في هذا الباب مرتب على قسمين:
أحدهما: في الاعتبارات المأخوذة من أصول الحكمة، الدالة على أن مدبر العالم، يجب أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً، لا علة موجبة.
والثاني: الدلائل المذكورة في هذا الباب، في القرآن المجيد.
فإذا ضمت الدلائل السنة العقلية في باب القادر، إلى هذه الوجوه، بلغت مبلغاً عظيماً في الكثرة والقوة.

Know that we have already mentioned many proofs in the chapter on establishing that He, exalted, is capable, and we wish to mention here other proofs. We say: the discourse in this chapter is arranged into two sections:
One of them: concerning the considerations taken from the principles of wisdom, indicating that the manager of the universe must be a free agent, not a necessitating cause.
And the second: the proofs mentioned in this chapter, in the glorious Qur'an.
So if the six rational proofs in the chapter on the capable are combined with these proofs, they reach a great degree in multitude and strength.
As for the first section: which is the considerations taken from the principles of wisdom: know that before delving into the explanation of those considerations, a premise must be presented. Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter (the third article) on proofs that the God of the universe is a free agent (fāʿil bil-ikhtiyār). The author states that while many proofs of God's power (qudra) have already been given, additional proofs will now be presented. The entire discussion is divided into two parts:

1. The first part consists of considerations (iʿtibārāt) derived from the principles of wisdom (uṣūl al-ḥikma) — i.e., rational philosophical principles — which demonstrate that the manager (mudabbir) of the universe must be a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and not a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba). A necessitating cause produces its effect necessarily without choice, while a free agent acts by will and choice.

2. The second part consists of proofs found in the glorious Qur'an.

The author notes that if we combine the six rational proofs already given in the chapter on God's power with these additional proofs, the total becomes very numerous and strong.

Then the author begins to elaborate the first part, but before explaining the considerations, a premise (muqaddima) is required: that sensible bodies (al-ajsām al-maḥsūsa) are finite (mutanāhiya). Everything finite is shaped (mushakkal) — meaning it has a specific form or configuration. Therefore, these sensible bodies are shaped. The shapes are of two types, and the text cuts off here, presumably to list and discuss those types in the following pages.

علة موجبة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational considerations and Quranic proofs are independent and unrelated.
Correction: The author presents them as complementary; combining them increases strength.

Key Takeaway
The chapter on God's free agency is divided into rational and Quranic proofs, and the rational part begins with the premise that all sensible bodies are finite and thus have shapes.

The Plan: Two Divisions of Proof for God as a Free Agent
Razi introduces the plan of the chapter and lists the two sources of arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi structures his new arguments for God's free agency into two divisions: philosophical reasons and Quranic proofs, and how these complement the earlier rational proofs.

Core Idea
Razi's case for God's free agency rests on two pillars: philosophical reasoning and Quranic testimony.

Razi has presented several proofs earlier, but he now introduces a new set of arguments. He clarifies that his discussion will follow a clear plan: first, arguments derived from philosophical principles, and second, proofs from the Qur'an. Together with six earlier rational proofs, these form a powerful cumulative case. This lesson outlines this foundational structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Razi first mentions previous arguments, then lays out the two divisions.
The passage is an introduction, so it sets the stage.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage explicitly states two divisions: considerations from principles of wisdom, and proofs from the Quran. Find these in the text and note their descriptions.
Razi emphasizes that the manager of the world must be a free agent (فاعل مختار), not a necessitating cause (علة موجبة).
3
Understand the cumulative strength
Razi mentions that when these are added to the six rational proofs from the earlier chapter, the arguments reach great abundance and strength. Reflect on why he is building a cumulative case.
This shows the importance of multiple lines of evidence.
4
Consider the integration of reason and revelation
The first division is philosophical, the second is Quranic. Think about how Razi sees both as complementary rather than contradictory.
This is a common theme in Islamic theology: using both rational and revealed sources.

Source Text

اعلم أنا قد ذكرنا وجوها كثيرة في باب إثبات كونه تعالى قادراً ، ونريد أن نذكر ههنا وجوهاً أخرى. فنقول : الكلام في هذا الباب مرتب على قسمين : أحدهما : في الاعتبارات المأخوذة من أصول الحكمة ، الدالة على أن مدبر العالم ، يجب أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً . لا علة موجبة . والثاني : الدلائل المذكورة في هذا الباب، في القرآن المجيد . فإذا ضمت الدلائل السنة العقلية في باب القادر ، إلى هذه الوجوه ، بلغت مبلغاً عظيماً في الكثرة والقوة . أما القسم الأول : وهو الاعتبارات المأخوذة من أصول الحكمة : فاعلم أنه قبل الخوض في شرح تلك الاعتبارات، يجب تقديم مقدمة . وهي إن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة : متناهية . وكل متناه ، فهو مشكل ، ينتج : أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة فهي مشكلة . وهذه الأشكال قسمان أحدهما : الأشكال التي حصلت على سبيل الاتفاق من غير أن يحتاج حصولها إلى فعل فاعل حكيم . والثاني : الأشكال التي يشهد صريح العقل ، بأنها لا تحصل إلا بقصد فاعل حكيم
Know that we have mentioned many arguments in the chapter on proving that He, exalted, is powerful, and we intend to mention here other arguments. We say: the discussion in this chapter is arranged into two divisions: One: regarding considerations taken from the principles of wisdom, indicating that the manager of the world must be a free agent, not a necessitating cause. This passage is an outline of the new arguments for God being a free agent. Then he introduces the first division and provides a necessary premise: all perceptible bodies are finite and therefore have shapes. He then classifies shapes into two kinds: those that arise by chance without requiring a wise agent, and those that reason declares can only arise from the purpose of a wise agent. This sets the stage for the examples to follow.

The Premise: Finite Bodies and Two Kinds of Shapes
Razi presents the premise that finite bodies have shapes and classifies shapes into accidental and intentional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's premise that all perceptible bodies are finite and have shapes, and how he classifies shapes into those that occur by chance and those that require a wise agent.

Core Idea
All finite bodies have shapes; some shapes point to a wise designer, others to chance.

Before diving into the philosophical arguments, Razi lays down a crucial premise. He observes that all perceptible bodies are finite, and everything finite has a shape. He then divides shapes into two categories: those that arise by coincidence and those that can only be explained by the intention of a wise agent. This classification is the foundation for the arguments that follow, as it allows us to distinguish between natural accidents and designed order.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise and its derivation
Read the passage carefully. Razi states: perceptible bodies are finite, everything finite has a shape, so perceptible bodies have shapes. Note the logical deduction.
This is a straightforward syllogism.
2
Identify the two types of shapes
The passage divides shapes into two kinds: those arising by coincidence without a wise agent, and those that sound reason says can only come from a wise agent's intention. Find these in the text.
This classification is key to the upcoming arguments.
3
Understand the criterion
What distinguishes a shape that is from coincidence from one that requires a wise agent? Razi says it is 'sound reason' (صريح العقل) that testifies. This is a rational judgment.
Razi will later provide examples.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Think about why Razi introduces this premise. It sets up a framework for proving that some shapes in the world cannot be accidental and thus point to a wise creator.
This is an inductive argument from design.

اتفاق (coincidence)
فاعل حكيم (wise agent)
صريح العقل (sound reason)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all shapes imply a designer, but Razi distinguishes shapes that occur by chance.
Correction: Not all shapes require a wise agent; only those that sound reason judges as requiring intention.

Razi establishes that finite bodies have shapes, and that shapes are of two types: those arising by coincidence and those requiring a wise agent's intention.

The Ewer and the Broken Jug: Razi's Argument from Design
Razi distinguishes two types of shapes and argues from the ewer's design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of a ewer to argue from design for a wise creator.

Core Idea
If a simple ewer demands a designer, how much more the universe.

Razi gives concrete examples to illustrate two kinds of shapes: those that occur by chance (like a broken jug) and those that clearly serve a purpose (like a ewer). He then focuses on the ewer, detailing its three parts, each with a benefit, to prove that it must have been made by a wise agent. This argument from design is then applied to the larger cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Let the author's own examples and reasoning come through.
The passage uses concrete objects; visualize them.
2
Compare the two types of shapes
Notice how Razi contrasts shapes that occur by coincidence (broken stone/jug) with shapes that align with benefit (ewer).
The first type requires no free agent; the second type demands a wise maker.
3
Analyze the three parts of the ewer
Identify the wide head, narrow spout, and handle. Reflect on why each part is necessary for the ewer's function.
Each part serves a clear benefit: easy filling, controlled pouring, and handling.
4
Understand Razi's refutation of chance
Razi imagines someone claiming the ewer came to be by itself. He then invokes sound innate nature (الفطرة السليمة) to reject this as impossible.
This shows that rational intuition rejects unguided chance for well-designed objects.
5
Apply the premise to the cosmos
Consider how Razi's argument from the ewer sets up a premise: if a simple artifact requires a designer, the entire universe, with far greater complexity and benefit, must also have a wise creator.
The text cuts off, but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الأول : فمثل الحجر المنكسر ، والكوز المنكسر . فإنه لا بـد وأن يكون لتلك القطعة من الحجر والخزف شكل مخصوص معين ، إلا أن صريح العقل شاهد بأن ذلك الشكل ، وقع على سبيل الاتفاق ، ولا يتوقف حصولها على فعل فاعل مختار . وأما القسم الثاني : فهو مثل الأشكال الواقعة على وفق المصالح والمنافع . ونذكر منها مثالاً واحداً : وهو أنا لما نظرنا إلى الإبريق ، رأينا فيه ثلاثة أشياء : أحدهما : الرأس الواسعة . وثانيها : البلبلة الضيقة . وثالثها : العروة . فلما تأملنا في هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة ، ووجدناها موافقة لمصلحة الخلق ، فإنه لابد من توسيع رأس الإبريق ، حتى يدخل الماء فيه بالسهولة ، لا بد من ضيق بلبلة الإبريق ، حتى يخرج منها الماء ، بقدر الحاجة . ولا بد لها من العروة ، حتى يقدر الإنسان على أن يأخذها بيده. فلما وجدنا هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة في الإبريق مطابقة للمصلحة : شهد عقل كل أحد بأن فاعل هذا الإبريق ، لا بد وأن يكون قد فعله بناء على الحكمة ، ورعاية للمصلحة . ولو أن قائلاً قال : إن هذا الإبريق تكون بنفسه من غير قصد قاصد حكيم ولا فعل فاعل عالم . بل اتفق تكونه بنفسه كما اتفق تشكل هذه القطعة من الخزف بهذا الشكل الخاص ، من غير قصد قاصد حكيم ، ولا جعل جاعل عليم ، لشهدت الفطرة السليمة ، بأن هذا القول : قول باطل محال . إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فنقول : إنا لما شاهدنا في الإبريق هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة مطابقة للمنفعة ، وموافقة للمصلحة ، شهدت الفطرة الأصلية
As for the first type: such as the broken stone and the broken jug. For that piece of stone and pottery must have a specific particular shape, but sound reason testifies that that shape occurred by coincidence, and its occurrence does not depend on the act of a free agent. As for the second type: it is like shapes that occur in accordance with benefits and interests. We will mention one example: when we look at the ewer, we see in it three things: one: the wide head; second: the narrow spout; third: the handle. When we reflect on these three parts and find them agreeing with the benefit of people, for it is necessary to widen the head of the ewer so that water enters it easily, necessary to narrow the spout of the ewer so that water comes out from it according to need, and necessary for it to have a handle so that a person can take it by hand. When we found these three parts in the ewer matching the benefit, everyone's reason testifies that the maker of this ewer must have made it based on wisdom and consideration of benefit. If someone said: 'This ewer came to be by itself without the intention of an intending wise agent or the act of a knowing agent. Rather, its coming to be by coincidence just as this piece of pottery shaped itself in this particular shape, without the intention of a wise agent or the making of a knowing maker,' then sound innate nature would testify that this statement is false and impossible. Now that you have understood this premise, we say: when we observed in the ewer these three parts matching benefit and agreeing with interest, the original innate nature testifies...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples for the two types of shapes previously defined. For the first type (shapes by chance), Razi gives the broken stone or jug: they have a shape, but reason sees it as accidental, not requiring a free agent. For the second type, he gives the example of a ewer. He details its three parts: the wide head for easy filling, the narrow spout for controlled pouring, and the handle for holding. Each part serves a clear benefit. Therefore, any rational person concludes that the ewer's maker acted with wisdom and purpose. Razi then refutes a hypothetical objection: if someone claimed the ewer came to be by itself without a designer, sound human nature declares this false. Then he says, 'Now that you have understood this premise,' as if to apply it to the larger world, but the text cuts off. The force of the argument is clear: if a simple ewer demands a designer, how much more does the entire complex universe demand a wise agent.

فاعل مختار
مصلحة
فطرة سليمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ewer example is too trivial to prove anything about the universe.
Correction: Razi uses the ewer to establish a clear principle: if a simple object with three parts demands a wise maker, then the entire cosmos, with far greater complexity, certainly does.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The example of the ewer demonstrates that rational observation of design in everyday objects points to a wise creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If a simple ewer demands a wise creator, what does your own existence—with your unique abilities, circumstances, and purpose—demand of you in response to the Creator? Is there an area of your life where you are living as if you came about by 'chance', and how will you change that this week?

Proof of the Wise Creator through Design in the Pitcher and the Cosmos
Author classifies shapes and argues from the pitcher's design to a wise agent, then extends to the cosmos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from design in a simple pitcher demonstrates the necessity of a wise creator, and how this argument extends to the entire universe, establishing the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
Design in a pitcher proves a wise maker; the cosmos displays even greater design.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a classic kalam argument for the existence of Allah through intelligent design. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by distinguishing between shapes that occur by chance and those that clearly require a wise agent. He then uses the example of a pitcher—with its wide head, narrow spout, and handle—to illustrate how every rational person recognizes the need for a purposeful maker. Finally, he extends this reasoning to the heavens, elements, plants, animals, and humans, showing that the universe is even more replete with wisdom, thus proving the existence of a free, wise, and merciful Creator, and consequently the temporal nature of the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of shapes
Read the passage's opening where the author divides shapes into two types: those occurring by chance (like broken stones) and those that reason testifies require a wise agent.
This sets up the key distinction.
2
Analyze the pitcher example
Focus on the three parts of the pitcher: wide head, narrow spout, and handle. Notice how each part serves a specific benefit, and why this demands a wise maker.
The author appeals to the innate disposition (fitra).
3
Consider the objection and refutation
The author imagines someone claiming the pitcher formed by chance. See how he dismisses this as absurd using sound innate disposition.
4
Extend the argument to the cosmos
Read how the author moves from the pitcher to the heavens, stars, elements, minerals, plants, animals, and especially humans. Note the overwhelming wisdom.
This shows the argument is far stronger for the cosmos.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that establishing a free agent implies the world's temporal origination (huduth). Understand why this follows.
This connects to broader kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : الأشكال التي حصلت على سبيل الاتفاق من غير أن يحتاج حصولها إلى فعل فاعل حكيم . والثاني : الأشكال التي يشهد صريح العقل ، بأنها لا تحصل إلا بقصد فاعل حكيم . أما القسم الأول : فمثل الحجر المنكسر ، والكوز المنكسر . فإنه لا بد وأن يكون لتلك القطعة من الحجر والخزف شكل مخصوص معين ، إلا أن صريح العقل شاهد بأن ذلك الشكل ، وقع على سبيل الاتفاق ، ولا يتوقف حصولها على فعل فاعل مختار . وأما القسم الثاني : فهو مثل الأشكال الواقعة على وفق المصالح والمنافع . ونذكر منها مثالاً واحداً : وهو أنا لما نظرنا إلى الإبريق ، رأينا فيه ثلاثة أشياء : أحدهما : الرأس الواسعة . وثانيها : البلبلة الضيقة . وثالثها : العروة . فلما تأملنا في هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة ، ووجدناها موافقة لمصلحة الخلق ، فإنه لابد من توسيع رأس الإبريق ، حتى يدخل الماء فيه بالسهولة ، لا بد من ضيق بلبلة الإبريق ، حتى يخرج منها الماء ، بقدر الحاجة . ولا بد لها من العروة ، حتى يقدر الإنسان على أن يأخذها بيده. فلما وجدنا هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة في الإبريق مطابقة للمصلحة : شهد عقل كل أحد بأن فاعل هذا الإبريق ، لا بد وأن يكون قد فعله بناء على الحكمة ، ورعاية للمصلحة . ولو أن قائلاً قال : إن هذا الإبريق تكون بنفسه من غير قصد قاصد حكيم ولا فعل فاعل عالم . بل اتفق تكونه بنفسه كما اتفق تشكل هذه القطعة من الخزف بهذا الشكل الخاص ، من غير قصد قاصد حكيم ، ولا جعل جاعل عليم ، لشهدت الفطرة السليمة ، بأن هذا القول : قول باطل محال . إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فنقول : إنا لما شاهدنا في الإبريق هذه الأجزاء الثلاثة مطابقة للمنفعة ، وموافقة للمصلحة ، شهدت الفطرة الأصلية ، والغريزة الفطرية ، بأنه لا بد لها من فاعل حكيم ، ومقدر عليم . فإذا تأملنا في السموات وفي الكواكب، وفي أحوال العناصر الأربعة وفي أحوال الآثار العلوية ، والمعادن والنبات والحيوان ، ولا سيما الإنسان وجدنا من الحكم القاهرة، والدلائل الباهرة، ما غرقت العقول فيها ، وحارت الألباب في وصفها . لا جرم كانت هذه الاعتبارات بالدلالة على وجود الفاعل المختار ، الحكيم الرحيم : أولى. ومتى ثبت القول بالفاعل المختار ، فقد ثبت القول بحدوث العالم ، لا محالة والله أعلم
One of them: the shapes that occur by chance without needing the act of a wise agent. And the second: the shapes that clear reason testifies that they occur only by the intention of a wise agent. As for the first type: it is like the broken stone and the broken jug. For that piece of stone and pottery must have a specific determined shape, except that clear reason testifies that that shape occurred by chance, and its occurrence does not depend on the act of a free agent. As for the second type: it is like shapes that occur in accordance with benefits and advantages. And we mention from them one example: that when we looked at the pitcher, we saw in it three things: one of them: the wide head. Second: the narrow spout. Third: the handle. When we pondered these three parts and found them conforming to the benefit of creation, for it is necessary to widen the head of the pitcher so that water enters it easily, it is necessary to narrow the spout of the pitcher so that water comes out of it in the amount needed, and it must have a handle so that a person can take it by his hand. When we found these three parts in the pitcher matching the benefit, the mind of everyone testified that the maker of this pitcher must have made it based on wisdom and regard for benefit. And if someone said: 'This pitcher came to be by itself without the intention of an intending wise agent nor the act of a knowing agent, rather its coming to be happened by chance just as this piece of pottery took on this particular shape by chance, without the intention of an intending wise agent nor the making of a knowing maker,' sound innate disposition would testify that this statement is a false, impossible statement. Now that you know this premise, we say: When we observed in the pitcher these three parts matching utility and conforming to benefit, the original innate disposition and natural instinct testified that it must have a wise agent and a knowing determiner. So if we reflect on the heavens and the stars, and on the states of the four elements and on the states of the celestial effects, and minerals, plants, and animals, especially man, we find of overwhelming wisdoms and brilliant proofs that drown intellects in them and bewilder minds in describing them. Certainly these reflections are more deserving of indicating the existence of the free, wise, merciful agent. And when the statement of the free agent is established, the statement of the temporal origination of the world is established without doubt, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) for the existence of a wise agent (al-fa'il al-hakim) through the argument from design. It begins by classifying shapes (ashkal) into two types: those that occur by chance (ittifaq) without need for a wise agent, and those that reason testifies cannot occur except by the intention of a wise agent. The first type is exemplified by broken stones or pottery — their shapes happen accidentally. The second type is demonstrated by shapes arranged according to benefit and utility. The author then provides a detailed example: a pitcher (ibriq) with three parts — a wide head to easily receive water, a narrow spout to dispense water in controlled amounts, and a handle for grasping. Since these parts are perfectly suited to human needs, every rational person intuitively knows the pitcher must have been made by a wise, purposeful agent. The author even considers the objection that the pitcher could have formed by chance, just like the broken pottery, and dismisses it as absurd (batil muhal), appealing to sound innate disposition (fitra salima). Having established this premise, the author extends the argument to the universe: the heavens, stars, four elements, celestial effects, minerals, plants, animals, and especially man display even greater and more overwhelming wisdom and proofs. Therefore, these reflections are even more decisive in demonstrating the existence of a free, wise, merciful agent. The conclusion is that establishing the existence of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) necessarily implies the temporal origination (huduth) of the world.

اتفاق
فاعل حكيم
مصلحة
إبريق
فطرة
فاعل مختار
حدوث

Correction: The author argues that sound fitra recognizes the absurdity of such a claim; the pitcher's ordered parts for benefit necessitate a wise agent.

Key Takeaway
The argument from design in a simple pitcher demonstrates that any object with parts arranged for benefit requires a wise maker, and the universe displays even greater design, proving the existence of a free, wise Creator and the temporal origination of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof from Contemplation for the Existence of the Free-Acting Agent
Author argues that contemplation of creation proves a wise agent and temporal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the combination of innate instinct and contemplation of creation leads to affirming a free-acting, wise, and merciful agent, and why this necessarily establishes the temporal creation of the world.

Core Idea
Contemplating creation makes innate instinct intellectually explicit.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: that both our inner disposition (fitra) and our observation of the cosmos point to the existence of a wise, free-acting Creator. The author first appeals to innate instinct, then invites the reader to reflect on the heavens, elements, and living beings, concluding that the overwhelming wisdoms in creation are best explained by a free-acting agent. Once this agent is established, the temporal creation of the world follows necessarily. As you read, notice how the argument moves from instinct to observation to a definite conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the strong concluding phrases like 'لا جرم' and 'لا محالة'.
2
Identify the two sources of evidence
The author uses two sources: innate instinct (غريزة فطرية) and contemplation (تأمل) of creation. Notice how the instinct is mentioned first as a foundation, then expanded by observation.
The instinct is not elaborated; it is simply stated as a given.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: innate instinct demands a wise agent; contemplation reveals overwhelming wisdoms; therefore, these considerations indicate the free-acting agent; and once that is established, the world's temporal creation follows. Ask yourself why temporal creation is a necessary consequence.
The connection between free-acting agent and temporal creation is standard in kalam: a free agent acts in time, so the world must have a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغريزة الفطرية ، بأنه لا بد لها من فاعل حكيم ، ومقدر عليم . فإذا تأملنا في السموات وفي الكواكب، وفي أحوال العناصر الأربعة وفي أحوال الآثار العلوية ، والمعادن والنبات والحيوان ، ولا سيما الإنسان وجدنا من الحكم القاهرة، والدلائل الباهرة، ما غرقت العقول فيها ، وحارت الألباب في وصفها . لا جرم كانت هذه الاعتبارات بالدلالة على وجود الفاعل المختار ، الحكيم الرحيم : أولى. ومتى ثبت القول بالفاعل المختار ، فقد ثبت القول بحدوث العالم ، لا محالة [ والله أعلم (۱) ]
And the innate instinct, that there must be for it a wise agent and a knowing determiner. For if we contemplate the heavens and the stars, and the states of the four elements, and the states of the upper influences, and minerals, plants, animals, especially man, we find overwhelming wisdoms and dazzling proofs that drown intellects in them and bewilder minds in describing them. Without doubt, these considerations are more fitting for indicating the existence of the free-acting, wise, merciful agent. And when the statement of the free-acting agent is established, then the statement of the temporal creation of the world is established without fail. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God and the temporal creation of the world, drawing on both innate disposition (fitra) and empirical observation. The author begins by stating that innate instinct necessitates a wise and knowing agent. Then he invites the reader to contemplate the heavens, stars, the four elements (earth, water, air, fire), celestial influences, minerals, plants, animals, and especially humans. In all these, one finds overwhelming wisdoms and dazzling proofs that overwhelm intellects. The author concludes that these considerations are more fitting (awla) to indicate the existence of the free-acting (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), wise, and merciful agent. He then asserts that once the existence of such an agent is established, the temporal creation of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) is necessarily established. The argument follows the standard kalam sequence: proving a Maker, then deriving that the world is created (since the Maker acts freely and creates).

رحيم (merciful)

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage explicitly starts with innate instinct (غريزة فطرية) and then invites contemplation of the physical world, showing that both instinct and observation are integral to the proof.

The Creator Must Be a Voluntarily Choosing Agent
The author introduces the first consideration proving the Creator is a voluntary agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational distinction between a voluntarily choosing agent and a necessitating cause, and why the Creator must be the former.

Core Idea
The Maker acts by will, not by necessity.

This passage opens a major section of the work. The author announces the first consideration—derived from the principles of wisdom—which serves as a proof that the Creator is a freely choosing agent, not a cause that acts by compulsion. This distinction is essential for understanding divine attributes and refuting deterministic views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the literal translation. Notice the contrast between 'voluntarily choosing agent' and 'necessitating cause'.
The Arabic has uncertain words; focus on the clear contrast.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms: اعتبارات (considerations), أصول الحكمة (principles of wisdom), صانع العالم (Maker of the world), فاعل مختار (voluntarily choosing agent), علة موجبة (necessitating cause). Understand each term's meaning.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on the difference between a free agent who acts by will and a cause that produces effects necessarily. Why does the author insist the Creator is the former?
This distinction is central to Islamic theology.
4
Ask why this matters
Consider the theological implications: if God acted by necessity, He would not be praiseworthy for creation; if He acts by will, His actions are purposeful and deserving of gratitude and worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول من هذه المقالة في الاعتبارات المأخوذة من أصول الحكمة، الدالة على أن [؟] العالم يجب أن يكون [؟] مختارا، لا علة موجبة.
The first of this treatise: Concerning the considerations taken from the principles of wisdom, indicating that the [؟] of the world must be a [؟] choosing [agent], not a necessitating cause.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage opens a section of the work. It announces that the first part of this treatise deals with considerations (iʿtibārāt) derived from the principles of wisdom (uṣūl al-ḥikma). These considerations serve as proofs that the Maker (ṣāniʿ) of the world—the Creator—must be a voluntarily choosing agent (fāʿil mukhtār), and not a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba). This is a foundational claim in kalām: the distinction between a free agent who acts by will and a cause that produces its effect by necessity. The author is setting up a rational argument against determinism and for divine voluntary action.

علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Creator acts by necessity or natural compulsion.
Correction: This passage asserts that the Creator must be a voluntarily choosing agent, not a necessitating cause.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, the Maker of the world must be which of the following?
A) A necessitating cause
B) A voluntarily choosing agent
C) A natural force
D) An accidental cause

Key Takeaway
The Creator is a free agent who acts by will, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessitating cause, your prayers would be pointless. Do you truly believe that your dua can change what He has decreed? How does that belief shape your desperation in supplication today?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse it as 'I couldn't help it' or 'God made me this way'? This passage insists God is not a force of necessity but a free agent. How does that truth confront the excuses you make for your own choices?
?
The passage says God acts according to principles of wisdom, not arbitrary will. Do you trust that His decisions for your life are wise, even when they contradict your desires? What specific situation are you holding back from surrendering to His wisdom?

The Sun as the Sultan: Why It Sits at the Center of the Cosmos
The author explains why the sun is placed at the center of the cosmos using a royal analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cosmological argument for the sun's central position, based on the analogy of a sultan ruling from the center of his kingdom.

Core Idea
The sun is like a sultan—its palace must be at the center.

Imagine a king who rules over a vast kingdom. Where would he place his palace? Naturally, in the center, so that his authority reaches all regions. The author applies this analogy to the cosmos: the sun, as the ruler over the world of bodies, is placed at the center of the eleven spheres. This lesson explores that argument and the structure of the heavens as understood by classical cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the author's use of analogy and enumeration.
The passage is concise—every word matters.
2
Understand the Royal Analogy
The author compares the sun to a sultan and states that a king's palace must be central. Ask yourself: Why does this analogy justify the sun's position?
This is the core argument: the sun's role as ruler requires centrality.
3
Enumerate the Spheres
Identify the eleven spheres: five outside the sun's sphere (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, fixed stars, greatest sphere) and five inside (Venus, Mercury, Moon, subtle sphere, dense sphere). Notice how the sun's location is precisely in the middle.
This enumeration shows the sun is surrounded symmetrically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك ، لا جرم صار موضعها في الفلك المتوسط. كما أن دار الملك ، يجب أن تكون في وسط المملكة . وبيانه : أن جملة العالم إحدى عشرة كرة ، خمسة منها فلك الشمس وهي : فلك المريخ ، والمشتري، وزحل ، وفلك الثوابت ، والفلك الأعظم . وخمسة أخرى في داخل فلك الشمس . وهي : فلك الزهرة ، وعطارد ، والقمر ، ثم الكرة اللطيفة وهي النار والهواء ، والكرة الكثيفة وهي الماء والأرض . فلما كانت الشمس كالسلطان لعالم الأجسام ، لا جرم جعل مكانها في وسط كرات العالم .
Thus, necessarily its place was made in the middle sphere. Just as the king's palace must be in the center of the kingdom. And its explanation: that the totality of the world is eleven spheres: five of them are the sphere of the sun, namely the sphere of Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, the sphere of fixed stars, and the greatest sphere. And five others are inside the sphere of the sun, namely the sphere of Venus, Mercury, the Moon, then the subtle sphere which is fire and air, and the dense sphere which is water and earth. When the sun became like the sultan over the world of bodies, necessarily its place was made in the middle of the spheres of the world.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage provides a cosmological argument for the central position of the sun. The author begins by stating a principle: the ruler's seat is in the middle of his domain. He then describes the structure of the cosmos, enumerating eleven spheres. The first five are the outermost spheres including the fixed stars and the largest sphere. The next five are within the solar sphere: the planets Venus, Mercury, the Moon, and then the sublunar spheres of fire, air, water, and earth. Since the sun acts as a sultan over the world of bodies, it is fitting that it be placed at the center, just as a king's palace is central. This demonstrates wise design.

Misconception: The sun is placed at the center because it is the largest or hottest celestial body.
Correction: The author argues that the sun's central position is due to its role as the sultan (ruler) over the world of bodies, not because of its physical properties.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is the hottest body

C) Because it is the largest sphere
D) Because it is the source of light

Key Takeaway
The sun's central position in the cosmos is explained by its role as the ruler over bodily existence, analogous to a king's palace at the center of his kingdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Sun as Source of Life and Motion
The author likens the sun to the soul giving life and movement to animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the daily cycle of animals to argue that the sun is a divinely appointed source of life and motion.

Core Idea
The sun's daily cycle reveals a divinely designed order that governs all life.

This passage presents the fourth theological benefit: the observable link between the sun's daily movement and the vitality of living beings. By tracing the correlation from dawn to dusk, the author builds a persuasive argument for divine design. The vivid description invites you to see the hand of God in the rhythmic changes of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the progression from night to dawn, to full day, to sunset, and back to night.
The author uses a sequential observation to build his case.
2
Identify the key observations
Notice how the author describes animals as 'dead' at night, then 'revived' at dawn, with movement increasing as the sun rises, then weakening as it declines, and finally stillness at night.
These observations are the raw data for the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence: 'This indicates that the Most High regulated the conditions of the great luminary so that it became like a sultan over the world of bodies.' This is the doctrinal punchline.
The sun is not a god but an obedient deputy.
4
Reflect on the broader argument
Ask yourself: How does this daily cycle point to a wise Creator? Think about the consistency and the benefits of this design.
This connects to the larger theme of divine existence and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الرابع : إنا نرى جميع الحيوانات في الليل ، كالميتة . فإذا طلع نور الصبح ظهرت في أجساد الحيوانات ، نور الحياة ، فيجري هذا ، مجرى ما إذا قيل : إن طلوع نور الشمس نفخ في أبدان الحيوانات قوة الحياة . وكلما كان طلوع ذلك النور أتم ، كان ظهور قوة الحياة في الأبدان أكمل . ثم كلما طلع قرص الشمس رأيت الناس وسائر الحيوانات يخرجون من مساكنهم ويبتدئون الحركة. وما دامت الشمس صاعدة إلى وسط سمائهم ، كانت حركتهم في الزيادة والقوة فإذا انحدرت الشمس من وسط السماء ، فكأنها أخذت في الضعف ، لا جرم أخذت حركات أهل تلك المساكن وقواهم في الضعف . ولا يزال كذلك إلى زمان غيبوبة الشمس . فإذا غابت وظهر الظلام في العالم ، استولى الخوف والفزع ، والفتور والنقصان على الخلق . ورجعت الحيوانات إلى بيوتها وأحجارها . فإذا غاب الشفق ، هدأت الأبدان وسكنت . وصارت كالميتة المعدومة. فإذا طلع الصبح عليهم ، عادت الأحوال المذكورة مرة أخرى. وهذا يدل على أنه تعالى دبر أحوال النير الأعظم ، بحيث صار كالسلطان لعالم الأجسام في السموات وفي العناصر .
The fourth aspect: we see all animals at night as if dead. When the light of dawn rises, the light of life appears in the bodies of animals. This is similar to if it were said: the rising of the sun's light breathes the power of life into the bodies of animals. The more complete the rising of that light, the more complete the appearance of the power of life in the bodies. Then when the disk of the sun rises, you see people and other animals leaving their dwellings and beginning movement. As long as the sun is ascending to the middle of their sky, their movement increases in strength. When the sun declines from the middle of the sky, it is as if it weakens; necessarily, the movements and powers of the inhabitants of those dwellings weaken. This continues until the time of the sun's setting. When it sets and darkness appears in the world, fear, terror, languor, and deficiency overcome the creation. The animals return to their houses and dens. When the twilight disappears, the bodies become still and calm, becoming like a non-existent dead thing. When dawn rises upon them, the aforementioned states return once more. This indicates that the Most High regulated the conditions of the great luminary so that it became like a sultan over the world of bodies in the heavens and the elements.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth benefit: the sun governs the cycle of life and activity. At night, animals appear lifeless. With dawn, life energy enters them. The sun's light is compared to a breath that gives vitality. As the sun rises higher, movement and strength increase. When it declines, strength wanes. At sunset, fear and weakness take over, and by nightfall, stillness dominates. The observation that this cycle repeats daily shows that God designed the sun as a sultan over all bodies, both celestial and elemental.

The Fifth Benefit: Sun's Motion Prevents Extreme Temperatures
The author explains the sun's motion as a divine benefit preventing extreme temperatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun's motion and axial tilt distribute heat evenly across the earth, preventing extreme temperatures.

Core Idea
The sun's motion prevents localised extremes of heat and cold.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the fifth benefit of the sun's creation: its movement. The author argues that if the sun were stationary, heat would be concentrated in one place, leaving others frigid. Instead, the sun's daily rotation and annual inclination ensure all regions receive balanced warmth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author builds the argument: first the consequence of a stationary sun, then the description of its east-west motion, then the north-south inclination.
The passage is incomplete, but the main idea is clear.
2
Identify the two types of solar motion
The sun has two motions: daily rotation from east to west, and annual inclination north-south relative to the celestial equator. Each distributes heat differently.
3
Understand the consequences without motion
If the sun were stationary (no motion at all), one spot would become intensely hot and all others would be extremely cold. Motion prevents this imbalance.
4
Reflect on the design argument
The author presents this as a divine benefit, showing purposeful design in creation. Consider how this fits into the larger theological context.
The passage is cut off; the full argument about inclination is not concluded.

والوجه الخامس من منافع الشمس : أنها متحركة . فإنها لو كانت واقفة في موضع واحد ، لاشتدت السخونة في ذلك الموضع ، ولاشتد البرد في سائر المواضع ، لكنها تطلع في أول النهار من المشرق ، فيقع نورها على ما يحاذيها من جهة المغرب ، ثم لا تزال تدور وتغشى جهة بعد جهة ، حتى تنتهي إلى المغرب. فتشرق حينئذ على الجوانب الشرقية، وحينئذ لا يبقى موضع مكشوف في الشرق والغرب، إلا ويأخذ حظاً من شعاع الشمس. وأما بحسب الجنوب والشمال فجعلت حركتها مائلة عن منطقة الفلك الأعظم ، فإنه لو لم يكن للشمس حركة في الميل ، لكان تأثيرها مخصوصاً بمدار واحد ، فكانت سائر المدارات تخلو عن المنافع الحاصلة منه ، وكان يبقى كل واحد من المدارات على كيفية واحدة أبداً . فإن كانت حارة
The fifth benefit of the sun: that it is moving. For if it were stationary in one place, the heat would intensify in that place and extreme cold would intensify in other places. But it rises at the beginning of the day from the east, so its light falls on what faces it from the direction of the west. Then it continues rotating and covering region after region until it reaches the west. Then it shines on the eastern sides, and no exposed place in the east and west remains but that it takes a portion of the sun's rays. As for the south and north, its motion is made inclined relative to the zone of the greatest sphere. For if the sun had no motion in inclination, its effect would be confined to one orbit, so the other orbits would be devoid of the benefits arising from it, and each orbit would remain in one perpetual state. If it were hot...
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth benefit: the sun's motion prevents extreme temperatures. The author argues that if the sun were stationary, one area would become intensely hot while others would be frigid. Instead, the sun moves from east to west, covering all longitude zones. Additionally, for latitude, the sun has a north-south inclination relative to the celestial equator (the zone of the greatest sphere). Without this tilt, the sun's influence would be limited to a single orbital band; other latitudes would never receive its benefits. The argument is cut off mid-sentence.

Evidence from the Sun's Daily Cycle on Life
Proof of divine design through the sun's daily cycle on life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consistent daily pattern of the sun's rising and setting on living beings serves as a teleological proof for the existence of a divine planner.

Core Idea
The sun's daily cycle on life reveals a design that demands a divine administrator.

In this passage, the author presents a compelling observation from everyday life: the sun's light seems to infuse energy into animals and humans at sunrise, while sunset brings lethargy and stillness. This regular pattern, he argues, is not a mere natural coincidence but points to an intentional arrangement by God, who has made the sun like a sovereign over the physical world. Let's read the original text and unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once or twice without analysis. Then read the English translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the vivid description of how life responds to the sun's position.
2
Identify the key observation
The author describes a pattern: at sunrise, animals become active; at sunset, they weaken; at night, they become still; at dawn, they revive. Find these stages.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this pattern indicates that God has 'administered' (dabbara) the sun's states. Think about why he draws that conclusion.
The word 'dabbara' implies intentional planning.
4
Reflect on the teleological argument
This is a classic design argument: regular, beneficial order points to a wise designer. Consider why this is not a simple description but a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما إذا قيل : إن طلوع نور الشمس نفخ في أبدان الحيوانات قوة الحياة . وكلما كان طلوع ذلك النور أتم ، كان ظهور قوة الحياة في الأبدان أكمل . ثم كلما طلع قرص الشمس رأيت الناس وسائر الحيوانات يخرجون من مساكنهم ويبتدئون الحركة. وما دامت الشمس صاعدة إلى وسط سمائهم ، كانت حركتهم في الزيادة [ والقوة (۲) ] فإذا انحدرت الشمس من وسط السماء ، فكأنها أخذت في الضعف ، لا . جرم أخذت حركات أهل تلك المساكن وقواهم في الضعف . ولا يزال كذلك إلى زمان غيبوبة الشمس . فإذا غابت وظهر الظلام في العالم ، استولى الخوف والفزع ، والفتور والنقصان على الخلق . ورجعت الحيوانات إلى بيوتها وأحجارها . فإذا غاب الشفق ، هدأت الأبدان وسكنت . وصارت كالميتة المعدومة. فإذا طلع الصبح عليهم ، عادت الأحوال المذكورة مرة أخرى. وهذا يدل على أنه تعالى دبر أحوال النير الأعظم ، بحيث صار كالسلطان لعالم الأجسام في السموات وفي العناصر .
If it is said: The rising of the light of the sun blows the power of life into the bodies of animals. And whenever the rising of that light is more complete, the appearance of the power of life in the bodies is more perfect. Then whenever the disk of the sun rises, you see people and all other animals coming out of their dwellings and beginning to move. And as long as the sun is ascending to the middle of their sky, their movement is increasing [and strength (2)]. When the sun declines from the middle of the sky, it is as if it begins to weaken; of course the movements and powers of the inhabitants of those dwellings begin to weaken. And it continues like that until the time of the sun's setting. When it sets and darkness appears in the world, fear and terror, and lethargy and deficiency overcome the creation. And the animals return to their houses and dens. When the twilight disappears, bodies become calm and still, and become like a dead, non-existent thing. When morning dawns upon them, the mentioned states return once more. And this indicates that He, exalted, has administered the states of the great luminary, such that it has become like the sovereign of the world of bodies in the heavens and in the elements.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a proof from design, specifically from the regular effect of the sun's daily cycle on living beings. The author presents a hypothetical statement ('if it is said') that the sun's light gives life, then describes the observable pattern: at sunrise, people and animals become active and strong; at sunset, they become weak, fearful, and still; at night they become like dead; and at dawn they revive. The author concludes that this consistent correlation shows that God has arranged the sun's states so that it acts as a sovereign over the physical world. The argument is teleological: the regular and beneficial pattern cannot be accidental; it points to a wise administrator (dabbara). The author uses 'nayyir aʿẓam' (great luminary) to refer to the sun, and 'dabbara' implies intentional planning.

Key Terms
طلوع نور الشمس
قوة الحياة
النير الأعظم
دبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage is merely describing natural cycles without any theological significance.
Correction: The passage is a teleological argument: it uses the consistent, beneficial pattern of the sun's cycle to argue that a wise God must have designed it. The author explicitly concludes that God has 'administered' (dabbara) the sun's states.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the daily pattern of the sun's rising and setting?
A) That the sun itself is divine.

The consistent daily cycle of the sun's effect on living beings is evidence that God has intentionally designed and governs the physical world.

The Sun's Motion as a Sign of Divine Wisdom
Argument that the sun's motion and inclination distribute heat evenly, showing divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun's motion and inclination are precisely calibrated to distribute heat and enable life, demonstrating intentional divine design.

Core Idea
The sun's motion is a sign of wisdom, not randomness.

This passage presents the fifth benefit of the sun explored by the author: its motion. He argues that if the sun were stationary, extreme temperatures would destroy life. Instead, its daily rotation and annual inclination create balanced climates and seasons. This careful calibration points to a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument from hypotheticals (if stationary, if no inclination) to the observed reality.
Pay special attention to the conditional statements starting with 'law' (if).
2
Identify the two motions of the sun
The passage discusses two motions: the daily east-west motion and the annual north-south inclination. List the benefits attributed to each motion.
The east-west motion prevents extreme heat in one spot; the inclination creates seasons.
3
Reflect on the theological conclusion
Consider why the author presents these details as a 'benefit' of the sun. What does the precise calibration imply about the sun's origin?
The passage implies that the sun's properties are not accidental but designed for life.

والوجه الخامس من منافع الشمس : أنها متحركة . فإنها لو كانت واقفة في موضع واحد ، لا شتدت السخونة في ذلك الموضع ، ولإشتد البرد في سائر المواضع ، لكنها تطلع في أول النهار من المشرق ، فيقع [ نورها ) )] على ما يحاذيها من جهة المغرب ، ثم لا تزال تدور وتغشى جهة بعد جهة ، حتى تنتهي إلى المغرب. فتشرق حينئذ على الجوانب الشرقية، وحينئذ لا يبقى موضع مكشوف في الشرق والغرب، إلا ويأخذ حظاً من شعاع الشمس. وأما بحسب الجنوب والشمال فجعلت حركتها مائلة عن منطقة الفلك الأعظم ، فإنه لو لم يكن للشمس حركة في الميل (1) ، لكان تأثيرها مخصوصاً بمدار واحد ، فكانت سائر المدارات تخلو عن المنافع الحاصلة منه ، وكان يبقى كل واحد من المدارات على كيفية واحدة أبداً . فإن كانت حارة أفنت الرطوبات واحالتها كلها إلى النارية. ولم تتكون المتولدات . فيكون الموضع المحاذي لممر الشمس على كيفية الاحتراق، والموضع البعيد عنه على كيفية البرد، والمتوسط بينهما على كيفية (۱)] متوسطة . فيكون في موضع : شتاء دائم، فيه النهوة والفجاجة. وفي موضع آخر صيف دائم، يوجب الاحتراق. وفي موضع آخر ربيع، وآخر خريف لا يتم فيه النضج، وأيضاً: لو لم تكن عودات متتالية للشمس ، بل كانت تتحرك بطيئة ، لكان هذا الميل(٢) قليل ، وكان التأثير شديد الإفراط ، فكان يعرض قريباً ، مما لو لم يكن ميل . النفع ولو كانت حركتها أسرع من هذه ، لما كملت المنافع وما تمت . فأما إذا كان هناك ميل ، يحفظ [ الحركة في جهة : ثم تنتقل عنها إلى جهة أخرى ، بمقدار الحاجة ، وتبقى في كل (۳) ] جهة برهة من الدهر : تم بذلك تأثيرها وكملت منفعتها .
The fifth aspect of the benefits of the sun: that it is moving. For if it were stationary in one place, the heat would intensify in that place and the cold would intensify in all other places. But it rises at the beginning of the day from the east, so its light falls on what is opposite it from the west, then it continues to revolve and cover region after region until it ends at the west. Then it shines at that time on the eastern sides, and then no uncovered place in the east and west remains except that it takes a share of the sun's rays. As for the south and north, its motion was made inclined from the zone of the greatest sphere, for if the sun had no motion in inclination (1), its effect would be confined to one orbit, and the other orbits would be devoid of the benefits resulting from it, and each of the orbits would remain in a single state forever. If it were hot, it would destroy the moisture and convert it all into fire, and generated things would not form. So the place opposite the sun's path would be in a state of burning, the distant place in a state of cold, and the intermediate between them in a state (1) intermediate. So there would be a place: perpetual winter, with crudeness and coarseness; another place perpetual summer, causing burning; another spring, and another autumn with no ripening. Also, if the sun had no successive returns, but moved slowly, its inclination (2) would be small and the effect would be extremely excessive, so it would be close to what would happen if there were no inclination. The benefit: if its motion were faster than this, the benefits would not be complete and perfected. But when there is an inclination that preserves [ motion in one direction, then it transfers to another direction according to need, and it stays in each (3) direction for a period of time: then its effect is accomplished and its benefit is perfected.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a proof of divine wisdom from the beneficial motion of the sun. The author presents the fifth face (wajh) of the sun's benefits: its motion. He argues that if the sun were stationary, it would cause extreme heat in one place and extreme cold elsewhere. Instead, it moves from east to west, spreading light and heat gradually. Additionally, its motion is inclined relative to the celestial sphere (minṭaqat al-falak al-aʿẓam), which prevents the sun from staying on one orbit and thus ensures seasonal variation and moderate climates. Without this inclination, each region would have a single unvarying climate—some perpetual winter, some perpetual summer, etc.—which would prevent the generation of life. The author also notes that the speed of the sun's motion and its inclination are precisely calibrated: if too slow or too fast, the benefits would not be achieved. The conclusion is that the sun's motion and inclination are perfectly arranged to maximize benefit, indicating intentional design.

Key Terms
متحركة (mutaḥarrika)
مدار (madār)
ميل (mayl)
منطقة الفلك الأعظم (minṭaqat al-falak al-aʿẓam)
كيفية متوسطة (kayfiyya mutawassiṭa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is merely describing astronomical facts about the sun's motion.
Correction: The author is presenting a theological argument: the precise characteristics of the sun's motion are best explained by divine wisdom and intentional design.

Checkpoint

C) The sun would still provide equal light everywhere.
D) There would be no change in temperature.

Key Takeaway
The precise motion and inclination of the sun are perfectly calibrated to distribute heat and enable life, serving as a sign of divine wisdom.

The Sun's Apogee and Perigee: Making Land Habitable
Proof that the sun's varying distance causes land to emerge from water, making it habitable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun's varying distance (apogee and perigee) is used in a teleological argument to show divine design in making land suitable for living beings, especially humans.

Core Idea
The sun's orbit is designed to create habitable land through vapor displacement.

In this sixth benefit, the author presents a detailed physical explanation of how the sun's motion directly contributes to the emergence of dry land. By examining the sun's apogee and perigee, he shows that the resulting heat variation draws vapor to one side, uncovering the other. This elegant mechanism demonstrates purposeful design, preparing the earth for terrestrial life, particularly humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author builds the causal chain from the sun's motion to land exposure.
The passage is a continuous argument; pay attention to each cause-effect link.
2
Identify the key concepts
Find the terms 'apogee' (أوج) and 'perigee' (حضيض). Understand their role in the argument.
These are astronomical terms used in a theological context.
3
Trace the causal chain
List the sequential steps: sun's motion → apogee/perigee → heat increase → vapor attraction → land uncovered.
This chain is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the author emphasizes that this makes the earth suitable for 'land animals' and especially 'man'. What does this reveal about the argument's teleological nature?
The argument aims to show divine wisdom and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس من منافع وجود الشمس : إنه تعالى حركها . بحيث يحصل لها في الحركة أوج وحضيض ، فعند حصولها في الأوج تبعد عن الأرض ، وعند حصولها في الحضيض تقرب من الأرض . وبسبب القرب من الأرض ، تعظم السخونة من المواضع التي يحاذيها مدار الحضيض ، والسخونة جاذبة للرطوبات ، فلا جرم تجذب البخار إلى الجانب الذي يسامته حضيض الشمس ، وإذا انجذب البخار إلى ذلك الجانب ، انكشف الجانب الذي يحاذيه الأوج ، ولم يبق البحر على وجهه ، فصار أحد جوانب الأرض ، صالحاً لمساكن الحيوانات البرية . لا سيما الإنسان الذي هو أشرف الحيوانات الموجودة في عالم الكون والفساد .
The sixth aspect of the benefits of the existence of the sun: that He, exalted, moved it such that in its motion it has an apogee and a perigee. When it is at apogee, it is far from the earth, and when it is at perigee, it is near to the earth. And because of the nearness to the earth, the heat is great in the places that are opposite the orbit of perigee, and heat is attractive to moisture, so of course it draws vapor to the side that faces the perigee of the sun. And when the vapor is drawn to that side, the side that faces the apogee is uncovered, and the sea does not remain on its surface. Thus one side of the earth becomes suitable for dwellings of land animals, especially man, who is the most noble of the animals existing in the world of generation and corruption.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is the sixth face of the sun's benefits, focusing on its variation in distance (apogee and perigee). The author argues that God moved the sun in an elliptical orbit so that it approaches the earth (perigee) and recedes (apogee). During perigee, the increased heat draws vapor (bukhār) from the earth's surface. This vapor accumulates on the side facing the perigee, while the opposite side (facing apogee) becomes uncovered, causing the sea to recede and exposing land. This dry land becomes suitable for terrestrial animals, especially humans, who are the noblest beings in the sublunary world (ʿālam al-kawn wa-l-fasād). The argument is teleological: the sun's varying distance is not random but designed to make the earth habitable for living beings, particularly the most noble creature, man.

Key Terms
أوج (awj)
حضيض (ḥaḍīḍ)
بخار (bukhār)
عالم الكون والفساد (ʿālam al-kawn wa-l-fasād)
مساكن الحيوانات البرية (masākin al-ḥayawānāt al-barriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a scientific explanation for tectonic plate movement or continental drift.
Correction: This is a theological argument using observable phenomena (seasons, tides, etc.) to argue for purposeful design, not a modern geological theory.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The sun's apogee and perigee are designed to draw vapor to one side, exposing land for habitation, especially for humans.

The Sun's Alignment and Extreme Cold at the Poles
Argument that the sun's distance from poles causes extreme cold, limiting life to moderate zones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun's distance from direct alignment causes extreme cold at the poles, rendering them uninhabitable and highlighting the design of habitable moderate zones.

Core Idea
The sun's misalignment creates uninhabitable extremes, proving a purposeful habitable zone.

This passage presents the seventh benefit of the sun: its role in regulating temperature across the earth. The author argues that when the sun is far from direct alignment, cold becomes so intense that no life can exist. By examining the polar regions, we see that only moderate zones sustain life, pointing to purposeful design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'
2
Identify the cause and effect
The cause is the sun being far from alignment (musāmata). The effect is intense cold leading to no life and six months of day at the poles.
The example of the poles illustrates the extreme case.
3
Reflect on the teleological implication
Ask yourself: Why does this benefit matter? It shows that the sun's placement is not random but designed to make moderate zones livable.
This connects to the broader argument for divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع من منافع الشمس : إن كان موضع تكون الشمس بعيد جداً عن مسامتها ، اشتد البرد فيه . مثل الموضعين اللذين تحت القطبين ، فإنه لا يتكون هناك حيوان ، ولا ينبت فيه نبات ويكون هناك ستة أشهر نهاراً .
The seventh aspect of the benefits of the sun: If the place where the sun is formed is very far from its alignment, the cold intensifies there. Such as the two places under the poles, for no animal is formed there, nor does any plant grow, and there is six months of day.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is the seventh face of the sun's benefits, focusing on the extremities of the earth. The author states that when a place is very far from the sun's direct alignment (musāmata), the cold becomes intense. The example given is the two polar regions, where no animals or plants can survive, and there is a six-month day. This indicates that the sun's placement and motion are such that only certain zones are habitable, implying purposeful design. The extreme conditions at the poles serve as a contrast to the moderate regions, highlighting the wisdom in the sun's position and movement.

Key Terms
مسامتة
القطبين
ستة أشهر نهاراً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage claims the sun is farther from the Earth at the poles in general.
Correction: It is about the angle of incidence (alignment), not distance from Earth. The sun's rays hit at a very oblique angle, causing cold.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The extreme cold at the poles, caused by the sun's distance from alignment, prevents life, highlighting the design that confines habitable zones to moderate regions.

The Third Division: Northern Peoples and Their Traits
Description of the third division and its environmental causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author describes the third division of earth's inhabitants and explains their physical and moral characteristics as a result of their extreme northern location.

Core Idea
Climate shapes both body and character.

The author continues his classification of earth's inhabitants, moving to the third division: the northern peoples such as the Slavs and Russians. In this passage, he attributes their physical and moral traits to the extreme cold and humidity caused by their distance from the sun's path. This observation sets the stage for a broader argument about the connection between environment and civilization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the group described
Read the passage and identify which people are being discussed as the third division. Note their location and the author's description.
Look for the names Saqalibah (Slavs) and Russians.
2
Trace the causal chain
Follow the author's reasoning: distance from sun leads to cold and humidity, which leads to physical traits (white skin, blond hair) and moral traits (wild morals).
The author uses 'fa' (therefore) to indicate cause and effect.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider what this description implies about the author's view of the relationship between environment and human nature.
This is an example of environmental determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث من أهل الأرض: فهم الذين مساكنهم محاذية لبنات نعش. وهم : الصقالبة ود الروس، فإنهم لكثرة بعدهم عن ممر البروج وحرارة الشمس : صار البرد عليهم أبرد، والرطوبة الفصلية أكثر. لأنه ليس هناك من الحر ما ينشفها وينضجها، فلذلك صارت ألوانهم بيضاء وشعورهم سبطة شقرة، وأبدانهم عظيمة رخصة، وطبائعهم مائلة إلى البرد، وأخلاقهم وحشية. واعلم: أن كل واحد من هذين الطرفين. وهما الإقليم الأول والسابع، فإنه يقل فيه العمران، وتنقطع بعض العمارات عن بعض، لغلبة الكيفيتين الفاعلتين، ثم لا تزال العمارة في الإقليم الثاني والسادس والثالث والخامس تزداد ويقل الخراب فيه وأما الإقليم الرابع فإنه متواصل العمارات، ويقل الخراب فيه. وذلك لأجل فضل الوسط على الأطراف باعتدال المزاج. وكل هذه الأحوال: دالة على أن أحوال هذا العالم مربوط بأحوال الشمس.
As for the third division of the people of the earth: they are those whose dwellings are opposite the Banat Na'sh (Ursa Major). They are the Saqalibah (Slavs) and the Russians, for due to their great distance from the path of the zodiacal signs and the heat of the sun, the cold became colder upon them, and the seasonal humidity more abundant. Because there is not there any heat to dry and ripen it, therefore their colors became white, their hair lank and blond, their bodies large and flabby, their natures inclined toward cold, and their morals wild. And know: that each one of these two extremes—namely the first and seventh climes—civilization decreases in them, and some inhabited areas are cut off from others, due to the dominance of the two active qualities. Then civilization continues to increase in the second, sixth, third, and fifth climes, and ruin decreases in them. As for the fourth clime, its civilizations are continuous, and ruin decreases in it. That is because of the superiority of the middle over the extremities due to balanced temperament. And all these states indicate that the states of this world are connected to the states of the sun.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument from design. The author begins by describing the third division of people, the northern inhabitants (Slavs and Russians), whose extreme distance from the sun's path causes cold and humidity, resulting in white skin, blond hair, large bodies, and wild morals. Then he draws a general rule: the two extreme climes (first and seventh) have sparse and disconnected civilization because of the dominance of active qualities (extreme heat or cold). In contrast, the intermediate climes (second, sixth, third, fifth) have increasing civilization, and the fourth clime (the center) has the most continuous civilization with little ruin. This is attributed to the superiority of the middle over the extremities due to balanced temperament (i'tidal al-mizaj). He concludes that these observations point to the connection between earthly states and the sun, implying the wisdom of the Creator in arranging the cosmos.

Key Terms
صقالبة (Saqalibah/Slavs)
روس (Russians)
بنات نعش (Ursa Major)
مزاج (temperament)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the northern peoples have white skin and blond hair?
A) Because they are far from the sun's heat, resulting in cold and humidity.
B) Because they eat a special diet.
C) Because they live near mountains.
D) Because they are descendants of a different lineage.

Key Takeaway
The author attributes the physical and moral traits of northern peoples to climatic extremes, illustrating the environmental determinism of classical geography.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Virtue of the Fourth Clime and the Cosmic Order
General classification of climes and argument for the middle's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the fourth clime superior and how he uses this to argue for the connection between earthly conditions and the sun.

Core Idea
The middle clime is best because it is balanced.

Having described the extremes, the author now presents a general rule: the two extreme climes (first and seventh) have sparse civilization, while the intermediate climes improve, and the fourth clime is the most prosperous. He attributes this to balanced temperament and concludes that all earthly states are linked to the sun, pointing to a designed order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the pattern
Read the passage carefully and observe how the author describes civilization across the climes from extremes to middle.
Look for the progression: extremes (1st, 7th) → intermediates (2nd, 6th, 3rd, 5th) → middle (4th).
2
Identify the reason for superiority
Find the phrase where the author explains why the fourth clime has continuous civilization.
The key phrase is 'فضل الوسط على الأطراف باعتدال المزاج'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final sentence: 'وكل هذه الأحوال دالة على أن أحوال هذا العالم مربوط بأحوال الشمس.' What does this imply about the author's worldview?
The author sees a cosmic connection, which supports a teleological argument for a Creator.

وأما القسم الثالث من أهل الأرض: فهم الذين مساكنهم محاذية لبنات نعش. وهم : الصقالبة ود الروس، فإنهم لكثرة بعدهم عن ممر البروج وحرارة الشمس : صار البرد عليهم أبرد، والرطوبة الفصلية أكثر. لأنه ليس هناك من الحر ما ينشفها وينضجها، فلذلك صارت ألوانهم بيضاء وشعورهم سبطة شقرة، وأبدانهم عظيمة رخصة، وطبائعهم مائلة إلى البرد، وأخلاقهم وحشية. واعلم: أن كل واحد من هذين الطرفين. وهما الإقليم الأول والسابع، فإنه يقل فيه العمران، وتنقطع بعض العمارات عن بعض، لغلبة الكيفيتين الفاعلتين، ثم لا تزال العمارة في الإقليم الثاني والسادس والثالث والخامس تزداد ويقل الخراب فيه وأما الإقليم الرابع فإنه متواصل العمارات، ويقل الخراب فيه. وذلك لأجل فضل الوسط على الأطراف باعتدال المزاج. وكل هذه الأحوال: دالة على أن أحوال هذا العالم مربوط بأحوال الشمس.
As for the third division of the people of the earth: they are those whose dwellings are opposite the Banat Na'sh (Ursa Major). They are the Saqalibah (Slavs) and the Russians, for due to their great distance from the path of the zodiacal signs and the heat of the sun, the cold became colder upon them, and the seasonal humidity more abundant. Because there is not there any heat to dry and ripen it, therefore their colors became white, their hair lank and blond, their bodies large and flabby, their natures inclined toward cold, and their morals wild. And know: that each one of these two extremes—namely the first and seventh climes—civilization decreases in them, and some inhabited areas are cut off from others, due to the dominance of the two active qualities. Then civilization continues to increase in the second, sixth, third, and fifth climes, and ruin decreases in them. As for the fourth clime, its civilizations are continuous, and ruin decreases in it. That is because of the superiority of the middle over the extremities due to balanced temperament. And all these states indicate that the states of this world are connected to the states of the sun.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument from design. The author begins by describing the third division of people, the northern inhabitants (Slavs and Russians), whose extreme distance from the sun's path causes cold and humidity, resulting in white skin, blond hair, large bodies, and wild morals. Then he draws a general rule: the two extreme climes (first and seventh) have sparse and disconnected civilization because of the dominance of active qualities (extreme heat or cold). In contrast, the intermediate climes (second, sixth, third, fifth) have increasing civilization, and the fourth clime (the center) has the most continuous civilization with little ruin. This is attributed to the superiority of the middle over the extremities due to balanced temperament (i'tidal al-mizaj). He concludes that these observations point to the connection between earthly states and the sun, implying the wisdom of the Creator in arranging the cosmos.

Key Terms
إقليم (clime)
اعتدال المزاج (balanced temperament)
عمران (civilization)
كيفيتان فاعلتان (two active qualities)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the fourth clime is superior because it is physically larger or has more resources, but the author specifically attributes it to balanced temperament (i'tidal al-mizaj).
Correction: The author explicitly states that the superiority is due to the middle region having a balanced temperament, which moderates the extremes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the reason for the fourth clime's continuous civilization?
A) It has abundant water resources.
B) It has a balanced temperament.
C) It is closest to the sun.
D) It has fewer inhabitants.

Key Takeaway
The balanced temperament of the fourth clime makes it the most civilized, and this pattern shows that earthly conditions are connected to the sun, pointing to a designed order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your own life: which 'clime' are you in? Is your spiritual and worldly pursuit balanced, or are you leaning toward an extreme (like the first or seventh clime) where your faith or character becomes sparse? What one step can you take today to return to the balanced middle?
?
The sun's connection to earthly states is a sign of Allah's precise order. Do you recognize His hand in the small, consistent rhythms of your day—the rising sun, the seasons, your own heartbeat? If you truly believed these are not random, how would your gratitude or sense of accountability change?
?
The fourth clime is most civilized because of balance. What is the 'fourth clime' in your relationships, your spending, or your worship? Name one area where you have tipped into excess or neglect, and commit to a concrete action that restores balance before tomorrow ends.
