The First Evidence: Creation as Measurement and Order
Al-Razi introduces the first type of evidence from creation and its foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's methodology in using creation as evidence for God's attributes, and why he defines creation as 'measurement' (taqdīr).

Core Idea
Creation as evidence means measuring the cosmic order, not just pointing at existence.

Al-Razi begins a detailed exploration of the evidences from creation. He clarifies that the term 'creation' (khalq) as used in the Quran refers not to bringing something from nothing, but to the precise measurement and proportional arrangement of the cosmos. This shift in definition grounds the argument in observable order rather than sheer power, making it a rational proof accessible to all. In this lesson, we examine the opening of this argument, where he sets out the foundational principle that all bodies are similar, and then begins the first aspect by noting the shared characteristics of celestial and elemental bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the attributes
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the list of divine attributes at the beginning: knowing, powerful, wise, free-acting, merciful. This sets the stage for the evidence that follows.
The attributes are a key part of the argument's conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of 'khalq' as 'taqdīr'
Al-Razi explicitly states that 'creation' (khalq) means 'measurement' (taqdīr). Reflect on how this redefinition focuses the evidence on the quantitative and proportional order in the cosmos rather than mere existence.
This is a crucial shift from a common-sense understanding of creation.
3
Trace the logic: similarity of bodies to the first aspect
Al-Razi explains that all types of evidence from creation are built on the principle that bodies are similar (tamāthul al-ajsām). Then he begins the first aspect: celestial and elemental bodies share in 'entirety' (tamām). Consider what this shared entirety might refer to.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full first aspect is completed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع العلائل على أن الله العالم قادر حكيم، مختار رحيم
اعلم أنا قد بالغنا في شرح هذا الباب في التفسير الكبير ونحن نذكر ههنا جملا وأصولا فنقول:
النوع الأول:
اعلم أن الاستدلال بخلق السموات والأرض على وجود الإله القادر الحكيم كثير الورود في القرآن. وقد علمت أن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير فوجب علينا أن نبين أقسام التقدير والتدبير في أجرام السموات، وجميع هذه الوجوه مبنية على تماثل الأجسام فنقول:
أما الوجه الأول: فهو أن الأجسام الفلكية والعنصرية متشاركة في تمام
Types of evidences that God is knowing, powerful, wise, free-acting, merciful. Know that we have elaborated on explaining this chapter in The Great Commentary (al-Tafsīr al-Kabīr) and we will mention here summaries (jumal) and principles (uṣūl), so we say: The first type: Know that reasoning by the creation (khalq) of the heavens and earth for the existence of the powerful, wise deity is frequent in the Quran. And you have known that creation (khalq) is [a term for] measurement (taqdīr), so it is necessary for us to explain the divisions of measurement (taqdīr) and arrangement (tadbīr) in the celestial bodies (ajrām al-samāwāt), and all these aspects are based on the similarity (tamāthul) of bodies (ajsām), so we say: As for the first aspect (wajh awwal): it is that the celestial and elemental bodies share in entirety...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a section on the types of evidence (علائل) that establish God's attributes. The author, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, first lists the attributes: knowing (عالم), powerful (قادر), wise (حكيم), free-acting (مختار), and merciful (رحيم). He then notes that he has already provided a full explanation of this chapter in his larger work, al-Tafsīr al-Kabīr, but here he will present concise summaries and principles. He proceeds to the first type of evidence: reasoning from the creation of the heavens and earth to prove the existence of a powerful, wise deity—a method frequently used in the Qur'an. Al-Rāzī then clarifies that 'creation' (خلق) linguistically means 'measurement' (تقدير). From this definition, he derives the need to explain the divisions of 'measurement' and 'arrangement' (تدبير) observable in the celestial bodies. He states that all these aspects or 'faces' (وجوه) of evidence are built upon the premise that bodies (الأجسام) are similar (تماثل) to each other. He then begins the first aspect: that the celestial bodies (الأجسام الفلكية) and the elemental bodies (الأجسام العنصرية) share in 'entirety' (تمام). The passage cuts off here, so the completion of this first aspect is not included in this lesson. The reader should note that al-Rāzī is laying a foundation: because bodies are similar, the observation of order and measurement in some bodies can lead to conclusions about a wise creator.

Key Terms
علائل
خلق
تقدير
تماثل الأجسام
أجرام السموات
وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'خلق' means creation ex nihilo (إبداع), implying evidence from mere existence.
Correction: Al-Rāzī specifies that 'خلق' means 'تقدير' (measurement), shifting the focus to the quantitative and proportional order in the cosmos as evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rāzī, what is the meaning of 'خلق' (creation) in the context of this evidence?
A) Bringing into existence from nothing (إيجاد من العدم)
B) Measurement and proportion (تقدير)
C) Formation and shaping (تكوين)
D) Origination and invention (ابتداع)

Key Takeaway
The evidence from creation focuses on the precise measurement and arrangement in celestial bodies, not mere existence, and is grounded in the similarity of all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky, do you see only stars or do you see the precise measurement (taqdīr) that testifies to a wise Creator? What would it take for you to truly internalize this evidence and let it transform your certainty?
?
Al-Razi says that all bodies are similar, making the cosmic order a universal sign. How does your own body—its exact proportions and functions—reflect that same design? Write down one specific moment today when you can pause to recognize the taqdīr in your own creation.
?
If the evidence from measurement is so clear, why do you still struggle with doubt in Allah's wisdom? What would change if you began each day by reciting the names al-ʿAlīm, al-Qādir, al-Ḥakīm and truly felt their meaning in your life?

Introduction and First Five Arguments from Celestial Diversity
Al-Razi introduces the argument and presents the first five proofs from celestial and elemental diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational premise that bodies are similar in essence, and how the first five arguments apply this to different levels of celestial and elemental diversity to prove a free creator.

Core Idea
Shared essence plus differing attributes proves a free chooser.

Al-Razi begins by noting the Qur'anic method of inferring God from creation. Since creation implies planning, he sets out to explain the types of planning in heavenly bodies. He then presents the first five arguments, each showing that similarity in essence yet difference in attributes necessitates a free agent, not a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qur'anic context
Read the opening sentence where Al-Razi connects the argument to the Qur'an's frequent reference to creation. Note that 'creation' (khalq) means 'planning' (taqdir).
This sets the stage for a rational analysis of design in the cosmos.
2
Grasp the fundamental premise
Identify the key principle: 'all these aspects are based on the similarity of bodies' (tamathul al-ajsam). This means that bodies share the same essence (mahiyya).
This is a kalām axiom; without it, the arguments would not follow.
3
Analyze the first argument
Read the first argument carefully. Celestial and elemental bodies share the same essence yet differ in attributes. This difference cannot come from the essence or its necessary concomitants, so it must come from a free agent.
This is the template for all subsequent arguments.
4
Follow arguments 2-5
Each argument applies the same logic to different pairs: celestial spheres, spheres vs planets, planets among themselves, and the four elements. Notice the pattern: equal in essence, different in attributes, therefore free agent.
The fifth argument introduces the concept of 'places' (ahiyaz) as well.
5
Reflect on the pattern
Consider why the argument works only if bodies are essentially the same. If they were different in essence, the diversity could be due to their natures. The premise forces the conclusion to a voluntary creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن الاستدلال بخلق السموات والأرض على وجود الإله القادر الحكيم : كثير الورود في القرآن. وقد علمت : أن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير فوجب علينا أن نبين أقسام التقدير والتدبير في اجرام السموات ، وجميع هذه الوجوه مبنية على تماثل الأجسام . فنقول :

أما الوجه الأول : فهو أن الأجسام الفلكية والعنصرية متشاركة في تمام الماهية. ولا شك أن الفلكيات مخالفة للعنصريات بالصفات التي باعتبارها صارت الأفلاك أفلاكاً والعناصر عناصراً. فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص ليس لتلك الذوات ولا لشيء من لوازمها. فوجب أن يكون لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثاني : فهو أن كل واحد من الأفلاك مخالف للآخر في الصفات والمقادير. فوجب أن يكون ذلك لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثالث : فهو أن أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، ووجه الدلالة ما ذكرناه.

وأما الوجه الرابع : فهو أن أجرام الكواكب متساوية في الذوات، ومختلفة في الصفات. فيعود الدليل المذكور.

وأما الوجه الخامس : فهو أن العناصر متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات والأحياز.

وأما الوجه السادس : فهو أن المواليد الثلاثة متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.

وأما الوجه السابع : فهو أن العناصر متساوية المواليد في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.

وأما الوجه الثامن : فهو أن الأجرام الفلكية مختلفة في المقادير. لأن بعضها أعظم من بعض، مع استوائها في الذوات.

وأما الوجه التاسع : فهو اختلاف مواقع أجزائها. فإن الفلك المعين وقع بعض أجزائه في عمقه وداخل ثخنه، وبعضها في خارج سطحه.

وأما الوجه العاشر : فهو اختلافها في كيفية الحركات. فبعضها سريعة.
Know: that inferring from the creation of the heavens and the earth the existence of a powerful, wise deity is frequent in the Qur'an. And you have known that creation (al-khalq) is planning (al-taqdir). So it is necessary for us to explain the types of planning and arrangement in the bodies of the heavens, and all these aspects are based on the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. So we say:

As for the first aspect: it is that celestial and elemental bodies share in complete essence. There is no doubt that celestial bodies differ from elemental bodies in the attributes by virtue of which the celestial spheres became spheres and the elements became elements. So it is necessary that this specification (al-ikhtisas) is not due to those essences nor due to any of their necessary concomitants. So it is necessary that it is due to the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar).

As for the second aspect: it is that each of the celestial spheres differs from another in attributes and magnitudes. So it is necessary that this is due to the free agent.

As for the third aspect: it is that the bodies of the celestial spheres differ from the bodies of the planets in attributes, and the indication (wajh al-dalala) is what we mentioned.

As for the fourth aspect: it is that the bodies of the planets are equal in essences and differ in attributes. Thus the mentioned proof applies again.

As for the fifth aspect: it is that the elements are equal in corporality (al-jirmiyya) and differ in attributes and places (al-ahiyaz).

As for the sixth aspect: it is that the three genera of generated beings (al-mawalid al-thalatha) are equal in corporality and differ in attributes.

As for the seventh aspect: it is that the elemental bodies underlying generated beings are equal in corporality and differ in attributes.

As for the eighth aspect: it is that celestial bodies differ in magnitudes; for some are greater than others, while they are equal in essences.

As for the ninth aspect: it is the difference of positions of their parts. For a particular celestial sphere, some of its parts occur in its depth and inside its thickness, and some occur on its outer surface.

As for the tenth aspect: it is their difference in the manner of motions; for some are fast.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a set of ten arguments (wujuh) from the diversity of celestial and elemental bodies to prove the existence of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) who is wise and powerful. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, introduces the topic by noting that inferring God from creation is common in the Quran, and since creation implies planning (taqdir), he will explain the types of planning in heavenly bodies. All these arguments are built on the premise that bodies are similar in essence (tamathul al-ajsam).

1. First argument: Celestial and elemental bodies share the same complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya), but they differ in attributes. This difference cannot be due to their essence or any necessary concomitant of it. Therefore, it must be due to a free agent who chooses to assign these attributes.

2. Second argument: Each celestial sphere differs from the others in attributes and magnitudes. Since they are all similar in essence, this diversity again points to a free agent.

3. Third argument: The bodies of the celestial spheres differ from the bodies of the planets (al-kawakib) in attributes; the same reasoning applies.

4. Fourth argument: The planetary bodies are equal in essence but differ in attributes, so the same proof applies.

5. Fifth argument: The four elements (earth, water, air, fire) are equal in corporality (jirmiyya) but differ in attributes and spatial positions, necessitating a free agent.

6. Sixth argument: The three genera of generated beings (mawalid: minerals, plants, animals) are equal in corporality but differ in attributes, again requiring a free agent.

7. Seventh argument: The elemental substances that serve as matter for generated beings are equal in corporality but differ in attributes, again similar reasoning.

8. Eighth argument: Celestial bodies differ in magnitude (some larger, some smaller) while being equal in essence, thus requiring a free agent.

9. Ninth argument: The positions of parts within a celestial sphere vary (some deeper, some on the surface), which cannot be due to the essence itself.

10. Tenth argument: The celestial spheres differ in the manner of their motions (some fast, some slow), indicating a free agent.

Each argument follows the same logical structure: similarity in essence, difference in accidents or attributes, thus the difference cannot be necessary, so it must be from a free choice. This is a classic kalam argument for a voluntary Creator.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
تماثل الأجسام
تخالف بالصفات
اختصاص
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments prove that the creator is one.
Correction: The arguments only prove a free agent, not necessarily that there is only one. Additionally, they rely on the kalām premise that bodies are similar in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why must the differences between celestial and elemental bodies be due to a free agent?
A) Because they share the same essence and the differences cannot come from the essence itself.
B) Because celestial bodies are larger than elemental bodies.
C) Because elemental bodies are changeable.
D) Because the Qur'an says so.

Key Takeaway
The first five arguments establish a recurring pattern: shared essence plus differing attributes necessitates a free creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the diversity of the universe—celestial spheres, elements, planets—do you see it as a sign pointing to a purposeful Creator, or do you take it for granted? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly believed that every difference, from the stars to your own circumstances, is chosen by a free agent? Will you start looking for His choice in the details of your day?
?
This argument shows that where essence is shared but attributes differ, chance cannot be the cause. Consider your own life: In which areas do you assume that your differences from others—your talents, trials, or opportunities—are random or deserved, rather than divinely specified? How would your gratitude or patience change if you attributed every specification to Allah's free choice?
?
Al-Razi uses the principle that bodies are similar in essence. If you reflect on your own nature and the nature of others, do you treat people as if they share a common essence created by Allah, or do you let differences in attributes make you forget their common origin? What would change if you saw every human as sharing the same createdness, with differences only as signs from the Free Agent?

Arguments 6-10: Further Evidence from Generation and Celestial Properties
Al-Razi presents the remaining five arguments from celestial and terrestrial diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how arguments 6-10 extend the same logic to generated beings, magnitudes, positions, and motions, reinforcing the proof for a free creator.

Core Idea
Diversity in magnitudes, positions, and motions among similar bodies points to a free agent.

Al-Razi continues his series of arguments, applying the same principle to new domains: the three genera of generated beings (minerals, plants, animals), their underlying material, and the varying magnitudes, positions, and motions of celestial bodies. Each case reinforces the conclusion that such diversity cannot be explained by necessity alone.

Guided Path
1
Review the pattern
Recall that all arguments rely on the similarity of bodies. In each new case, we will see the same structure: equality in essence, difference in attributes.
Arguments 6-10 introduce new categories of objects.
2
Examine arguments 6 and 7
Argument 6 focuses on the three types of generated beings (mawalid): minerals, plants, animals. Argument 7 considers the elemental matter underlying these beings. Both are equal in corporality but differ in attributes.
Note: 'al-mawalid al-thalatha' refers to minerals, plants, and animals.
3
Study arguments 8-10
These arguments turn to celestial bodies themselves: varying magnitudes (8), positions of parts (9), and speeds of motion (10). Despite sharing essence, these differences require a free agent.
Argument 9 mentions 'depth and thickness' of a sphere.
4
Synthesize the overall proof
Consider how all ten arguments together build a cumulative case. Each is independent but all converge on the same conclusion: the cosmos exhibits marks of deliberate planning and free choice.
Al-Razi does not claim these are exhaustive, but they are representative.

Source Text

اعلم : أن الاستدلال بخلق السموات والأرض على وجود الإله القادر الحكيم : كثير الورود في القرآن. وقد علمت : أن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير فوجب علينا أن نبين أقسام التقدير والتدبير في اجرام السموات ، وجميع هذه الوجوه مبنية على تماثل الأجسام . فنقول :

أما الوجه الأول : فهو أن الأجسام الفلكية والعنصرية متشاركة في تمام الماهية. ولا شك أن الفلكيات مخالفة للعنصريات بالصفات التي باعتبارها صارت الأفلاك أفلاكاً والعناصر عناصراً. فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص ليس لتلك الذوات ولا لشيء من لوازمها. فوجب أن يكون لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثاني : فهو أن كل واحد من الأفلاك مخالف للآخر في الصفات والمقادير. فوجب أن يكون ذلك لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثالث : فهو أن أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، ووجه الدلالة ما ذكرناه.

وأما الوجه الرابع : فهو أن أجرام الكواكب متساوية في الذوات، ومختلفة في الصفات. فيعود الدليل المذكور.

وأما الوجه الخامس : فهو أن العناصر متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات والأحياز.

وأما الوجه السادس : فهو أن المواليد الثلاثة متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.

وأما الوجه السابع : فهو أن العناصر متساوية المواليد في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.

وأما الوجه الثامن : فهو أن الأجرام الفلكية مختلفة في المقادير. لأن بعضها أعظم من بعض، مع استوائها في الذوات.

وأما الوجه التاسع : فهو اختلاف مواقع أجزائها. فإن الفلك المعين وقع بعض أجزائه في عمقه وداخل ثخنه، وبعضها في خارج سطحه.

وأما الوجه العاشر : فهو اختلافها في كيفية الحركات. فبعضها سريعة.
Know: that inferring from the creation of the heavens and the earth the existence of a powerful, wise deity is frequent in the Qur'an. And you have known that creation (al-khalq) is planning (al-taqdir). So it is necessary for us to explain the types of planning and arrangement in the bodies of the heavens, and all these aspects are based on the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. So we say:

As for the first aspect: it is that celestial and elemental bodies share in complete essence. There is no doubt that celestial bodies differ from elemental bodies in the attributes by virtue of which the celestial spheres became spheres and the elements became elements. So it is necessary that this specification (al-ikhtisas) is not due to those essences nor due to any of their necessary concomitants. So it is necessary that it is due to the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar).

As for the second aspect: it is that each of the celestial spheres differs from another in attributes and magnitudes. So it is necessary that this is due to the free agent.

As for the third aspect: it is that the bodies of the celestial spheres differ from the bodies of the planets in attributes, and the indication (wajh al-dalala) is what we mentioned.

As for the fourth aspect: it is that the bodies of the planets are equal in essences and differ in attributes. Thus the mentioned proof applies again.

As for the fifth aspect: it is that the elements are equal in corporality (al-jirmiyya) and differ in attributes and places (al-ahiyaz).

As for the sixth aspect: it is that the three genera of generated beings (al-mawalid al-thalatha) are equal in corporality and differ in attributes.

As for the seventh aspect: it is that the elemental bodies underlying generated beings are equal in corporality and differ in attributes.

As for the eighth aspect: it is that celestial bodies differ in magnitudes; for some are greater than others, while they are equal in essences.

As for the ninth aspect: it is the difference of positions of their parts. For a particular celestial sphere, some of its parts occur in its depth and inside its thickness, and some occur on its outer surface.

As for the tenth aspect: it is their difference in the manner of motions; for some are fast.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a set of ten arguments (wujuh) from the diversity of celestial and elemental bodies to prove the existence of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) who is wise and powerful. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, introduces the topic by noting that inferring God from creation is common in the Quran, and since creation implies planning (taqdir), he will explain the types of planning in heavenly bodies. All these arguments are built on the premise that bodies are similar in essence (tamathul al-ajsam).

1. First argument: Celestial and elemental bodies share the same complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya), but they differ in attributes. This difference cannot be due to their essence or any necessary concomitant of it. Therefore, it must be due to a free agent who chooses to assign these attributes.

2. Second argument: Each celestial sphere differs from the others in attributes and magnitudes. Since they are all similar in essence, this diversity again points to a free agent.

3. Third argument: The bodies of the celestial spheres differ from the bodies of the planets (al-kawakib) in attributes; the same reasoning applies.

4. Fourth argument: The planetary bodies are equal in essence but differ in attributes, so the same proof applies.

5. Fifth argument: The four elements (earth, water, air, fire) are equal in corporality (jirmiyya) but differ in attributes and spatial positions, necessitating a free agent.

6. Sixth argument: The three genera of generated beings (mawalid: minerals, plants, animals) are equal in corporality but differ in attributes, again requiring a free agent.

7. Seventh argument: The elemental substances that serve as matter for generated beings are equal in corporality but differ in attributes, again similar reasoning.

8. Eighth argument: Celestial bodies differ in magnitude (some larger, some smaller) while being equal in essence, thus requiring a free agent.

9. Ninth argument: The positions of parts within a celestial sphere vary (some deeper, some on the surface), which cannot be due to the essence itself.

10. Tenth argument: The celestial spheres differ in the manner of their motions (some fast, some slow), indicating a free agent.

Each argument follows the same logical structure: similarity in essence, difference in accidents or attributes, thus the difference cannot be necessary, so it must be from a free choice. This is a classic kalam argument for a voluntary Creator.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
تماثل الأجسام
المواليد الثلاثة
تخالف بالصفات
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The differences in magnitudes and motions could be due to the inherent nature of the bodies themselves.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that since the bodies are equal in essence, their differences in accidents cannot be necessary but must be by choice.

Checkpoint
According to argument 8, why do celestial bodies differ in magnitude?
A) Because their essences differ.
B) Because a free agent made some larger and some smaller.
C) Because larger bodies are closer to the center.
D) Because motion causes size differences.

Key Takeaway
Arguments 6-10 extend the proof for a free creator to generated beings, magnitudes, positions, and motions, showing that diversity in accidents among similar essences always points to a voluntary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe countless differences among created things—your own body, the sky, the seasons. Are you content to see these as mere natural facts, or do they compel you to acknowledge a Willing Agent who personally chose each detail? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly lived by al-Razi's reasoning?
?
Think of one area in your life where you assume 'that's just how things are'—a habit, a personality trait, a circumstance. How does this lesson challenge you to see that diversity itself points to a free Creator who could have made you otherwise? Will you let that recognition move you to gratitude and submission today?
?
Al-Razi argues that even the positions of stars and the speeds of their motions are not random but chosen. Do you live as if every detail of your life is appointed by a free Agent, or do you sometimes treat events as meaningless chance? What would it mean for you to trust His choice in a specific current difficulty?

Four Arguments from Celestial Differences for a Free Agent
Al-Matalib presents four arguments from differences in celestial bodies for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differences in celestial bodies (among themselves and from elemental bodies) logically necessitate a free agent.

Core Idea
Differences in creation cannot arise from essences alone; they point to a free chooser.

The author presents a series of concise arguments based on observable differences in the celestial realm. Each argument follows the same logical pattern: if the differences cannot be explained by the intrinsic nature of the bodies or their necessary properties, they must be attributed to an external free agent. This lesson unpacks the first four such arguments, showing how the same principle applies in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the rhythm of the arguments sink in before digging deeper.
Notice the repeated phrase 'fawajaba an yakuna...' (so it is necessary that...).
2
Identify the four arguments
The passage lists four distinct 'wujuh' (aspects). Find each one and note the specific differences highlighted in each (celestial vs elemental, sphere vs sphere, spheres vs planets, planets among themselves).
3
Understand the logical pattern
Each argument shares a structure: difference cannot be from essences or necessary accompaniments (lawazim), therefore it is from a free agent. Trace this structure in each of the four cases.
The conclusion is identical across all four: 'fawajaba an yakuna dhalika li-ajli al-fa'il al-mukhtar'.
4
Reflect on the philosophical force
Consider why the author thinks these differences cannot be accidental or from the bodies themselves. This reasoning rules out materialism and chance, pointing to a deliberate choice.
Contrast with the common misunderstanding that these differences could be natural or inherent.
5
Test your understanding
Try the checkpoint question to see if you can apply the logical pattern. Then attempt the activity to solidify the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية. ولا شك أن الفلكيات مخالفة للعنصريات بالصفات، التي باعتبارها صارت الأفلاك أفلاكاً والعناصر عناصراً، فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص ليس لتلك الذوات، ولا لشيء من لوازمها. فوجب أن يكون لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثاني: فهو أن كل واحد من الأفلاك مخالف للآخر في الصفات والمقادير. فوجب أن يكون ذلك لأجل الفاعل المختار.

وأما الوجه الثالث: فهو أن أجرام الأفلاك مخالفة لأجرام الكواكب في الصفات، ووجه الدلالة ما ذكرناه.

وأما الوجه الرابع: فهو أن أجرام الكواكب متساوية في الذوات، ومختلفة في الصفات. فيعود الدليل المذكور.
The essence. There is no doubt that celestial bodies are different from elemental bodies in attributes, by which the celestial spheres became spheres and the elements became elements. So it is necessary that this specification is not for those essences, nor for any of their necessary accompaniments. So it is necessary that it is for the free agent.

As for the second aspect: it is that each one of the celestial spheres is different from the other in attributes and magnitudes. So it is necessary that that is for the free agent.

As for the third aspect: it is that the bodies of the celestial spheres are different from the bodies of the planets in attributes, and the indication is what we mentioned.

As for the fourth aspect: it is that the bodies of the planets are equal in essences and different in attributes. So the mentioned proof returns.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents the first four of ten arguments (wujuh) for the necessity of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar). The author begins by stating that celestial bodies (al-fulkiyyat) differ from elemental bodies (al-unsuriyyat) in attributes—attributes that make them what they are. This difference cannot arise from their essences or their necessary accompaniments (lawazim), so it must be due to a free chooser. The second argument: each celestial sphere differs from another in attributes and magnitudes, requiring a free agent. The third argument: the bodies of spheres differ from the bodies of planets in attributes, using the same indication. The fourth argument: although planets are equal in essence, they differ in attributes, thus the same proof applies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the differences between celestial and elemental bodies could be due to chance or inherent nature.
Correction: The author argues that such differences cannot be from the essences themselves or their necessary accompaniments, so they must be from a deliberate free agent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Differences in attributes among bodies of the same kind or between different kinds cannot be explained by their essences alone; they point to a free agent who specifies them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Three Arguments from Equality in Corporeality and Difference in Attributes
Three arguments from differences among elements and generations for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equality of elements and generations in corporeality combined with their differences in attributes points to a free agent.

Core Idea
Difference in attributes despite equal corporeality demands a chooser.

In this passage, the author presents three concise arguments that build on a common observation: things that share the same fundamental nature (corporeality) yet differ in qualities cannot be explained by their own essence. This points to an external agent who freely imparts those differences. These arguments are the fifth, sixth, and seventh aspects in a longer series.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the parallel structure: each begins with 'As for the [number] aspect' and states equality in corporeality and difference in attributes.
2
Identify the common premise
Observe that the arguments rely on the premise that equality in corporeality cannot produce difference in attributes. This is the key logical move.
3
Connect to the free agent
Ask yourself: why does this difference require a free agent rather than natural causes? The commentary explains that the difference cannot come from the essence, so it must come from an external chooser.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الخامس: فهو أن العناصر متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات والأحياز.

وأما الوجه السادس: فهو أن المواليد الثلاثة متساوية في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.

وأما الوجه السابع: فهو أن العناصر متساوية المواليد في الجرمية، ومختلفة في الصفات.
As for the fifth aspect: it is that the elements are equal in corporeality, and differ in attributes and places.

As for the sixth aspect: it is that the three generations are equal in corporeality, and differ in attributes.

As for the seventh aspect: it is that the elements are equal in generation in corporeality, and differ in attributes.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage contains three more arguments (the fifth, sixth, and seventh) for the necessity of a free agent. The fifth argument: the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) are equal in being bodies (jirmiyya) but differ in their qualities and spatial positions. This difference cannot be from their essence, so it requires a free agent. The sixth argument: the three generations (al-mawalid al-thalatha: minerals, plants, animals) are equal in corporeality but differ in attributes, again pointing to a free agent. The seventh argument is similar: elements and generations are equal in corporeality yet differ in attributes, reinforcing the same point.

Equality in fundamental nature (corporeality) combined with observed differences in attributes proves the need for a free agent who chooses those differences.

?

?

?
This lesson shows that the order of creation requires a free agent. When you observe the consistency in nature—like the fixed attributes of elements and generations—does it stir in you a sense of awe and dependence? Or do you take it for granted? Resolve today to see every difference around you as a sign of the Chooser.

Eighth Argument: Differences in Magnitudes
Eighth argument from magnitude differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the difference in magnitudes of celestial bodies, despite their equal essences, points to a free and wise creator.

Core Idea
Equal essences with different sizes demand a non-necessary cause.

The eighth argument examines celestial bodies that share the same essential nature yet vary in size. Such variation cannot arise from the essence itself, indicating external determination by a free agent. This passage sets up the reasoning from magnitude differences.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation for the eighth argument. Notice the key phrase 'مع استوائها في الذوات' (despite their equality in essences).
This is the premise that essence alone cannot explain size variation.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has two premises: (1) celestial bodies differ in magnitude, (2) they are equal in essence. The conclusion is implied: therefore, the difference must come from an external agent.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if essences are identical, why are some bodies larger? The only sufficient reason is a free chooser who assigned different sizes by wisdom.
This connects to the broader proof of a wise creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثامن: فهو أن الأجرام الفلكية مختلفة في المقادير. لأن بعضها أعظم من بعض، مع استوائها في الذوات.

وأما الوجه التاسع: فهو اختلاف مواقع أجزائها. فإن الفلك المعين وقع بعض أجزائه في عمقه، وداخل ثخنه، وبعضها في خارج سطحه.

وأما الوجه العاشر: فهو اختلافها في كيفية الحركات. فبعضها سريعة، وبعضها بطيئة. بل نقول: هذا المعنى حاصل في الفلك الواحد، فإنه إذا استدار الفلك، فكل نقطة تفرض في سطحه فقد استدارت أيضاً. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: أما قطبا الكرة، فهما ساكنان. وأما منطقتها فهي متحركة على أسرع الوجوه، وكل نقطة هي أقرب إلى المنطقة، فحركتها أسرع من حركة النقطة، التي هي أبعد من المنطقة. وإذا كانت تلك النقط غير متناهية، وجب أن يقال: إنه لا نهاية لمراتب الحركات الواقعة في الكرة الواحدة، بسبب السرعة والبطء. فحركة فلك الثوابت، وإن كانت بطيئة جداً إلا أن الدائرة الصغيرة جداً، المستديرة حول قطبها، إنما تتم حركتها في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، ومدار تلك الدائرة أصغر من مدار قشر الجاورسة الواحدة. والكرة الواحدة افترض فيها هذه الحركات المختلفة، بسبب البطء والسرعة، وتلك النقط والأجزاء بأسرها متشابهة. فاختصاص كل واحد منها بحركته الخاصة، وبمقداره الخاص في البطء والسرعة، لا بد وأن يكون بتقدير العزيز العليم.
As for the eighth aspect: it is that the celestial bodies differ in magnitudes. Because some of them are greater than others, despite their equality in essences.

As for the ninth aspect: it is the difference of the positions of their parts. For a particular sphere, some of its parts are located in its depth and inside its thickness, and some are on its outer surface.

As for the tenth aspect: it is their difference in the manner of motions. Some are fast, some are slow. Rather we say: this meaning occurs in a single sphere. For when the sphere rotates, every point assumed on its surface also rotates. If you know this, we say: As for the two poles of the sphere, they are stationary. As for its equatorial region, it moves in the fastest way. And every point that is closer to the equator moves faster than a point that is farther from the equator. And since those points are infinite, it must be said that there is no limit to the ranks of motions occurring in a single sphere, due to speed and slowness. So the motion of the sphere of fixed stars, although very slow, yet a very small circle revolving around its pole completes its motion in a period of thirty-six thousand years, and the orbit of that circle is smaller than the orbit of the shell of a single Jāwarsa. And in a single sphere are assumed these different motions, due to slowness and speed, and those points and parts are all similar. So the specification of each of them with its particular motion and its particular measure of slowness and speed must be by the determination of the Mighty, the Knowing.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth, ninth, and tenth arguments. The eighth argument: celestial bodies differ in magnitude while being equal in essence, so a free agent is needed. The ninth argument: the positions of parts within a sphere differ (some in depth, some on surface), requiring a chooser. The tenth argument is lengthy and detailed: motions within a single sphere vary infinitely. The author explains that in a rotating sphere, points closer to the equator move faster, while poles are stationary. Since points are infinite, ranks of motion are infinite. Even the sphere of fixed stars has extremely slow motion, with a small circle taking 36,000 years. All parts are similar yet each has a specific speed, so this must be by the precise determination of the Almighty, All-Knowing.

Key Terms
أجرام فلكية
مقادير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that size differences are inherent to the nature of each body, but the argument denies this by asserting equal essences.
Correction: If essences are truly equal, any difference in accidents like size must come from an external cause, not from within.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise that makes the eighth argument work?
A) Celestial bodies are of different sizes.
B) Celestial bodies are equal in essence.
C) Some bodies are larger than others.
D) Celestial bodies move in orbits.

Key Takeaway
When things share the same essence but differ in magnitude, that difference must come from a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that identical essences can differ in size only by an external cause. In your own life, do you ever assume that your inherent nature (your 'essence') determines everything about you, or do you acknowledge the role of Allah's will in shaping your circumstances? How does this affect your reliance on Allah?

?
The argument points to the need for a free agent who chooses sizes. Reflect: Do you live as if every detail of your life is under Allah's free choice, or do you fall into thinking some things are just 'natural' and outside His control? What would change if you truly believed every difference is from Him?

Ninth Argument: Differences in Positions
Ninth argument from position differences within a sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differing positions of parts within a celestial sphere (depth vs. surface) indicate intentional design.

Core Idea
Parts equal in nature occupy different positions, requiring a chooser.

The ninth argument focuses on the variation in the positions of parts within a single sphere: some parts are deep inside, others on the surface. Since all parts share the same nature, this positional difference cannot be due to essence alone. A free agent must have assigned each part its specific location.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the ninth argument. Note the phrase 'اختلاف مواقع أجزائها' (difference of positions of its parts).
The argument applies to a single sphere, not just between spheres.
2
Understand the internal structure
The sphere has parts in its depth (عمق) and on its outer surface (سطح). These parts are identical in essence but occupy different places.
3
Consider the implication
If all parts are essentially identical, why is one inside and another outside? This cannot be explained by the parts themselves; an external agent must have chosen the arrangement.
This strengthens the case for a wise creator who determines positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثامن: فهو أن الأجرام الفلكية مختلفة في المقادير. لأن بعضها أعظم من بعض، مع استوائها في الذوات.

As for the eighth aspect: it is that the celestial bodies differ in magnitudes. Because some of them are greater than others, despite their equality in essences.

As for the ninth aspect: it is the difference of the positions of their parts. For a particular sphere, some of its parts are located in its depth and inside its thickness, and some are on its outer surface.

As for the tenth aspect: it is their difference in the manner of motions. Some are fast, some are slow. Rather we say: this meaning occurs in a single sphere. For when the sphere rotates, every point assumed on its surface also rotates. If you know this, we say: As for the two poles of the sphere, they are stationary. As for its equatorial region, it moves in the fastest way. And every point that is closer to the equator moves faster than a point that is farther from the equator. And since those points are infinite, it must be said that there is no limit to the ranks of motions occurring in a single sphere, due to speed and slowness. So the motion of the sphere of fixed stars, although very slow, yet a very small circle revolving around its pole completes its motion in a period of thirty-six thousand years, and the orbit of that circle is smaller than the orbit of the shell of a single Jāwarsa. And in a single sphere are assumed these different motions, due to slowness and speed, and those points and parts are all similar. So the specification of each of them with its particular motion and its particular measure of slowness and speed must be by the determination of the Mighty, the Knowing.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth, ninth, and tenth arguments. The eighth argument: celestial bodies differ in magnitude while being equal in essence, so a free agent is needed. The ninth argument: the positions of parts within a sphere differ (some in depth, some on surface), requiring a chooser. The tenth argument is lengthy and detailed: motions within a single sphere vary infinitely. The author explains that in a rotating sphere, points closer to the equator move faster, while poles are stationary. Since points are infinite, ranks of motion are infinite. Even the sphere of fixed stars has extremely slow motion, with a small circle taking 36,000 years. All parts are similar yet each has a specific speed, so this must be by the precise determination of the Almighty, All-Knowing.

Tenth Argument: Infinite Variations in Motion
Tenth argument from motion variations in a single sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite gradations of speed within a single rotating sphere point to a wise and precise determiner.

Core Idea
Infinite motion ranks in one sphere require an infinite intelligence to assign them.

The tenth and most detailed argument examines the variation in motions within a single sphere. The author shows that points on a rotating sphere have different speeds depending on their distance from the equator, and since points are infinite, the ranks of motion are infinite. Such precise and infinite specification cannot come from nature alone; it requires the determination of the All-Knowing, All-Powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage for the tenth argument carefully. It is longer and more detailed. Notice the progression from poles to equator to infinite points.
2
Understand the geometry
In a rotating sphere, the poles are stationary, the equator moves fastest, and points in between have intermediate speeds. The author points out that there are infinite points, hence infinite speeds.
The example of the fixed stars' slow motion illustrates the extreme.
3
Grasp the conclusion
All points and parts are similar in essence, yet each has a unique speed. This cannot be due to the parts themselves; it must be by the precise determination of the Mighty, the Knowing.
The phrase 'تقدير العزيز العليم' (determination of the Mighty, the Knowing) is the key conclusion.

وأما الوجه التاسع: فهو اختلاف مواقع أجزائها. فإن الفلك المعين وقع بعض أجزائه في عمقه، وداخل ثخنه، وبعضها في خارج سطحه.

Abraham's Argument: Life, Death, and the Causality Objection
Abraham's argument and the opponent's causality-based objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Abraham's argument from life and death and the opponent's objection based on whether God acts directly or through intermediaries.

Core Idea
Abraham's argument forces a choice: direct divine action or mediated causation, both problematic for the opponent.

This passage presents the second type of Qur'anic proof: Abraham's argument that God gives life and death. When the opponent claims to do the same, the author unpacks the hidden dilemma behind this objection—whether God's action is direct or through secondary causes. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion of causality and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the opponent's objection is framed as a question about medium.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the author's full response comes later.
2
Identify the dilemma
The opponent presents two possibilities: God acts directly or through intermediaries. Identify these two branches in the text.
Look for the words 'بدون واسطة' and 'بواسطة'.
3
Analyze the opponent's reasoning
Understand why the opponent thinks each branch is problematic: direct action is unprovable, mediated action makes humans also capable.
This is a classic theological challenge about secondary causality.
4
Prepare for the resolution
Consider how the author might respond. This objection is not the final word—the next part of the text will answer it.
You may think the opponent has a point, but kalam offers a response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من الدلائل القرآنية: أن إبراهيم عليه السلام استدل بالحياة والموت في أجساد الحيوانات فقال: (ربي الذي يحي ويميت). فقال السائل: (أنا أحبي وأميت). وتفسير هذا السؤال: كأنه قال: كيف تدعي صدور الإحياء والإمانة من الإله؟ أتدعي بأنه يفعل الإحياء والإماتة بدون واسطة الأفلاك والكواكب، والطبائع، أو بواسطة هذه الأشياء؟ فإن ادعيت الأول لم تجد إلى إثباته سبيلا، وإن ادعيت الثاني فكل واحد منا يقدر على الإحياء
The second type of Qur'anic proofs: that Abraham, peace be upon him, argued using life and death in the bodies of animals, saying: 'My Lord is He who gives life and causes death.' So the questioner said: 'I give life and cause death.' And the interpretation of this question: it is as if he said: How do you claim that giving life and causing death come from God? Do you claim that He does giving life and causing death without the mediation of the celestial spheres, planets, and natures, or through these things? If you claim the first, you will not find a way to prove it; and if you claim the second, then every one of us is able to give life...

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of Qur'anic proofs, citing Abraham's argument from the Qur'an (2:258). Abraham argued that his Lord gives life and death. The opponent (Nebuchadnezzar or Nimrod) claimed to do the same. The author explains the opponent's objection: he asks whether Abraham claims that God causes life and death directly without any intermediaries (celestial spheres, planets, natures) or through them. If Abraham claims direct action, he cannot prove it empirically; if through secondary causes, then humans can also do the same. The passage ends as the author begins to respond.

Ibrahim's Argument from Life and Death Refuting Human Agency
Ibrahim's Quranic argument refuting human agency in life and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Prophet Ibrahim refuted the skeptic's claim that humans can give life and death through intermediaries, and why God is the ultimate cause.

Core Idea
True causation belongs to God; human actions are only secondary through intermediaries.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from a skeptic who argues that if life and death occur through celestial and natural intermediaries, then humans can also cause them. Prophet Ibrahim's response demonstrates that humans lack control over the ultimate movers, thereby establishing God's exclusive authorship of life and death.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Pay attention to the skeptic's objection and Ibrahim's answer.
Notice that the skeptic uses an 'if-then' argument.
2
Identify the skeptic's challenge
The skeptic claims that if God causes life and death through intermediaries, then humans can also cause them through the same intermediaries. See how this challenges the proof of God's existence.
This is a common objection against divine causation.
3
Understand Ibrahim's first argument
Ibrahim argues that the celestial spheres cannot move by themselves because they are permanent and motion is change. Also, infinite regress of movers is impossible, so God is the ultimate mover.
This establishes God's unique control over the intermediaries.
4
Understand Ibrahim's second argument (sun example)
Ibrahim uses the example of the sun: God brings the sun from the east by moving it; humans cannot move the sun, so any effect via the sun is not truly theirs.
This clarifies the difference between God's agency and human agency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that life and death caused through intermediaries are truly God's acts, not human acts. The skeptic's objection fails.
This passage also illustrates the Quranic method of argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من الدلائل القرآنية: أن إبراهيم عليه السلام استدل بالحياة والموت في أجساد الحيوانات فقال: (ربي الذي يحيي ويميت). فقال السائل: (أنا أحيي وأميت). وتفسير هذا السؤال: كأنه قال: كيف تدعي صدور الإحياء والإماتة من الإله؟ أتدعي بأنه يفعل الإحياء والإماتة بدون واسطة الأفلاك والكواكب والطبائع، أو بواسطة هذه الأشياء؟ فإن ادعيت الأول لم تجد إلى إثباته سبيلا، وإن ادعيت الثاني فكل واحد منا يقدر على الإحياء والإماتة بواسطة حركات الأفلاك والكواكب والطبائع. ألا ترى أن الرجل يطأ زوجته فيتولد منه حيوان بعدد الطبائع، وقد يقتل الإنسان حيواناً آخر بالآلات والأدوات. فالأحياء والإماتة بهذا الطريق لا يدل على وجود الإله. فأجاب إبراهيم عليه السلام وقال: هب أن إله العالم يخلق الموت والحياة في هذا العالم، بواسطة تحريك الأفلاك وتمزيج الطبائع، إلا أن محرك الأفلاك والطبائع هو الله تعالى لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال: إنها متحركة لذواتها. لأن الذات باقية مصونة عن التغير. والحركة نفس التغير. والباقي لا يكون علة للمتغير. ولا يجوز أن يقال: إن محرك هذه الأفلاك: أفلاك أخرى. وإلا لزم التسلسل، فيثبت: أن محرك هذه الأفلاك هو الله تعالى. فإذا كان يحيي ويميت بواسطة حركات الأفلاك، كان الإحياء والإماتة من الله تعالى بخلاف الواحد منا، فإنا إذا أحيينا وأمتنا بواسطة الطبائع، لم تكن الحياة والموت منا. لأنا لا نقدر على تحريك الأفلاك. فظهر الفرق. وهذا الجواب هو الذي قاله إبراهيم حيث قال: «فإن الله يأتي بالشمس من المشرق، فأت بها من المغرب». والمراد كأنه قال: هب أنه تعالى أتى بها بواسطة حركات الشمس إلا أن محرك الشمس هو الله تعالى وأنت لا تقدر على تحريك الشمس. وكل فعل يفعله الله بواسطة حركة الشمس كان ذلك الأثر فعلاً له. وأما كل فعل تفعله أنت بواسطة حركة الشمس لم يكن ذلك الفعل فعلا لك البتة. فظهر الفرق. واعلم: أنا فسرنا هذه المناهدة على هذا الوجه، ليظهر منها وجه التحقيق في ذكر هذه الحجة.
The second type of Quranic evidence: that Abraham, peace be upon him, argued by life and death in the bodies of animals, saying: 'My Lord is the One who gives life and causes death.' The questioner said: 'I give life and cause death.' The interpretation of this question: it is as if he said: How do you claim that giving life and causing death proceed from God? Do you claim that He does giving life and causing death without the intermediary of the spheres, planets, and natures, or by means of these things? If you claim the first, you will find no way to prove it. And if you claim the second, then each one of us is able to give life and cause death by means of the motions of the spheres, planets, and natures. Do you not see that a man has intercourse with his wife, and from him an animal is generated according to the natures, and a man may kill another animal with tools and instruments. So giving life and causing death in this way does not indicate the existence of God. So Abraham, peace be upon him, answered and said: Suppose that the God of the world creates death and life in this world by means of moving the spheres and mixing the natures, yet the mover of the spheres and natures is God, may He be exalted, because it cannot be said that they are moving by themselves. For the essence remains preserved from change. Motion is change itself. The permanent cannot be the cause of the changing. And it is not permissible to say that the mover of these spheres is other spheres. Otherwise, infinite regress would follow. So it is established that the mover of these spheres is God, may He be exalted. So if He gives life and causes death by means of the motions of the spheres, then giving life and causing death are from God, may He be exalted, unlike one of us. For if we give life and cause death by means of the natures, life and death are not from us, because we are not able to move the spheres. So the difference became clear. And this answer is what Abraham said where he said: 'Indeed God brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west.' The meaning is as if he said: Suppose that He, exalted, brought it by means of the motions of the sun, yet the mover of the sun is God, exalted, and you are not able to move the sun. And every action that God does by means of the motion of the sun, that effect is His action. As for every action that you do by means of the motion of the sun, that action is not your action at all. So the difference became clear. Know that we have interpreted this discussion in this manner, so that the aspect of verification in mentioning this argument may appear from it.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer passage from al-Razi (likely) presenting and refuting a skeptic's challenge to Ibrahim's argument. The skeptic claims that if life and death are caused through celestial and natural intermediaries, then humans can also cause life and death through those same intermediaries, so God's existence is not proven. Ibrahim's answer has two parts. First, he argues that even if God uses intermediaries, He is the ultimate mover of those intermediaries, because celestial spheres cannot move by themselves (since they are permanent and motion is change, a permanent thing cannot be the cause of change), and an infinite regress of movers is impossible. Thus God is the ultimate mover. Second, he gives the example of the sun: if God brings the sun from the east, He does so by moving the sun; humans cannot move the sun, so their actions via the sun are not truly theirs. The difference is that God has direct control over the celestial spheres, while humans do not. Therefore, the life and death caused through intermediaries are truly God's actions, not human actions. The author concludes by explaining that he has clarified this argument so that the true verification becomes apparent.

Key Terms
الإحياء
الإماتة
الطبائع
الأفلاك
التسلسل
محرك الأفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can independently cause life and death through natural intermediaries, thereby sharing divine power.
Correction: Humans only appear to cause life and death via intermediaries, but they do not control the ultimate celestial movers; only God does, so the effect is truly God's.

Checkpoint
How does Ibrahim refute the claim that humans give life and death through celestial intermediaries?
A) By saying that humans cannot move the celestial spheres, so the effect is truly God's.
B) By denying that celestial spheres exist.
C) By asserting that life and death are illusions.
D) By claiming humans can never cause any effect.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Ibrahim establishes that God is the sole giver of life and death by demonstrating that humans lack control over the celestial intermediaries, thus refuting any claim of independent human causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a significant success in your life, do you ever pause to realize that the very ability to act came from God, and that you didn't control the ultimate causes? If you truly believed this, how would your gratitude—or your pride—change?
?
Ibrahim's argument shows that without control over the celestial spheres, your actions are not truly yours. Are there moments in your day where you act as though you are the independent cause of your deeds? What would it look like to consciously attribute every small act—breathing, thinking, moving—to God's power?
?
The skeptic's objection sounds familiar: 'I can cause life and death through medicine, technology, my choices.' How does this argument dismantle that arrogance? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that you are merely an instrument, not the source?

Ibrahim's Refutation of Nimrod: Divine Agency Behind Natural Causes
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains Ibrahim's argument that life and death are from God via celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibrahim (AS) refuted Nimrod's argument that natural causation of life and death disproves God's existence, by demonstrating that God is the ultimate mover of the celestial spheres.

Core Idea
The mover behind the motion is God alone.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi unpacks the famous debate between Prophet Ibrahim and Nimrod. Nimrod claimed that since life and death occur through natural processes, there is no need for God. Ibrahim's response is a sophisticated causal argument: while God uses the spheres as instruments, He alone is their mover, and humans cannot claim agency over effects they do not control. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow: objection, then Ibrahim's response with a detailed causal argument.
This passage is from al-Razi's commentary on the Quranic verse.
2
Understand Nimrod's objection
Nimrod argued that life and death occur through natural means (e.g., reproduction, killing) so they don't prove God's existence. Identify this in the text.
This is a common challenge: if we can explain events naturally, why invoke God?
3
Follow Ibrahim's reply
Ibrahim concedes that God uses the spheres and natures as instruments, but argues that the spheres themselves cannot move without a mover. Pay attention to the proof: the sphere's essence is permanent, motion is change, so the sphere cannot be the cause of its own motion. Also, no infinite regress of movers.
4
Recognize the key difference
The crucial point: humans cannot move the spheres, so effects produced through natural means are not attributable to us, but to God who moves the spheres. This is why Ibrahim's argument succeeds.
This refutes the idea that natural causation excludes divine action.
5
Reflect on the Quranic verse
Ibrahim's saying 'God brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' is interpreted similarly: the sun's motion is from God, so its effects are God's actions. Connect this to the main argument.
This solidifies the point that God is the ultimate agent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإماتة بواسطة حركات الأفلاك والكواكب والطبائع. ألا ترى أن الرجل يطأ زوجته فيتولد منه حيوان بعدد الطبائع، وقد يقتل الإنسان حيواناً آخر بالآلات والأدوات. فالأحياء والإماتة بهذا الطريق لا يدل على وجود الإله. فأجاب إبراهيم عليه السلام وقال: هب أن إله العالم يخلق الموت والحياة في هذا العالم، بواسطة تحريك الأفلاك وتمزيج الطبائع، إلا أن محرك الأفلاك والطبائع هو الله [تعالى] لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال: إنها متحركة لذواتها. لأن الذات باقية مصونة عن التغير. والحركة نفس التغير. والباقي لا يكون علة للمتغير. ولا يجوز أن يقال: إن محرك هذه الأفلاك: أفلاك أخرى. وإلا لزم التسلسل، فيثبت: أن محرك هذه الأفلاك هو الله تعالى. فإذا كان يحيي ويميت بواسطة حركات الأفلاك، كان الإحياء والإماتة من الله [تعالى] بخلاف الواحد منا، فإنا إذا أحببنا وأمتنا بواسطة الطبائع، لم تكن الحياة والموت منا. لأنا لا نقدر على تحريك الأفلاك. فظهر الفرق. وهذا الجواب هو الذي قاله إبراهيم حيث قال: «فإن الله يأتي بالشمس من المشرق، فأت بها من المغرب». والمراد كأنه قال: هب أنه تعالى أتى بها بواسطة حركات الشمس إلا أن محرك الشمس هو الله [تعالى] وأنت لا تقدر على تحريك الشمس. وكل فعل يفعله الله بواسطة حركة الشمس [كان ذلك الأثر فعلاً له. وأما كل فعل تفعله أنت بواسطة حركة الشمس] لم يكن ذلك الفعل فعلاً لك البتة. فظهر الفرق. واعلم: أنا فسرنا هذه المناهدة على هذا الوجه، ليظهر منها وجه التحقيق في ذكر هذه الحجة.
And causing death (الإماتة) by means of the motions of the spheres (الأفلاك), planets (الكواكب), and natures (الطبائع). Do you not see that a man treads his wife and from him an animal is produced according to the natures, and a man may kill another animal with instruments and tools. So giving life (الإحياء) and causing death (الإماتة) in this manner does not indicate the existence of God. So Abraham, peace be upon him, replied and said: Suppose that the God of the world creates death and life in this world, by means of setting the spheres in motion and mixing the natures, except that the mover of the spheres and natures is God [exalted] because it cannot be said that they are moving by themselves. For the essence (الذات) [of the sphere] is permanent, preserved from change, and motion is itself change, and the permanent cannot be the cause (علة) of the changing. Nor may it be said that the mover of these spheres is other spheres, for then there would be an infinite regress (التسلسل). So it is established that the mover of these spheres is God exalted. Thus if He gives life and causes death by means of the motions of the spheres, then giving life and causing death are from God [exalted] in contrast to one of us, for if we give life and cause death by means of the natures, life and death are not from us, for we are not able to set the spheres in motion. So the difference became manifest. And this answer is that which Abraham said when he said: 'Indeed God brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west.' The meaning is as if he said: Suppose that He brought it by means of the motions of the sun, except that the mover of the sun is God [exalted] and you cannot move the sun. And every action that God does by means of the motion of the sun [then that effect is His action; and as for every action that you do by means of the motion of the sun] that action is not your action at all. So the difference became manifest. Know that we have interpreted this debate (المناهدة) in this way so that the aspect of verification in mentioning this argument may appear from it.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument from Ibrahim (Abraham) against Nimrod, as a refutation of the claim that natural causation of life and death through celestial motions disproves divine agency. The passage begins by stating the objection: life and death are produced through natural means (like human reproduction and killing) and thus do not indicate God. Then Ibrahim responds by conceding that God creates life and death via the motions of spheres and natures, but argues that the mover of these spheres is God, not the spheres themselves, because they cannot move by themselves (their essence is permanent, motion is change, the permanent cannot cause change). Also, there cannot be an infinite regress of movers. Therefore, God is the true agent. The difference from humans is that humans cannot move the spheres, so when we produce life or death through natural means, the effect is not attributed to us but to God as the ultimate mover. Then the passage cites the Quranic verse (2:258) where Ibrahim says 'God brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' as a parallel argument: God is the mover of the sun, so He is the agent of what the sun does; you cannot move the sun, so your actions via the sun are not really yours. The author notes that he has interpreted this debate to show the verification of this argument.

Key Terms
أفلاك (spheres)
طبائع (natures)
إحياء (giving life)
إماتة (causing death)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
علة (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If life and death occur through natural causes, then God is not involved.
Correction: Ibrahim's argument shows that natural causes are instruments of God; He is the ultimate mover, so He is involved in every event.

Checkpoint
According to Ibrahim's argument, why is the effect produced through celestial motions attributed to God and not to humans?
A) Because humans cannot understand celestial motions
B) Because humans lack the power to move the spheres
C) Because the spheres are themselves divine
D) Because the motions are eternal

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's refutation shows that natural causation does not exclude divine agency; rather, God is the ultimate cause behind all secondary causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you credit yourself for a success or blame natural causes for a failure, do you truly recognize that God is the ultimate mover behind every cause? How would this change your attitude of gratitude and accountability?
?
Ibrahim's argument shows that even the sun's rising is an act of God. How often do you think of the daily sunrise as a direct act of God rather than a natural occurrence? What would it mean for your worship if you saw each sunrise as a personal gift from Him?
?
If you cannot move the sun or spheres, then your actions that depend on these motions (like life and death) are not truly yours. Does this humble your sense of control over your own life? In what area do you need to surrender the illusion that you are the ultimate agent?

The Argument from Celestial Motions: Breaking Infinite Regress
First argument: celestial motions lead to a free Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the movement of planets and spheres leads to the conclusion of a free Creator by refuting infinite regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; motion ends in an Unmoved Mover.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by considering the movements of celestial bodies. If each movement required another sphere, we would have an infinite chain—which reason rejects. Thus, the movements must originate from a Creator who Himself does not move. This argument is grounded in Quran 16:12, which ends with 'for a people who reason.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: observation → dilemma → rejection of infinite regress → conclusion.
Pay attention to the key term 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Understand the problem of infinite regress
Consider why infinite regress is rationally impossible. If every movement needs another cause, the chain never ends; no explanation is complete. The only way to stop the regress is a first cause that does not move.
This is a classic argument in Islamic theology and philosophy.
3
See how Razi uses the Quranic verse
Razi quotes Quran 16:12 to show that the conclusion is not merely philosophical but aligns with divine revelation. The verse calls on rational people to reflect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكواكب والأفلاك. فتلك الحركات كيف حصلت ؟ فإن كان حصولها بسبب أفلاك أخرى لزم التسلسل ، وإن كان من الخالق الحكيم ، فذلك يوجب الإقرار بوجود الإله تعالى . وهذا هو المراد بقوله تعالى : ( وسخر لكم الليل والنهار والشمس والقمر والنجوم : مسخرات بأمره . إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون ) فجعل مقطع هذه الآية قوله ( لقوم يعقلون ) والتقدير : كأنه قيل : إن كنت عاقلاً فاعلم أن التسلسل باطل . فوجب انتهاء الحركات إلى حركة يكون موجدها غير متحرك . وذلك هو الإله القادر المختار . ولما تم الدليل في هذا المقام ، لا جرم جعل مقطعه قوله : ( إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون ) والوجه الثاني من الجواب : هو أن نسبة الكواكب والطبائع إلى جميع أجزاء الورقة الواحدة ، والحبة الواحدة : واحدة . ثم إنا نرى الورقة الواحدة من الورد: أحد وجهيها في غاية الحمرة، والوجه الثاني في غاية السواد . فلو كان المؤثر موجباً بالذات لامتنع حصول هذا التفاوت ، لأنه تقرر في العقول : أن تأثير الموجب بالذات لا يختلف . وحيث حصل التفاوت في الآثار ، أن المؤثر قادر مختار . وهذا هو المراد من قوله : ( وماذراً لكم في الأرض مختلفاً الوانه . إن في ذلك لآية لقوم يذكرون ) كأنه قيل له : تذكر ما ترسخ في عقلك . أن الموجب بالذات والطبع لا يختلف تأثيره. فلما رأيت بعينك حصول هذا الاختلاف ، فاعرف أن المؤثر فيه ليس هو الطبائع الموجبة بالذات ، بل المؤثر فيه هو الفاعل المختار . فلهذا السبب جعل مقطع هذه الآية : قوله : ( لقوم يذكرون ) ولنكتف من هذا الجنس بهذا القدر ، فإنه بحر واسع . وقد ذكرناه على سبيل الاستقصاء في ( التفسير الكبير فمن أراد هذا النوع فعليه بذلك الكتاب . والله أعلم بالصواب
The planets and the spheres. So those movements, how did they occur? If their occurrence was due to other spheres, then infinite regress (tasalsul) follows; and if it was from the Wise Creator, then that necessitates acknowledgment of the existence of God, exalted is He. And this is what is meant by His statement, exalted: 'And He subjected for you the night and day, the sun and the moon, and the stars are subjected by His command. Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason' (Quran 16:12). So He made the conclusion of this verse His statement 'for a people who reason.' And the implication is: it is as if it were said: If you are rational, then know that infinite regress is invalid. Thus it is necessary that the movements terminate at a movement whose originator is not moving. And that is the powerful, free-choosing God (al-ilah al-qadir al-mukhtar). And since the proof was completed in this context, indeed He made its conclusion His statement: 'Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason.' The second aspect of the answer: the relationship of the planets and natural dispositions (taba'i') to all parts of a single leaf and a single grain is one and the same. Then we see the single leaf of a rose: one of its two sides is extremely red, and the other side is extremely black. So if the effecter were a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat), this variation would be impossible, because it is established in minds that the effect of a necessary agent (al-mujib bi al-dhat) does not vary. And whenever variation occurs in effects, the effecter is a powerful, free-choosing agent (qadir mukhtar). And this is what is meant by His statement: 'And whatever He has created for you on the earth of varying colors. Indeed in that is a sign for a people who remember' (Quran 16:13). It is as if it were said to him: Remember what is firmly established in your mind: that the necessary agent (al-mujib bi al-dhat) and nature (al-tab') do not have their effect vary. So when you saw with your own eye the occurrence of this variation, then know that the effecter in it is not the necessary natures (al-taba'i' al-mujiba bi al-dhat), but rather the effecter in it is the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar). For this reason He made the conclusion of this verse His statement: 'for a people who remember.' And we will suffice with this amount from this type, for it is a vast sea. And we have mentioned it in an exhaustive manner in 'al-Tafsir al-Kabir,' so whoever desires this type should refer to that book. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents two complementary arguments (wajhān) for the existence of a free Creator, drawn from the nature of celestial movements and from observable variation in the natural world. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, is refuting the view that natural phenomena are caused by necessary, blind forces (al-mūjib bi al-dhāt) and instead proving they originate from a powerful, free-choosing agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār).

First argument: The movements of planets and spheres. If these movements were caused by other spheres, an infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow—which is rationally impossible. Therefore, they must ultimately be caused by a creator who is Himself not subject to motion—that is, God. Razi connects this to Quran 16:12, which ends with "for a people who reason," implying that rational reflection leads to the conclusion that infinite regress is false and the chain of motion ends in an unmoved mover, who is the powerful, free-choosing God.

Second argument: Consider a single rose leaf: one side is intensely red, the other intensely black. The influence of the planets and natural dispositions is uniform across all parts of a single leaf. Yet we see variation. If the cause were a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt), its effect would be uniform, because a necessary cause acts without choice and produces the same result every time. Since variation occurs, the cause must be a free agent who chooses to produce different effects. Razi quotes Quran 16:13, which ends with "for a people who remember," urging the reader to recall the established rational principle that a necessary nature does not vary, and then recognize that the observed variation points to a free agent.

Finally, Razi notes that this topic is vast and refers the reader to his own Tafsir al-Kabir for a comprehensive treatment.

This is a classic kalam argument combining metaphysical reasoning (refutation of infinite regress) and empirical observation (variation in nature) to demonstrate a Creator who acts by choice, not by necessity.

Key Terms
تسلسل
فاعل مختار
قادر مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's first argument, what would happen if the movements of celestial bodies were caused by other spheres?
A) They would be eternal.
B) They would be caused by a free agent.
C) An infinite regress would result.
D) They would cease immediately.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress in celestial motions points to a free, unmoved Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument says infinite regress is invalid. Think of one area in your life where you act as if causes go back forever, avoiding acknowledgment of a First Cause. What specific step will you take today to recognize the Creator's direct involvement in that matter?
?
The verse ends with 'for a people who reason.' When you contemplate the order of the universe—sun, moon, day and night—do you truly pause to see them as signs pointing to the Unmoved Mover, or do you take them for granted? Commit to one intentional act of reflection today.
?
The Creator is described as 'free-choosing'. In what area of your life do you struggle to submit to His absolute freedom over causes? How can you actively surrender your own planning to acknowledge His free choice?
