The Argument from Variation: Free Agency in Nature
Second argument: natural variation refutes necessary agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed variation in a single rose leaf proves that the cause is a free agent rather than a necessary nature.

Core Idea
Uniform cause yields uniform effect; variation demands a free chooser.

Al-Razi presents a second argument, this time from the natural world. He points to a single rose leaf with two different colors—one side red, one side black. Since the natural influences are uniform, the difference cannot come from a necessary cause; it must come from a free agent who chooses to create diversity. This is supported by Quran 16:13, which ends with 'for a people who remember.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Focus on the example of the rose leaf and the logical inference.
Key term: 'mujib bi al-dhat' (necessary agent) and 'fa'il mukhtar' (free agent).
2
Understand the principle of uniform causation
The passage states that a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat) produces uniform effects. This is a rational principle: if the cause is the same and acts by necessity, the effect cannot vary.
Contrast with a free agent who can choose different effects.
3
Apply to the rose leaf example
The rose leaf has two distinct colors despite uniform natural influences. Since variation is observed, the cause cannot be a necessary agent; it must be a free agent.
This argument uses empirical observation combined with rational principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You claim to believe in Allah, but do you sometimes attribute the diversity in creation—different plants, animals, human appearances—to impersonal forces like evolution or chance, as if Allah was not directly involved? How does this lesson expose that subtle negligence?
?
The verse ends 'for a people who remember.' Do you truly remember this rational principle every time you see variation? Will you make a conscious effort today to pause at the next leaf you see and say, 'This variation is from the Free Agent'?

The First Principle and the Emanation Scheme
Razi enumerates two views and the philosophers' consensus on emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' emanation scheme, where the First Principle directly causes only one intellect, which then causes multiplicity.

Core Idea
From the One only one proceeds — the first cause produces a single effect.

Razi presents the first point of research: does the First Principle (God) directly cause all contingent things, or only one? The philosophers unanimously reject direct causation and instead propose an emanation hierarchy. This lesson examines their reasoning and the initial steps of that hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first point carefully. Notice the two options presented: direct causality or indirect through a single intermediary.
2
Identify the philosophers' consensus
The author states that no philosopher holds the direct view. They all agree that 'from the One only one proceeds.' Understand this principle.
3
Trace the emanation chain
God causes the First Intellect. That intellect then causes three things: the Second Intellect, the Soul, and the Sphere. Note this branching.
4
Reflect on the philosophical implications
Consider why philosophers adopted this scheme. It attempts to preserve divine simplicity while explaining multiplicity.
This connects to the broader issue of God's relationship to the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا موضعان للبحث : الموضع الأول : أن يقال : المبدأ الأول. هل يعقل أن يكون مبدأ الجميع الممكنات ، أو يقال : إنه مبدأ لشيء واحد ، ثم ذلك الشيء الواحد يكون مبدأ للكثرة ؟ . أما القول الأول : وهو أن المبدأ الأول لجميع الممكنات ابتداء . فما رأيت قوماً من الفلاسفة قالوا به. بل اتفقوا على القول الثاني ، وهو أنه تعالى واحد والواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، فهر تعالى علة للعقل الأول . ثم إن العقل الأول علة لثلاثة أشياء : العقل الثاني ، والنفس ، والفلك . وسيأتي تفصيل قولهم في هذا الباب . والموضع الثاني من البحث : هو أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن واجب الوجود يؤثر في وجود الأجسام، تأثير على سبيل الإيجاب الذاتي ، ثم إنه يركبها على الصفات المختلفة بحسب اختيارها ؟ فعل هذا التقدير : إنه موجب بالنسبة إلى الذوات ، ومختار بالنسبة إلى الصفات
Then here are two points for research: The first point: that it be said: The First Principle—is it conceivable that it be the principle of all contingent things directly, or is it said that it is the principle of one thing, then that one thing becomes the principle of multiplicity? As for the first saying, that the First Principle is the principle of all contingent things directly, I have not seen any group of philosophers who said it. Rather, they all agreed upon the second saying, that He, exalted, is one, and from the one only one proceeds; so He, exalted, is the cause of the First Intellect. Then the First Intellect is the cause of three things: the Second Intellect, the Soul, and the Sphere. And the detail of their saying in this chapter will come. And the second point of research: that it be said: Why is it not allowable to say that the Necessary Existent influences the existence of bodies by way of essential necessitation, and then composes them with various attributes according to His choice? So on this supposition, He is a necessitator with respect to essences, and a chooser with respect to attributes.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage lists two distinct points of research that arise from the previous discussion of the philosophers' position. 

**First point:** The question is whether the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) directly produces all contingent things or only one thing, which then produces multiplicity. The author notes that he has not seen any philosopher who holds the direct production view. Instead, all philosophers agree on the principle 'from the One only one proceeds' (al-wahid la yasdiru 'anhu illa al-wahid). Thus, God (exalted) is the cause of the First Intellect alone. That First Intellect then causes three things: the Second Intellect, the Soul, and the (outermost) Sphere. The details of this emanation scheme are promised later. 

**Second point:** The issue here is why one cannot combine essential necessitation and choice. That is, the Necessary Existent could cause the existence of bodies by necessity (essential necessitation) but then arrange or compose them with different attributes (qualities, forms) by choice. Under this view, God would be a necessitator (mujib) regarding essences (the very existence of bodies) but a chooser (mukhtar) regarding their attributes. This is a potential compromise between the necessitarian philosophers and the theologians who affirm free will in God. The author does not yet resolve it; it is presented as a topic for investigation.

Key Terms
مبدأ أول (First Principle)
واحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد (from the One only one proceeds)
عقل (Intellect)
نفس (Soul)
فلك (Sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle 'from the One only one proceeds' implies that God can only create one type of thing or is limited.
Correction: The principle refers to direct causality: God directly creates only one thing (the First Intellect), which then creates multiplicity. It does not limit God's power, but explains how multiplicity arises from a simple First Cause.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what is the first thing that proceeds from God?
A) The material world directly
B) The First Intellect
C) The Soul
D) The Sphere

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that God directly creates only the First Intellect; all other existence comes through that intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers agreed that from the One only one proceeds, implying a necessary order in creation. How does your own desire for instant answers or immediate results clash with accepting a chain of causes that ultimately traces back to Allah? Will you today trace one blessing back through its intermediate causes and praise Allah directly as the ultimate source?
?
If Allah's unity is so profound that His direct creation is limited to a single entity, does that challenge your assumptions about how He interacts with the world? Do you truly live as though every secondary cause is dependent on His will, or do you sometimes attribute power to created causes independently?
?
The passage shows a philosophical attempt to explain multiplicity from unity. In your own spiritual life, you experience fragmentation—multiple distractions, sins, worries. How can you recenter on the One, letting His oneness bring coherence to your scattered heart? What practical step will you take today to reduce the 'multiplicity' of worldly concerns and focus on the singular purpose of worship?

Combining Necessitation and Choice in Divine Action
Razi presents a proposal to combine essential necessitation and choice in divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second point of research: the proposal that God can be a necessitator for essences and a chooser for attributes.

Core Idea
God can be both a necessitator and a chooser — essential action for existence, free will for qualities.

The second point of research raises a possible synthesis between the philosophers' view of necessary emanation and the theologians' emphasis on divine choice. Razi asks: why can't we say that God causes the existence of bodies by essential necessitation, but then freely composes them with their attributes? This lesson examines that proposal.

Guided Path
1
Read the second point
Read the Arabic passage for the second point. Notice the question: why is it not allowable to say that God causes bodies by necessity but composes them by choice?
2
Identify the two components
The proposal divides God's action into two aspects: necessitation regarding essences, and choice regarding attributes.
3
Understand the supposition
The author presents this as a possible view ('on this supposition') without immediately endorsing or rejecting it.
This sets the stage for further discussion.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this reconciles the philosophers' determinism with the theologians' free will.

The Third Division: Affirming a God Who Acts by Choice
Razi defines the third division: a voluntary God with moral attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division of views on the efficient cause of the world, which affirms a God who acts by choice and is characterized by justice, care, and mercy.

Core Idea
God acts by choice, not necessity, and always for the good of creation.

Razi has been classifying different views on the cause of the world. After discussing those who deny a cause and those who affirm a necessary cause, he now presents the view of the majority of Muslim theologians: that God is a voluntary agent who creates with wisdom and care. This passage defines that position and its essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how it first states the view, then lists the attributes that follow from it.
The passage ends abruptly at a comma; it likely continues to contrast with other views.
2
Identify the core affirmation
What does the third division affirm? Focus on 'a God who acts by choice' and 'He does nothing except what agrees with the interests of creation'.
This directly opposes the idea of a necessary or indifferent cause.
3
List the attributes
The passage states that this God must be just, caring for His servants, and merciful. Ask yourself why these follow from acting by choice.
If God acts by choice, His actions are not forced; they reflect His wisdom and goodness.
4
Contrast with previous divisions
Consider how this view differs from denying a cause (first division) or affirming a necessary cause (second division). This prepares you for the broader argument.
The attributes of justice and mercy are unique to this division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يُقر بإثبات [ إله (۲) ] فاعل بالاختيار لهذا العالم ، ويُعترف بأنه لا يفعل إلا ما يوافق مصالح الخلق . وهؤلاء هم الذين أطبقوا على أنه إله العالم، يجب أن يكون عادلاً ناظراً لعباده ، رحيماً بهم ،
As for the third division: it is that one affirms [a god (2)] who acts by choice for this world, and acknowledges that He does nothing except what agrees with the interests of creation. And these are those who agreed that He is the God of the world, that He must be just, caring for His servants, merciful to them,
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage introduces the third and final division of views concerning the efficient cause of the world. Unlike the previous two divisions (those denying any cause, and those affirming a necessary cause that acts by essence), this third division affirms a God who acts by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar). Key characteristics of this view are: 

1. There is a God who is the creator of the world. 
2. This God acts freely, by choice, not by necessity. 
3. His actions are always in accordance with the interests (masalih) of His creation—He does what benefits them. 
4. Consequently, this God must be just ('adil), caring for His servants (naziran li 'ibadihi), and merciful (rahim) to them. 

This position is clearly the view of the mutakallimun (theologians) and the majority of Muslims. The passage ends abruptly, but it sets up the contrast between the necessitarian philosophers and the free-will-affirming theists.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار (agent by choice)
مصالح الخلق (interests of creation)
عادل (just)
ناظر لعباده (caring for His servants)
رحيم (merciful)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'acting by choice' implies arbitrariness, as if God could do good or evil whimsically.
Correction: The passage links choice to justice and mercy, showing God's actions are always wise and beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the third division, what kind of being is the God of the world?
A) A necessary cause by essence
B) A being who acts by choice and is just
C) A being who does not care for creation
D) A force that operates by necessity

Key Takeaway
The third division affirms a God who creates by choice and whose actions are always just, caring, and merciful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you truly believe that God's choice in that matter aligns with your best interests, as this passage teaches? If not, what would it take for you to trust His justice and mercy more than your own understanding?
?
This passage says God acts only for the welfare of creation. Have you ever resented God's decree or accused Him of being unfair? How does this text challenge that resentment, and what step will you take to cultivate gratitude instead?
?
Since God's attributes of justice, care, and mercy are directly linked to His voluntary action for your good, how should this affect the way you turn to Him in prayer? Will you now ask Him to increase your reliance on His wise choice rather than your own?

The Philosophers' View on Contingency and Necessitation
Presents the philosophical argument for a necessitating agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the world is contingent and requires a necessitating agent, not a choosing one.

Core Idea
The world is possible in itself but necessary through its cause.

Before refuting the philosophers, the author presents their position. They argue that every body is contingent, requiring an agent that is not a body and acts by essential necessitation rather than choice. This sets up the subsequent investigations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the philosophers' position
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the repeated emphasis on contingency.
2
Identify the three premises
Find the premises: bodies are contingent, contingent needs an agent, and the agent cannot be a body and acts by necessitation, not choice.
The rejection of choice is based on earlier doubts.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the agent is 'necessitating by essence' and the world is necessary through its cause.
This is attributed to Aristotle and his followers.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this view contrasts with the Ash'ari emphasis on divine choice and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهؤلاء هم الذين قالوا : هذا العالم جسم، وكل جسم فإنه ممكن لذاته ، بحسب ذاته ، وبحسب جميع أجزاء ماهيته. وكل ممكن فلا بد له مؤثر . والمؤثر في جميع الأجسام ، يمتنع أن يكون جسماً ، ثم قالوا : المؤثر على سبيل الاختيار : غير معقول، بناء على الشبهات التي ذكرناها . فثبت : أن ذلك المؤثر موجب بالذات ، فهذا العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود بوجوب سببه وعلته . وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة ، الذين هم أتباع أرسطاطاليس) فإن هذا القول هو الذي اختاره هذا الرجل، ونصره أصحابه وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين ثم ههنا موضعان للبحث :
الموضع الأول : أن يقال : المبدأ الأول. هل يعقل أن يكون مبدأ الجميع الممكنات ، أو يقال : إنه مبدأ لشيء واحد ، ثم ذلك الشيء الواحد يكون مبدأ للكثرة ؟ . أما القول الأول : وهو أن المبدأ الأول لجميع الممكنات ابتداء . فما رأيت قوماً من الفلاسفة قالوا به. بل اتفقوا على القول الثاني ، وهو أنه تعالى واحد والواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، فهر تعالى علة للعقل الأول . ثم إن العقل الأول علة لثلاثة أشياء : العقل الثاني ، والنفس ، والفلك . وسيأتي تفصيل قولهم في هذا الباب
·
والموضع الثاني من البحث : هو أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن واجب الوجود يؤثر في وجود الأجسام، تأثير على سبيل الإيجاب الذاتي ، ثم إنه يركبها على الصفات المختلفة بحسب اختيارها ؟ فعل هذا التقدير : إنه موجب بالنسبة إلى الذوات ، ومختار بالنسبة إلى الصفات
These are the ones who said: this world is a body, and every body is possible in its essence, in respect of its essence, and in respect of all parts of its quiddity. And every possible must have an agent. And the agent in all bodies cannot be a body. Then they said: the agent by way of choice is unintelligible, based on the doubts we mentioned. So it is established that that agent is necessitating by essence. So this world is possible in existence in itself, necessary in existence due to the necessity of its cause and its efficient cause. And this is the doctrine of the majority of philosophers, who are followers of Aristotle. For this statement is what this man chose and his companions and followers from the earlier and later [philosophers] supported. Then here there are two points for investigation:
The first point: is it conceivable that the first principle is the principle of all possibles, or is it said that it is a principle for one thing, and then that one thing becomes a principle for multiplicity? As for the first statement—that the first principle is for all possibles from the beginning—I have not seen any group of philosophers who said it. Rather, they agreed on the second statement, which is that He is one, and from the one only one proceeds. So He is the cause of the first intellect. Then the first intellect is the cause of three things: the second intellect, the soul, and the sphere. And the detail of their statement about this topic will come later.
The second point of investigation: why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary Being influences the existence of bodies by way of essential necessitation, and then He compounds them with different attributes according to their choice? On this assumption, He is necessitating with respect to essences, and choosing with respect to attributes.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical position and then raises two investigations. It begins by stating that the philosophers hold the world to be a body, and every body is contingent (possible) in its essence and in all its parts. Therefore, every contingent requires an agent. That agent cannot be a body, and moreover, the philosophers deny that the agent acts by choice, deeming it unintelligible based on earlier doubts. Hence, the agent is considered 'necessitating by essence' (mūjib bi-l-dhāt), meaning the world is contingent in itself but necessary through its cause. This is attributed to the majority of philosophers following Aristotle.

The author then notes two points for investigation. The first point asks: is the First Principle the direct cause of all possibles, or only of one thing which then causes multiplicity? The author says he has not seen any philosopher claim the former; they unanimously hold the latter. Their principle is 'from the one only one proceeds' — so the First Intellect emanates from God, and from that intellect come three: a second intellect, a soul, and a sphere. The second point asks: why can’t the Necessary Being cause bodies by essential necessitation but then combine them with differing attributes by choice? The suggested compromise is that He is necessitating with respect to essences but choosing with respect to attributes.

Key Terms
ممكن
واجب الوجود
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view that the agent acts by necessitation is sometimes mistaken for atheism or denial of a creator.
Correction: The philosophers affirm a First Principle as the cause of all existence, but they describe its causality as necessary emanation rather than deliberative choice.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, why is the agent not acting by choice?
A) Because choice is impossible for any being.
B) Because choice is unintelligible based on earlier doubts.
C) Because the agent is a body and bodies cannot choose.
D) Because choice would make the agent contingent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that the world is possible in itself but necessary through a First Principle that acts by essential necessitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you find a God who must create more distant or more intimate? How does the Islamic view of a free, choosing Creator change the way you relate to Him?
?
The passage claims that an agent acting by choice is 'unintelligible' to the philosophers. Have you ever struggled to reconcile divine omnipotence with human free will? In what ways does the concept of a God who freely chooses to create and command resonate with your own experience of being a responsible moral agent?
?
The philosophers say the world is necessary through its cause, not in itself. Does this idea challenge your sense of the world's contingency and dependency on God? How does it affect your certainty that the world could have been otherwise if God willed it? Are you willing to submit to a purely rational proof for God's nature, or do you find it lacking without revelation?

Two Investigations on the First Principle's Causality
Raises two investigations about the First Principle's causal role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two questions raised about how the First Principle relates to multiplicity and whether it can combine necessitation with choice.

Core Idea
Does the One directly cause all multiplicity, or only one, and can He necessitate essences but choose attributes?

After presenting the philosophers' view, the author introduces two points for investigation. The first concerns the emanation of multiplicity from the One, and the second explores whether the Necessary Being can be both necessitating and choosing.

Guided Path
1
Read the two points of investigation
Read the passage describing the two points. First, the question about whether the First Principle directly causes all possibles or only one. Second, whether necessity and choice can be combined.
The philosophers agree on the second option in the first point.
2
Understand the first investigation
The first point: either the First Principle is the direct cause of all possibles, or it causes one thing (the first intellect) which then causes multiplicity. The philosophers unanimously hold the latter.
Their principle: 'from the one only one proceeds.'
3
Understand the second investigation
The second point: why can't the Necessary Being cause bodies by essential necessitation but then combine them with different attributes by choice? This suggests a possible compromise.
On this view, He is necessitating for essences and choosing for attributes.
4
Consider the implications
Note that the second investigation is presented as an open question; the author does not settle it here.

فهؤلاء هم الذين قالوا : هذا العالم جسم، وكل جسم فإنه ممكن لذاته ، بحسب ذاته ، وبحسب جميع أجزاء ماهيته. وكل ممكن فلا بد له مؤثر . والمؤثر في جميع الأجسام ، يمتنع أن يكون جسماً ، ثم قالوا : المؤثر على سبيل الاختيار : غير معقول، بناء على الشبهات التي ذكرناها . فثبت : أن ذلك المؤثر موجب بالذات ، فهذا العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود بوجوب سببه وعلته . وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة ، الذين هم أتباع أرسطاطاليس) فإن هذا القول هو الذي اختاره هذا الرجل، ونصره أصحابه وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين ثم ههنا موضعان للبحث :
الموضع الأول : أن يقال : المبدأ الأول. هل يعقل أن يكون مبدأ الجميع الممكنات ، أو يقال : إنه مبدأ لشيء واحد ، ثم ذلك الشيء الواحد يكون مبدأ للكثرة ؟ . أما القول الأول : وهو أن المبدأ الأول لجميع الممكنات ابتداء . فما رأيت قوماً من الفلاسفة قالوا به. بل اتفقوا على القول الثاني ، وهو أنه تعالى واحد والواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، فهر تعالى علة للعقل الأول . ثم إن العقل الأول علة لثلاثة أشياء : العقل الثاني ، والنفس ، والفلك . وسيأتي تفصيل قولهم في هذا الباب
·
والموضع الثاني من البحث : هو أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن واجب الوجود يؤثر في وجود الأجسام، تأثير على سبيل الإيجاب الذاتي ، ثم إنه يركبها على الصفات المختلفة بحسب اختيارها ؟ فعل هذا التقدير : إنه موجب بالنسبة إلى الذوات ، ومختار بالنسبة إلى الصفات
These are the ones who said: this world is a body, and every body is possible in its essence, in respect of its essence, and in respect of all parts of its quiddity. And every possible must have an agent. And the agent in all bodies cannot be a body. Then they said: the agent by way of choice is unintelligible, based on the doubts we mentioned. So it is established that that agent is necessitating by essence. So this world is possible in existence in itself, necessary in existence due to the necessity of its cause and its efficient cause. And this is the doctrine of the majority of philosophers, who are followers of Aristotle. For this statement is what this man chose and his companions and followers from the earlier and later [philosophers] supported. Then here there are two points for investigation:
The first point: is it conceivable that the first principle is the principle of all possibles, or is it said that it is a principle for one thing, and then that one thing becomes a principle for multiplicity? As for the first statement—that the first principle is for all possibles from the beginning—I have not seen any group of philosophers who said it. Rather, they agreed on the second statement, which is that He is one, and from the one only one proceeds. So He is the cause of the first intellect. Then the first intellect is the cause of three things: the second intellect, the soul, and the sphere. And the detail of their statement about this topic will come later.
The second point of investigation: why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary Being influences the existence of bodies by way of essential necessitation, and then He compounds them with different attributes according to their choice? On this assumption, He is necessitating with respect to essences, and choosing with respect to attributes.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical position and then raises two investigations. It begins by stating that the philosophers hold the world to be a body, and every body is contingent (possible) in its essence and in all its parts. Therefore, every contingent requires an agent. That agent cannot be a body, and moreover, the philosophers deny that the agent acts by choice, deeming it unintelligible based on earlier doubts. Hence, the agent is considered 'necessitating by essence' (mūjib bi-l-dhāt), meaning the world is contingent in itself but necessary through its cause. This is attributed to the majority of philosophers following Aristotle.

The author then notes two points for investigation. The first point asks: is the First Principle the direct cause of all possibles, or only of one thing which then causes multiplicity? The author says he has not seen any philosopher claim the former; they unanimously hold the latter. Their principle is 'from the one only one proceeds' — so the First Intellect emanates from God, and from that intellect come three: a second intellect, a soul, and a sphere. The second point asks: why can’t the Necessary Being cause bodies by essential necessitation but then combine them with differing attributes by choice? The suggested compromise is that He is necessitating with respect to essences but choosing with respect to attributes.

Key Terms
المبدأ الأول
العقل الأول
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers completely deny divine choice, but this second investigation proposes a possible middle ground.
Correction: The philosophers are not necessarily committed to absolute necessitation; the author raises the possibility of combining necessitation for essences with choice for attributes.

Checkpoint
On the first investigation, what do the philosophers agree?
A) The First Principle directly causes all possibles.
B) The First Principle causes only the first intellect, from which multiplicity arises.
C) The First Principle causes many things directly.
D) The First Principle is not a cause of anything.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers unanimously hold that from the One only one proceeds, and the second investigation leaves open the possibility that the Necessary Being combines necessitation for essences with choice for attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the philosophers are right that 'from the One only one proceeds,' what does that imply about your own relationship with God? Do you feel distant from the ultimate cause, or does this chain of intermediaries increase your awe of His transcendence?
?
The second investigation suggests that God could necessitate essences but choose attributes. Have you ever considered that your own existence and traits might be a mix of divine necessity and divine choice? How does that shape your understanding of your own responsibility before Him?

Refutation of the Necessity of Celestial Spheres: Three Proofs
Razi presents the divine philosophers' three proofs against celestial spheres being necessary existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine philosophers refute the claim that celestial spheres are necessary existents, using three distinct arguments.

Core Idea
Multiple necessary existents are impossible; bodies are many and contingent.

Razi introduces the refutation by distinguishing between divine philosophers and theologians. He then presents three arguments from the divine philosophers, each showing why celestial bodies cannot be necessary existents. The first two arguments are complete; the third is cut off at the page break.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the structure: introduction, then three arguments introduced by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
The third argument is incomplete; try to infer what the full argument might be.
2
Identify the two groups of refuters
Razi mentions both divine philosophers and theologians. Focus on the philosophers' arguments in this lesson.
3
Analyze the first argument
The first argument says two necessary existents cannot coexist. Since bodies are many, they cannot be necessary.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic theology: God is one and necessary.
4
Analyze the second argument
The second argument distinguishes existence from essence in bodies. Anything with such distinction is possible, not necessary.
This parallels the proof that God's existence is identical with His essence.
5
Consider the incomplete third argument
The third argument begins: every extended body is divisible, and what is divisible depends on its parts. This implies contingency.
The argument is cut off; you may explore how it likely concludes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الرد على الحضرية. أما القائلون منهم بأن الأفلاك واجبة الوجود لذواتها. فاعلم أن الفلاسفة الإلهيين أبطلوا قولهم بطريق. والمتكلمون أبطلوا قولهم بطريق آخر. أما الفلاسفة الإلهيون فقد أبطلوا هذا المذهب من وجوه: الأول: قالوا: ثبت بالدليل أنه يمتنع وجود شيئين، يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته. والأجسام فيها كثرة، فيمتنع كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها. الثاني: إن هذه الأجسام، وجودها يغاير ماهياتها. وكل ما وجوده مغاير لماهيته، فإنه يكون ممكناً لذاته، لا واجباً لذاته. على ما قررنا هذا الدليل، في بيان أن وجوده تعالى لا يجوز أن يكون غير ماهيته. الثالث: إن كل متحيز منقسم، وكل منقسم فإنه محتاج إلى جزئه.
In refutation of the materialists. As for those among them who say that the celestial spheres are necessary existent in themselves, know that the divine philosophers have refuted their statement in one way, and the theologians have refuted their statement in another way. As for the divine philosophers, they have refuted this doctrine in [several] respects: First: They said: It is proven by evidence that it is impossible for two things to exist, each of which is necessary existent in itself. And bodies contain multiplicity, so it is impossible for them to be necessary existent in themselves. Second: These bodies, their existence is other than their essences. And everything whose existence is other than its essence is possible in itself, not necessary in itself, according to how we established this proof in the demonstration that His, the Exalted, existence is not allowable to be other than His essence. Third: Every occupant of space is divisible, and everything divisible is in need of its part.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the opinion that the celestial spheres are necessary existent in themselves. The author distinguishes two groups that refute this: divine philosophers and theologians. He then presents three arguments from the divine philosophers. The first argument: it is impossible for two necessary existents to coexist, but bodies are many, so they cannot be necessary. The second argument: bodies have existence distinct from their essence; anything with such distinction is possible, not necessary. The author references an earlier proof that God's existence is identical with His essence. The third argument (incomplete): every extended body is divisible; everything divisible depends on its parts, implying contingency. The text breaks off here.

The Five Proofs of the Ilāhiyūn Philosophers for the Contingency of Bodies
Lists five arguments from the Ilāhiyūn philosophers proving every body is contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct rational proofs that the Ilāhiyūn philosophers used to establish that every body is contingent (mumkin) and not necessary in itself.

Core Idea
Bodies are contingent because they exhibit multiplicity, composition, and dependence on external causes.

The Ilāhiyūn philosophers (divine philosophers) argued against the notion that bodies could be necessary in themselves. They provided five proofs, each attacking a different feature of bodies: multiplicity, distinction of essence and existence, divisibility, composition, and dependence on external determinants. These proofs collectively show that all bodies are possible (mumkin) rather than necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage lists five numbered arguments.
2
Identify the common logical structure
Each proof begins by noting a characteristic of bodies (e.g., multiplicity, composition) and then concludes that this characteristic makes them contingent. Identify this pattern in each proof.
3
Focus on the first two proofs
The first proof uses the impossibility of two necessary existents. The second uses the distinction between existence and essence. Understand why these imply contingency.
The second proof is especially important in philosophical theology.
4
Examine the last three proofs
The third proof uses divisibility, the fourth uses composition from matter and form, and the fifth uses dependence on external determinants. Notice how each leads to need for a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة الإلميون فقد أبطلوا هذا المذهب من وجوه:
الأول: قالوا: ثبت بالدليل أنه يمتنع وجود شيئين، يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته. والأجسام فيها كثرة، فيمتنع كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها.
الثاني: إن هذه الأجسام، وجودها يغاير ماهياتها. وكل ما وجوده مغاير لماهيته، فإنه يكون ممكناً لذاته، لا واجباً لذاته. على ما قررنا هذا الدليل، في بيان أن وجوده لا يجوز أن يكون غير ماهيته.
الثالث: إن كل متحيز منقسم، وكل منقسم فإنه محتاج إلى جزئه الذي هو غيره، وكل متحيز يحتاج إلى غيره، فيكون ممكناً.
الرابع: إن كل جسم فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة، وكل مركب ممكن. وقد دلنا أيضاً: على أنه لا يجوز أن تكون الهيولى والصورة واجبة لذاتها.
الخامس: إن كل جسم فإنه لا ينفك عن وضع معين، وعن شكل معين. والمقتضي لذلك الوضع والشكل، ليس هو ذاته بل غيره. وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته.
فهذه الوجوه الخمسة هي التي عليها تعويل الفلاسفة في بيان أن كل جسم فهو ممكن لذاته.
As for the Ilāhiyūn philosophers, they refuted this doctrine in several ways: First: They said: It is established by proof that it is impossible for two things to exist, each of which is necessary in itself. And bodies contain multiplicity, so it is impossible for them to be necessary in themselves. Second: These bodies, their existence is distinct from their essences. And everything whose existence is distinct from its essence is possible in itself, not necessary in itself. According to our elaboration of this proof in showing that His existence is not permissible to be other than His essence. Third: Every extended thing is divisible, and everything divisible is in need of its part which is other than itself, and every extended thing is in need of something other than itself, so it is possible. Fourth: Every body is composed of matter and form, and every composite is possible. And we have also demonstrated that it is not permissible for matter and form to be necessary in themselves. Fifth: Every body is not free from a specific position and a specific shape. The determinant of that position and shape is not its essence but something else. And everything that is like that is possible in itself. So these five ways are those upon which the philosophers rely in showing that every body is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of five proofs from the Ilāhiyūn philosophers that every body is contingent (mumkin) rather than necessary (wājib) in itself. The arguments are listed sequentially: (1) The impossibility of two necessary existents co-existing, while bodies are many. (2) The distinction between existence and essence in bodies, which implies possibility. (3) Divisibility of extended things leads to need for parts, hence contingency. (4) Composition from matter and form implies contingency, as matter and form themselves cannot be necessary. (5) Dependence on external determinants for position and shape shows contingency. The passage concludes by stating that these five proofs are the foundation of the philosophers' position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency means temporal creation (that bodies began in time).
Correction: Here contingency (imkān) means ontological dependence on a cause, not necessarily temporal origination. The proofs show bodies require a cause for their existence, not that they are created in time.

Checkpoint

B) Because if existence were distinct from essence, it would be an accident.
C) Because everything whose existence is distinct from its essence is possible in itself.
D) Because necessity requires that existence be an attribute of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The five proofs from multiplicity, essence-existence distinction, divisibility, composition, and external dependence collectively show that every body is contingent (mumkin) and not necessary in itself.

How would your daily actions change if you truly internalized that your existence at every moment depends on something outside yourself? Will you pause today to acknowledge that dependence on Allah?
?
The second proof says your existence is distinct from your essence. You are not the source of your own existence. Does that realisation unsettle your pride? When you succeed or fail, do you remember that both existence and attributes are given to you? What would it mean to live with constant awareness that you are not self-sufficient?

The philosophers argue that your body's shape and position are determined by external causes, not by your essence. Think of one physical feature you are proud of or dissatisfied with. Does it belong to you, or is it a gift from something else? How does this proof challenge your sense of ownership over your own body? Will you thank the One who determined it?

Refuting Democritus on Self-Existent and Self-Moving Parts
Refutes Democritus's doctrine using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two philosophical arguments that refute Democritus's claim that atomic parts are self-existent and self-moving.

Core Idea
A body cannot be necessary in itself or cause its own motion.

Democritus held that the building blocks of the universe are tiny, indivisible parts that exist necessarily and move on their own, eventually forming the heavens. The philosophers respond with two clear arguments: first, no body can be necessary in itself; second, a body cannot be the source of its own motion. This lesson unpacks these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the two main points the philosophers make.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument refers back to an earlier proof that no body can be necessary in itself. Understand why this applies directly to Democritus's claim.
This is a summary reference, not a new proof.
3
Examine the second argument and its proof
The second argument says a body cannot be self-moving. The proof: a body is unchanging (bāqin), motion is change, and the unchanging cannot be a cause of change. Trace this logic carefully.
The key premise is that the unchanging cannot produce change.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how these two arguments together dismantle Democritus's doctrine: if the parts are not necessary and cannot move themselves, his explanation of the universe fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المذهب الثاني: وهو قول ديمقراطيس، بأن الأجزاء واجبة الوجود لذواتها، وهي متحركة لذواتها، فحصل من حركاتها المضطربة هذه الأفلاك. فاعلم أن الفلاسفة أبطلوا هذا المذهب من وجوه: الأول: ما سبق أن الجسم لا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته. والثاني: إن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركاً لذاته. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه: الأول: إن الجسم غير متغير، والحركة عبارة عن نفس التغير. والباقي لا يكون علة للمتغير.
And as for the second doctrine—and it is the saying of Democritus that the parts are necessary in themselves, and they are moving by themselves, and from their erratic motions these celestial spheres arose. Know that the philosophers refuted this doctrine in several ways: First: What preceded, that a body cannot be necessary in itself. Second: A body is impossible to be self-moving. And they argued for it with proofs: First: The body is unchanging, and motion is itself change. And the unchanging cannot be a cause of the changing.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage states and then refutes Democritus's doctrine that atomic parts are necessary existents and self-moving, producing the heavens from their random motions. The philosophers offer two main refutation points: (1) The previous proof that no body can be necessary in itself. (2) A body cannot be self-moving. For the second point, they provide a proof: a body is unchanging (bāqin) in its essence, while motion is change. Since the unchanging cannot cause change, a body cannot be the cause of its own motion. The argument here is complete as far as presented, though the text alludes to further proofs.

Key Terms

متحركة لذواتها
غير متغير
باقٍ
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument is about motion as a property that bodies have, rather than specifically about self-motion.
Correction: The argument targets self-motion (being the cause of one's own motion) using the principle that the unchanging cannot produce change. It is not a general objection to motion itself.

Checkpoint

C) The celestial spheres would be chaotic if motion were random.
D) Democritus's view contradicts Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers refute Democritus by showing that no body can be necessary in itself and no body can be the cause of its own motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Democritus believed that random motion of particles could produce order. Where in your life have you trusted in chance or random circumstances rather than in the purposeful creation of God?

The Third Refutation: Why the Heavens Cannot Be by Chance
Presents the third refutation: heavens not by chance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the perfect order of the heavens cannot be attributed to chance, and how theologians refute it through the origination of bodies.

Core Idea
The perfect design of the heavens makes chance an impossible explanation.

The author presents the third refutation against materialists who claim the heavens occur by chance. He argues that the heavens possess perfect attributes, so their occurrence by sheer coincidence is intellectually improbable. He also notes that theologians have refuted this same claim by demonstrating the origination (huduth) of bodies. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: first the rational improbability, then the reference to the theologians' argument.
2
Understand the rational argument
The author says it is 'distant in the intellect' that the heavens, with their perfect attributes, occur by chance. Consider why perfection and design lead to this conclusion.
Chance tends to produce randomness, not intricate order.
3
Reflect on the theological approach
Theologians refute the same claim by proving the origination (huduth) of bodies, which implies a creator. How does this connect to the chance argument?
If bodies originate, they require a cause, so chance is ruled out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا المذهب : إن هذه الأفلاك مع كونها موصوفة بأكمل الصفات ، وأحسن النعوت اللائقة بالأجسام ، يبعد في العقل حدوثها ووقوعها ، على سبيل الاتفاق. وأما المتكلمون فقد أبطلوا هذا القول أيضاً بالبناء على حدوث الأجسام
وأما المذهب الثالث وهو قول من يقول : الحوادث تحدث لا لمؤثر . فالكلام المستقصى فيه ، قد تقدم في مسألة إثبات الصانع واجب الوجود لذاته. وأيضاً : فكل ما كان اتفاقياً، فإنه لا يكون دائماً ولا أكثرياً . ونحن نشاهد أحوال العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل واقعة على ترتيب لازم ، ونظم غير متغير، فيمتنع وقوعها على سبيل الاتفاق
فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معاقد الدلائل في إبطال أقوال الطوائف الدهرية
The third aspect in refuting this doctrine: That these heavenly spheres, despite being described with the most perfect attributes and the best qualities befitting bodies, it is distant in the intellect that they occur and happen by way of coincidence. As for the theologians, they have also refuted this statement by building upon the origination of bodies.
As for the third doctrine, which is the statement of those who say: 'Events occur without any effecter' - the detailed discussion about it has preceded in the issue of establishing the Maker, the Necessary Existent by essence. Moreover, everything that is coincidental is neither perpetual nor frequent. And we observe the states of the higher world and the lower world occurring upon a necessary order and an unchanging system, so it is impossible for them to occur by way of coincidence.
This is the indication of the proofs in refuting the statements of the materialist sects.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the third refutation (al-wajh al-thalith) against the materialists (dehriyya) who claim the heavens are eternal or occur by chance. The author argues that the heavens have perfect attributes, so it is intellectually improbable that they arise by accident. The mutakallimun refute this by proving the origination (huduth) of bodies. Then the author addresses the third doctrine (al-madhhab al-thalith) which claims events occur without any cause (la li-mu'aththir). He says the detailed refutation was already given in the discussion of proving the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud). Additionally, he argues that coincidental things are not perpetual or frequent, but we observe the universe in a necessary and unchanging order, so it cannot be by chance. The passage concludes the refutation of the materialist sects.

Key Terms
أفلاك
اتفاق
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against chance is based purely on observed empirical order.
Correction: The argument combines rational improbability (perfect attributes make chance unlikely) and theological proof (origination of bodies), not just empirical observation.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the author gives for rejecting the idea that the heavens occur by chance?
A) The heavens are eternal and cannot be contingent.
B) The heavens have perfect attributes, making chance improbable.
C) The theologians have proven the heavens are created by Allah.
D) The heavens are observable and thus cannot be random.

Key Takeaway
The perfect attributes of the heavens make their occurrence by chance rationally impossible; additionally, the origination of bodies provides a theological refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky, do you recognize the perfect order as a sign of Allah's intentional creation, or do you take it for granted? What could change in your worship if you truly internalized that none of this is random?
?
The passage says it is 'distant in the intellect' that the heavens occur by chance. Think of a blessing in your life that you have dismissed as a coincidence. How would your gratitude increase if you attributed it directly to Allah?
?
The theologians refuted chance by focusing on the origination of bodies. Do you live as if every moment of your existence is a new creation from Allah? What would it mean for your sense of responsibility if you knew that nothing—not even your next breath—happens by accident?

Refuting the Doctrine of Events Without a Cause
Refutes the doctrine of events without a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that events cannot occur without a cause, based on the observation of constant and orderly patterns in the universe.

Core Idea
If events were uncaused, they would not be regular, but the universe shows constant order.

The author turns to a third doctrine — the claim that events occur without any effecter (la li-mu'aththir). He reminds that a detailed refutation was already given in the context of proving the Necessary Existent. Then he offers a fresh argument: coincidental events are neither perpetual nor frequent, yet we observe the higher and lower worlds in necessary order and unchanging system. Hence it is impossible for them to occur by chance. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: reference to previous discussion, then a new argument from regularity.
2
Understand the argument from constancy
The author argues that if events were coincidental (occurring without a cause), they would not be perpetual (da'im) nor frequent (akthari). But we observe the universe in a necessary order (tartib lazim) and unchanging system (nazm ghayr mutaghayyir). Therefore, it cannot be by chance.
Coincidence implies irregularity, but the universe is regular.
3
Connect to the prior proof
The author mentions that the detailed refutation of uncaused events has already been given in the proof of the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud). How does this new argument complement that?
The prior proof established that contingent beings need a cause; this one shows that the observed regularity excludes chance.

Tenth Objection: God's Distinct Existence with Each Created Thing
Al-Razi's tenth objection: reductio against distinct existence concepts leading to divine composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses a reductio argument to show that the opponent's principle (distinct concepts imply real composition) leads to absurd consequences, including the composition of God.

Core Idea
Distinct concepts about God don't make Him composite—al-Razi refutes that.

In this final objection, al-Razi extends the argument about God's existence relative to multiple created entities. He argues that if every distinct relational concept (like 'existence with the first intellect' vs 'existence with the second intellect') implied a real distinction in God, then even the simplest entities would be composite, leading to absurdities. This passage is a powerful reductio ad absurdum that exposes the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read through the tenth objection in Arabic, noting the lists of entities and the logical flow.
The passage uses enumeration of intellects, spheres, souls, etc.
2
Understand the opponent's principle
The opponent holds that distinct concepts about God correspond to real distinctions in His essence. Al-Razi applies this to God's existence with different created things.
3
Trace the reductio
Al-Razi shows that if this principle holds, then even God would be composite, which is absurd. Also, causation would be negated. Hence the principle is false.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
This objection illustrates how kalam arguments use logical consistency to defend divine simplicity.
The interlocutor's own standards are used against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض العاشر : المفهوم من قولنا : إنه تعالى موجود مع العقل الأول مغاير للمفهوم من قولنا : إنه موجود مع العقل الثاني ، ومع الفلك الأول والفلك الثاني . وهكذا الكلام في كونه تعالى موجود مع كل واحد من العقول والنفوس والأفلاك والأعراض والصور. ثم تذكر ذلك التقسيم إلى آخره أو نقول : إنه تعالى قبل كل واحد من هذه العقول والأفلاك ، إما بالعلة أو بالرتبة . ولا شك أن المفهوم من كونه قبل هذا مغاير للمفهوم من كونه قبل ذلك . فيثبت : أن الاعتبار الذي ذكروه لو صح لزم البسائط بأسرها مركبة ، ولزم كون الباري تعالى مركباً ، ولزم نفي التأثير والمؤثر على الاطلاق . ولما كانت هذه الأشياء باطلة ، علمنا : أن ما ذكروه باطل والله أعلم
The tenth objection: The concept of our saying that He, exalted, exists with the first intellect is other than the concept of our saying that He exists with the second intellect, and with the first sphere and the second sphere. And so on regarding His, exalted, being existent with each one of the intellects, souls, spheres, accidents, and forms. Then you mention that division to its end. Or we say: He, exalted, is prior to each one of these intellects and spheres, either by causality or by rank. There is no doubt that the concept of His being prior to this is other than the concept of His being prior to that. So it is established that the consideration they mentioned, if it were valid, would necessarily make all simple entities composite, and necessarily make the Creator, exalted, composite, and necessitate negating causation and the efficient cause absolutely. Since these things are false, we know that what they mentioned is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
The tenth and final objection applies the same reasoning to God's existence relative to various created entities (intellects, souls, spheres, accidents, forms). The concept of God existing with one created thing differs from the concept of His existing with another. Similarly, His priority (being before) each entity has distinct concepts. If the opponent's principle (that distinct concepts imply real composition) is accepted, then even the simplest entities, including God, would be composite. This is absurd, as is the denial of causation. Therefore, the opponent's argument is false.

Key Terms
مفهوم
موجود
عقل
فلك
قبل
بسيط
مركب
باري
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi believes God is composite.
Correction: Al-Razi is using reductio: if the opponent's principle were true, God would be composite, but that's absurd, so the principle is false.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say would follow if the opponent's consideration were valid?
A) God would be simple.
B) All simple entities would be composite, including God.
C) Causation would be affirmed.
D) The intellects would be independent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's tenth objection shows that the opponent's principle of distinct concepts leading to real composition, when applied to God's relations with multiple creations, leads to absurdities that invalidate the principle itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like 'Creator', 'Sustainer', 'Merciful', do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'parts' of Allah? How does al-Razi's reductio expose the danger of this subtle error? Will you pause today and consciously affirm that Allah is One without composition, even in your thoughts?
?
If you found yourself assuming that every different concept you have about God implies a real difference in His essence, what specific mental check will you use to correct yourself, based on this lesson? Write down one practical step you will take this week to guard against attributing composition to Allah in your worship and reflection.
?
This argument uses logic to protect tawhid. Have you ever felt that rational arguments are unnecessary for faith? Reflect: does your love for Allah move you to learn sound reasoning so you can safeguard His oneness in your heart? What will you do to strengthen your intellectual defence of tawhid starting today?

The First Emanation: One Thing and a Substance
Razi establishes the first emanation is one thing and a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle 'from the One only one proceeds' is used to establish that the first emanation is one thing, and how logical disjunction forces it to be a substance rather than an accident.

Core Idea
The first creation must be both one and a substance, because accidents depend on substances.

The argument begins with a foundational principle of Islamic philosophy: from the absolutely simple One (God) only one thing can directly emanate. Razi then employs a simple classification: every possible existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ). Through a careful elimination of accident, he concludes that the first emanation must be a substance. This lesson walks through that initial reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of 'one from one'
Read the opening statement: it is established that God is free from multiplicity, so from Him only one can proceed. This is the basis for the whole argument.
This principle is a major point of discussion in Islamic philosophy.
2
Learn the classification of existents
Every possible existent is either a substance (self-subsistent) or an accident (dependent on substance). The first emanation must be one of these.
3
See why accident cannot be first
An accident depends on a substance and is posterior. If the first emanation were an accident, it would be a cause of substances, making accident prior to substance – a circularity (dawr). This is impossible.
Circularity is a standard logical impossibility in kalam.
4
Conclude substance
Since accident is refuted, the first emanation must be a substance. This sets the stage for further determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الصادر الأول عن الله تعالى شيء واحد. قالوا: ثبت أنه تعالى فرد منزه عن جميع جهات الكثرة، وثبت أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، فوجب القطع بأن الصادر الأول عن الله تعالى شيء واحد. فنقول: ثبت أن كل ممكن فهو إما جوهر وإما عرض، فذلك الصادر الأول إما جوهر وإما عرض. لا جائز أن يكون عرضاً، لأن العرض محتاج إلى الجوهر، ومتأخر بالرتبة عن الجوهر. فلو كان الصادر الأول عرضاً، لكان علة للجواهر، فحينئذ يكون العرض سابقاً على الجوهر، مع أن الجوهر كان سابقاً على العرض، فيلزم الدور، وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الصادر الأول جوهر. فنقول: ذلك الجوهر إما أن يكون جسماً، أو جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم، أو لا جسماً ولا جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم. والأول باطل لأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فتكوين الجسم لا يمكن إلا بتقدم تكوين أجزائه. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع أن يكون الصادر الأول جسماً. ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم، لأن ذلك الجزء إما الهيولى أو الصورة، ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول هو الهيولى، لأن الصادر الأول يكون علة لما بعده، فلو كان الصادر الأول هيولى، لزم كون الهيولى علة مؤثرة، موجدة لسائر الأشياء. لكن الهيولى موجود قابل، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً. وهو محال. ولأن الهيوليات متساوية، فلو فرضنا هيولى تكون علة لهذه المعلولات، لكان كل هيولى كذلك. ومعلوم أنه باطل. ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول صورة، وذلك لأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك، لكانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى، فيكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى ليس بشركة من الهيولى، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وهو محال. فعلى هذا الصورة غنية في تأثيرها عن المادة، وكل ما كان غنياً في تأثيراته عن المادة، كان غنياً في وجوده عن المادة. فيلزم: أن لا تكون الصورة صورة. هذا خلف. فيثبت أن الصادر الأول ليس بجسم ولا بجزء من أجزاء الجسم. فنقول: فيجب أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً. ثم نقول: الجوهر المجرد، إما أن يتوقف صدور الآثار عنه على آلة جسمانية، أو لا يتوقف. والأول هو النفس. والثاني هو العقل. فنقول لا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول نفساً. لأن الصادر الأول علة لجميع الأجسام، وكونه علة لجميع الأجسام، يمتنع أن يكون موقوفاً على حصول آلة جسمانية، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وهو محال. وإذا بطل هذا، بطل كون الصادر الأول نفساً. وإذا بطل هذا، ثبت أن الصادر الأول عقل مجرد. وهو المسمى في لسان الشرع بالروح الأعظم. حيث قال سبحانه وتعالى: {يوم يقوم الروح والملائكة صفاً} وحيث قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أول ما خلق الله العقل».
That the first emanation from God Almighty is one thing. They said: It is established that He Almighty is one, free from all aspects of multiplicity, and it is established that the one does not emanate from him except one, so it is necessary to decide that the first emanation from God Almighty is one thing. We say: It is established that every possible thing is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ). So that first emanation is either a substance or an accident. It is not permissible that it be an accident, because the accident needs the substance and is posterior in rank to the substance. If the first emanation were an accident, it would be a cause for the substances, then the accident would be prior to the substance, while the substance was prior to the accident, leading to circularity (dawr), which is impossible. So it is established that the first emanation is a substance. We say: That substance is either a body (jism), or a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body, or neither a body nor a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body. The first is false because the body is composed of matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). The formation of a body is only possible after the formation of its parts. If that is so, it is impossible that the first emanation be a body. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body, because that part is either matter or form. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be matter, because the first emanation is a cause for what comes after it. If the first emanation were matter, it would necessitate that matter be an effective cause, bringing into existence other things. But matter is a receptive existent, so it would necessitate that one thing be both receptive and active together, which is impossible. And because matters are equal, so if we suppose a matter to be a cause for these effects, then every matter would be likewise, and it is known that this is false. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be form, because if the matter were so, then form would be a cause for the existence of matter, so its effect in the existence of matter would not be by partnership with matter; otherwise, it would necessitate the priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. On this basis, form is independent in its effect from matter, and everything that is independent in its effects from matter is independent in its existence from matter. It would then necessitate that form not be form. This is a contradiction (khulf). So it is established that the first emanation is neither a body nor a part of the parts of the body. We say: Then it must be a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Then we say: The simple substance, either its bringing about effects depends on a corporeal instrument or it does not depend. The first is the soul (nafs) and the second is the intellect (ʿaql). We say: It is not permissible that the first emanation be a soul, because the first emanation is the cause of all bodies, and its being the cause of all bodies makes it impossible that it be dependent on obtaining a corporeal instrument; otherwise, it would necessitate the priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. When this is refuted, the first emanation being a soul is refuted. When this is refuted, it is established that the first emanation is a simple intellect. And it is named in the language of the Law as the Supreme Spirit (al-rūḥ al-aʿẓam) where He, may He be exalted, said: {The day when the Spirit and the angels stand in rows} (Qur'an 78:38) and where he, peace be upon him, said: 'The first thing God created is the intellect.'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a proof by logical disjunction (taqsīm) aimed at identifying the first creation or emanation (al-ṣādir al-awwal) from God. The argument proceeds in stages. First, it invokes the principle that from the One (al-wāḥid) only one can proceed, establishing that the first emanation is a single thing. Then, since every possible existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ), the first emanation must be one of these. Accidents are dependent on substances and are posterior, so an accident cannot be the cause of substances (circularity). Therefore it is a substance. Next, this substance is either a body, a part of a body (matter or form), or neither. Bodies are composed, so they require prior parts; thus the first emanation cannot be a body. If it were matter, matter would be both receptive and effective, which is impossible; also matter is homogenous, so any matter would be a cause, which is false. If it were form, form would cause matter without matter's participation, making form independent of matter, which would deny its nature as form (form is inherently dependent on matter). So the first emanation is neither body nor part of body; therefore it is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Simple substances are either dependent on a corporeal instrument (soul) or independent (intellect). The first emanation causes all bodies, so it cannot depend on a body (otherwise circularity). Hence it is an intellect. The argument concludes by identifying this intellect with the Supreme Spirit (al-rūḥ al-aʿẓam) mentioned in the Qur'an (78:38) and with the first creation mentioned in the hadith: 'The first thing God created is the intellect.' Thus the philosophers' metaphysical proof is aligned with revealed texts.

Key Terms
الصادر الأول
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
جوهر
عرض
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the first creation could be an accident like light or the Pen.
Correction: This argument shows that an accident cannot be first because it depends on a substance and would cause circularity.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the first emanation be an accident?
A) Because accidents are multiple.
B) Because accidents are dependent on substances and would then be prior to substances, causing circularity.
C) Because accidents are not created by God.
D) Because accidents are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first emanation from God is one thing and must be a substance, not an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that the first creation must be a substance—an independent entity. What does this imply about the nature of your own existence? Are you living as a 'substance' that depends on Allah alone, or do you lean on created 'accidents' that cannot ultimately support you?
?
If the first emanation is one thing because of God's oneness, how does this principle challenge you when you try to serve multiple masters or divide your heart? In what specific area of your life are you attempting to derive benefit from something other than the One?
?
The circularity argument refutes the possibility of an accident causing a substance. In your spiritual life, have you ever inverted priorities—treating a secondary matter as foundational? What would it mean for you to realign your priorities so that your foundation is the substance of tawhid?
