From Simple Substance to Intellect: The Supreme Spirit
Razi completes the proof by elimination to identify the first emanation as a simple intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed reasoning that leads from substance to a simple intellect, and how this intellect is identified with the Supreme Spirit in revelation.

Core Idea
The first substance cannot be a body or part of a body; it must be a simple intellect.

Having established that the first emanation is a substance, Razi now probes deeper into what kind of substance it is. He eliminates body, matter, and form, concluding that it must be a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). He then distinguishes between soul (dependent on body) and intellect (independent), affirming it is an intellect. Finally, he links this philosophical conclusion to the Qur'anic 'Spirit' and the Prophetic hadith on the creation of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three possibilities for a substance
Razi divides substance into three: body, part of the quiddity of a body (matter or form), or neither. Read this classification carefully.
The term 'body' here means a composite of matter and form.
2
Examine why body is impossible
A body is composed of matter and form, and its formation requires these parts to exist first. The first emanation cannot be composed or depend on prior parts.
This is because the first emanation is the immediate cause of all else.
3
Examine why matter and form are impossible
Matter is receptive, not active; form depends on matter. Either would entail contradictions: matter cannot be both receptive and effective, form cannot be independent of matter.
Each is eliminated by showing it would lead to a logical absurdity.
4
Conclude it is a simple substance
Since body, matter, and form are all impossible, the first emanation must be a simple substance, neither a body nor a part of a body.
5
Distinguish soul vs. intellect
A simple substance is either a soul (depends on a corporeal instrument) or an intellect (does not depend). The first emanation causes all bodies, so it cannot depend on a body – thus it is an intellect.
The intellect is also called the Supreme Spirit in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الصادر الأول عن الله تعالى شيء واحد. قالوا: ثبت أنه تعالى فرد منزه عن جميع جهات الكثرة، وثبت أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، فوجب القطع بأن الصادر الأول عن الله تعالى شيء واحد. فنقول: ثبت أن كل ممكن فهو إما جوهر وإما عرض، فذلك الصادر الأول إما جوهر وإما عرض. لا جائز أن يكون عرضاً، لأن العرض محتاج إلى الجوهر، ومتأخر بالرتبة عن الجوهر. فلو كان الصادر الأول عرضاً، لكان علة للجواهر، فحينئذ يكون العرض سابقاً على الجوهر، مع أن الجوهر كان سابقاً على العرض، فيلزم الدور، وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الصادر الأول جوهر. فنقول: ذلك الجوهر إما أن يكون جسماً، أو جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم، أو لا جسماً ولا جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم. والأول باطل لأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فتكوين الجسم لا يمكن إلا بتقدم تكوين أجزائه. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع أن يكون الصادر الأول جسماً. ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم، لأن ذلك الجزء إما الهيولى أو الصورة، ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول هو الهيولى، لأن الصادر الأول يكون علة لما بعده، فلو كان الصادر الأول هيولى، لزم كون الهيولى علة مؤثرة، موجدة لسائر الأشياء. لكن الهيولى موجود قابل، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً معاً. وهو محال. ولأن الهيوليات متساوية، فلو فرضنا هيولى تكون علة لهذه المعلولات، لكان كل هيولى كذلك. ومعلوم أنه باطل. ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول صورة، وذلك لأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك، لكانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى، فيكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى ليس بشركة من الهيولى، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وهو محال. فعلى هذا الصورة غنية في تأثيرها عن المادة، وكل ما كان غنياً في تأثيراته عن المادة، كان غنياً في وجوده عن المادة. فيلزم: أن لا تكون الصورة صورة. هذا خلف. فيثبت أن الصادر الأول ليس بجسم ولا بجزء من أجزاء الجسم. فنقول: فيجب أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً. ثم نقول: الجوهر المجرد، إما أن يتوقف صدور الآثار عنه على آلة جسمانية، أو لا يتوقف. والأول هو النفس. والثاني هو العقل. فنقول لا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول نفساً. لأن الصادر الأول علة لجميع الأجسام، وكونه علة لجميع الأجسام، يمتنع أن يكون موقوفاً على حصول آلة جسمانية، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وهو محال. وإذا بطل هذا، بطل كون الصادر الأول نفساً. وإذا بطل هذا، ثبت أن الصادر الأول عقل مجرد. وهو المسمى في لسان الشرع بالروح الأعظم. حيث قال سبحانه وتعالى: {يوم يقوم الروح والملائكة صفاً} وحيث قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أول ما خلق الله العقل».
That the first emanation from God Almighty is one thing. They said: It is established that He Almighty is one, free from all aspects of multiplicity, and it is established that the one does not emanate from him except one, so it is necessary to decide that the first emanation from God Almighty is one thing. We say: It is established that every possible thing is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ). So that first emanation is either a substance or an accident. It is not permissible that it be an accident, because the accident needs the substance and is posterior in rank to the substance. If the first emanation were an accident, it would be a cause for the substances, then the accident would be prior to the substance, while the substance was prior to the accident, leading to circularity (dawr), which is impossible. So it is established that the first emanation is a substance. We say: That substance is either a body (jism), or a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body, or neither a body nor a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body. The first is false because the body is composed of matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). The formation of a body is only possible after the formation of its parts. If that is so, it is impossible that the first emanation be a body. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be a part of the parts of the quiddity of a body, because that part is either matter or form. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be matter, because the first emanation is a cause for what comes after it. If the first emanation were matter, it would necessitate that matter be an effective cause, bringing into existence other things. But matter is a receptive existent, so it would necessitate that one thing be both receptive and active together, which is impossible. And because matters are equal, so if we suppose a matter to be a cause for these effects, then every matter would be likewise, and it is known that this is false. And it is not permissible that the first emanation be form, because if the matter were so, then form would be a cause for the existence of matter, so its effect in the existence of matter would not be by partnership with matter; otherwise, it would necessitate the priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. On this basis, form is independent in its effect from matter, and everything that is independent in its effects from matter is independent in its existence from matter. It would then necessitate that form not be form. This is a contradiction (khulf). So it is established that the first emanation is neither a body nor a part of the parts of the body. We say: Then it must be a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Then we say: The simple substance, either its bringing about effects depends on a corporeal instrument or it does not depend. The first is the soul (nafs) and the second is the intellect (ʿaql). We say: It is not permissible that the first emanation be a soul, because the first emanation is the cause of all bodies, and its being the cause of all bodies makes it impossible that it be dependent on obtaining a corporeal instrument; otherwise, it would necessitate the priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. When this is refuted, the first emanation being a soul is refuted. When this is refuted, it is established that the first emanation is a simple intellect. And it is named in the language of the Law as the Supreme Spirit (al-rūḥ al-aʿẓam) where He, may He be exalted, said: {The day when the Spirit and the angels stand in rows} (Qur'an 78:38) and where he, peace be upon him, said: 'The first thing God created is the intellect.'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a proof by logical disjunction (taqsīm) aimed at identifying the first creation or emanation (al-ṣādir al-awwal) from God. The argument proceeds in stages. First, it invokes the principle that from the One (al-wāḥid) only one can proceed, establishing that the first emanation is a single thing. Then, since every possible existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident (ʿaraḍ), the first emanation must be one of these. Accidents are dependent on substances and are posterior, so an accident cannot be the cause of substances (circularity). Therefore it is a substance. Next, this substance is either a body, a part of a body (matter or form), or neither. Bodies are composed, so they require prior parts; thus the first emanation cannot be a body. If it were matter, matter would be both receptive and effective, which is impossible; also matter is homogenous, so any matter would be a cause, which is false. If it were form, form would cause matter without matter's participation, making form independent of matter, which would deny its nature as form (form is inherently dependent on matter). So the first emanation is neither body nor part of body; therefore it is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). Simple substances are either dependent on a corporeal instrument (soul) or independent (intellect). The first emanation causes all bodies, so it cannot depend on a body (otherwise circularity). Hence it is an intellect. The argument concludes by identifying this intellect with the Supreme Spirit (al-rūḥ al-aʿẓam) mentioned in the Qur'an (78:38) and with the first creation mentioned in the hadith: 'The first thing God created is the intellect.' Thus the philosophers' metaphysical proof is aligned with revealed texts.

هيولى
صورة
جوهر مجرد
نفس
عقل
الروح الأعظم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the intellect (ʿaql) is purely a philosophical concept, unrelated to Islamic scripture.
Correction: Razi explicitly cites the Qur'an (78:38) and the hadith 'The first thing God created is the intellect' to show that the intellect is the Supreme Spirit, a key part of Islamic cosmology.

Checkpoint

A) Because souls are multiple.
B) Because a soul depends on a corporeal instrument, but the first emanation is the cause of all bodies and cannot depend on a body.
C) Because souls are created later.
D) Because the soul is not simple.

Key Takeaway
The first emanation is a simple intellect, identified with the Supreme Spirit in the Qur'an and hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the very first creation was an intellect, what does that say about the purpose of your own intellect? Is it being used to seek the First Cause, or is it absorbed in trivialities? Today, how will you redirect your thinking toward what truly matters?
?
Razi argues that the first emanation cannot depend on any material instrument. In your life, where have you made your spiritual well-being dependent on material things? What step can you take this week to detach your soul from such dependencies?
?
The Supreme Spirit is identified with the intellect. Have you ever recognized your own intellect as a spiritual gift, not just a tool for worldly success? Will you commit to using it for contemplation of divine truths, even for a few minutes daily?

The First Emanation as Separate Intellect
Author proves the first emanation is an intellect, not body or soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the first created entity must be a separate intellect, not a body, form, or soul.

Core Idea
The first emanation is a pure intellect, independent of matter.

This passage presents a logical elimination argument used by philosophers to identify the first created thing. Beginning with a principle about cause and effect, the author systematically rules out material possibilities until only a separate intellect remains. The proof is concluded with a Qur'anic verse and a hadith identifying this intellect as the Supreme Spirit.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial principle
The passage opens with a key principle: a single thing cannot be both active and receptive simultaneously. This sets up the impossibility of matter being both cause and effect in the same respect.
This principle is used to reject certain candidates for the first emanation.
2
Eliminate body and prime matter
First, the author argues that prime matters (hyles) are all alike, so if one were the cause, all would be, which is false. Thus the first emanation cannot be prime matter or any body.
3
Eliminate form as a candidate
If the first emanation were a form, it would be independent of matter in its influence, which would then require form to exist without matter—contradicting the definition of form. Hence form is impossible.
4
Distinguish soul and intellect
Having concluded the first emanation is a separate substance (jawhar mujarrad), the author divides such substances into those dependent on a bodily instrument (soul) and those not (intellect).
5
Eliminate soul and conclude intellect
The first emanation is the cause of all bodies, so it cannot depend on a bodily instrument—otherwise it would be prior to itself. Thus it cannot be a soul; it must be an intellect. The passage then cites revelation confirming this as the Supreme Spirit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلا معاً. وهو محال . ولأن الهيوليات متساوية ، فلو فرضنا هيولى تكون علة لهذه المعلولات، لكان كل هيولى كذلك . ومعلوم أنه باطل . ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول : صورة ، وذلك لأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى ، فيكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى ليس بشركة من الهيولى، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ، وهو محال . فعلى هذا الصورة غنية في تأثيرها عن المادة ، وكل ما كان غنياً في تأثيراته عن المادة ، كان غنياً في وجوده عن المادة. فيلزم : أن لا تكون الصورة : صورة. هذا خلف : فيثبت أن الصادر الأول ليس بجسم ولا بجزء من أجزاء الجسم . فنقول : فيجب أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً. ثم نقول : الجوهر المجرد، إما أن يتوقف صدور الآثار عنه على آلة جسمانية، أو لا يتوقف. والأول : هو النفس . والثاني : هو العقل . فنقول لا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول نفساً . لأن الصادر الأول علة لجميع الأجسام ، وكونه علة الجميع الأجسام ، يمتنع أن يكون موقوفاً على حصول آلة جسمانية ، وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ، وهو محال . وإذا بطل هذا ، بطل كون الصادر الأول نفساً وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت أن الصادر الأول عقل مجرد . وهو المسمى في لسان الشرع : بالروح الأعظم. حيث قال سبحانه وتعالى : ( يوم يقوم الروح والملائكة صفا ) وحيث قال : عليه الصلاة والسلام : « أول ما خلق الله العقل » هذا تلخيص كلام القوم على أحسن الوجوه . والاعتراض على هذا الكلام من وجوه :
It is impossible for one thing to be both receptive and active simultaneously. And because the prime matters (hayulayat) are all the same, if we assumed a prime matter that is the cause of these effects, then every prime matter would be like that. And it is known that this is false. And it is not possible that the first emanation (al-sadir al-awwal) be a form (sura), because if the matter were so, then the form would be the cause of the existence of the prime matter, so its influence in the existence of the prime matter would not be by partnership with the prime matter, otherwise there would be the priority of the thing over itself, which is impossible. On this basis, the form is independent in its influence from matter, and whatever is independent in its influences from matter is independent in its existence from matter. So it follows that the form would not be a form. This is a contradiction. So it is established that the first emanation is not a body (jism) nor a part of the parts of the body. So we say: it must be a separate substance (jawhar mujarrad). Then we say: the separate substance either depends for the emanation of effects from it on a bodily instrument (ala jismaniyya) or does not depend. The first is the soul (nafs). The second is the intellect ('aql). So we say: it is not possible for the first emanation to be a soul, because the first emanation is the cause of all bodies, and its being the cause of all bodies makes it impossible for it to be dependent on obtaining a bodily instrument, otherwise the priority of the thing over itself would follow, which is impossible. And when this is refuted, the possibility of the first emanation being a soul is refuted, and when this is refuted, it is established that the first emanation is a separate intellect. And it is called in the language of the Law (lisan al-shar') the Supreme Spirit (al-ruh al-a'zam). Where He, glorified and exalted, said: (The day when the Spirit and the angels stand in rows) [78:38] and where he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'The first thing God created is the intellect.' This is a summary of the people's discourse in the best manner. And the objection to this discourse is from several aspects:
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an extended proof that the first emanation (the first created thing) must be a separate intellect, not a body, a form, a prime matter, or a soul. The author begins by stating a principle: a single thing cannot be both active (fa'il) and receptive (qabil) simultaneously. Then he argues that prime matters (hyles) are all alike in essence, so if one prime matter were the cause of certain effects, all prime matters would be the same cause, which is false. Next, he eliminates the possibility that the first emanation is a form (sura). If it were a form, it would be the cause of the existence of prime matter (hayula), but then its influence would not require partnership with matter; yet matter is needed for a form's actuality, leading to a contradiction (the form would not be a form). Therefore, the first emanation is not a body or any bodily part; it must be a separate substance (jawhar mujarrad). A separate substance is either dependent on a bodily instrument (like the soul) or not dependent (like the intellect). The first emanation cannot be a soul because it is the cause of all bodies, and being the cause of all bodies cannot depend on a bodily instrument—that would require the cause to be prior to itself, which is impossible. Thus the first emanation is a separate intellect, called in religious language the Supreme Spirit, supported by a Qur'anic verse and a hadith ('The first thing God created is the intellect'). The author concludes this section with a remark that this is a summary of the philosophers' discourse and then introduces objections.

جوهر مجرد

Misconception: Some think the first emanation could be a soul because it is a spiritual substance.
Correction: The passage shows that a soul depends on a bodily instrument to act, but the first emanation is the cause of all bodies and therefore cannot depend on a bodily instrument—eliminating the soul possibility.

Key Takeaway

Fifth Objection: Two Arguments Against the First Intellect as Sole Emanation
Two objections against the first intellect as sole emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections against the view that the first emanation is a single intellect: (1) the first intellect's composite essence implies multiplicity from the One, and (2) limiting direct causation to one effect diminishes divine power.

Core Idea
Two objections challenge the singularity of the first emanation: composition and power limitation.

The opponent presents two arguments against the claim that the first emanation is only one intellect. The first is rooted in the nature of the intellect as a composite substance, which would violate the principle that from the One only one can proceed. The second argues that if God only directly produces one effect, His power would be less than that of ordinary causes. These objections are meant to expose contradictions in the emanation theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two distinct arguments labeled 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
The response is not included in this excerpt.
2
Analyze the first objection
Identify the premise: substance is a genus, the intellect is a species under it. Therefore the intellect has a composite essence of genus and differentia. Conclude: multiplicity in the cause implies multiple effects.
This uses the Aristotelian hylomorphic framework.
3
Analyze the second objection
Notice the reductio ad absurdum: if God only creates one effect directly, He would be less powerful than lowly causes that produce multiple effects. The conclusion is deemed false, so the premise must be false.
This objection targets the principle of 'one cause, one effect'.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these objections are serious for the Neoplatonic emanation scheme. The author will respond in subsequent pages.
The absence of a response here shows the need to continue reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس: إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن الصادر الأول ليس إلا ذلك العقل الواحد، إلا أن ههنا ما يمنع منه. وذلك من وجهين: الأول: إن مذهبكم أن الجوهر جنس واحد. وأنواعه: العقل. فهذا العقل الأول داخل تحت الجنس، وكل ما كان داخلاً تحت الجنس كان ماهية مركبة من الجنس والفصل. فالعقل الأول ماهية مركبة. فإذا جعلناه معلولاً لذات الله تعالى، فقد صدر عن الله أكثر من الواحد. والثاني: إنه يقتضي أن يقال: إنه تعالى لم يقدر إلا على تكوين الأثر الواحد، فيكون رتبة واجب الوجود في التكوين والتأثير أقل من رتبة جميع المؤثرات الحقيرة، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل.
The fifth question: If what you have mentioned indicates that the first emanation is nothing but that one intellect, yet there is something here that prevents it. That is in two aspects: First: Your doctrine is that substance is one genus. And its species are: the intellect. So this first intellect falls under the genus, and everything that falls under the genus has an essence composed of genus and differentia. Thus the first intellect has a composite essence. If we make it an effect of the essence of God, then more than one has emanated from God. Second: It necessitates that it be said: He, exalted, is able only to produce one effect, so the rank of the Necessary of Existence in production and influence is lower than the rank of all the lowly efficient causes, and it is known that this is false.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the view that the first emanation is a single intellect. The first objection is based on the doctrine that substance (jawhar) is one genus and the intellect is its species. Since the first intellect falls under this genus, its essence (māhiyya) is composed of genus and differentia (fasl). Composition implies multiplicity in the essence, so if it proceeds directly from God's essence, more than one (i.e., the components) would emanate from the One. The second objection argues that if God only produces one effect directly, then His power and influence would be less than that of even lowly causes, which is absurd. The text does not include the response; it ends with the statement that such a consequence is false. The author likely refutes these objections on subsequent pages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections are about the intellect's own nature, not about divine unity.
Correction: The objections target the implications of the intellect's composition or God's limited power, which are problems for divine unity and omnipotence.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the first intellect being the sole first emanation?
A) The intellect cannot be an effect of God because it is too perfect.
B) The intellect has a composite essence, so more than one would emanate from God.
C) The intellect is not a substance, so it cannot be a first emanation.
D) The intellect is eternal, so it cannot be created.

Key Takeaway
Two arguments against the first intellect as sole emanation: its composite essence implies multiplicity from the One, and limiting God to one direct effect diminishes His power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the objection that limiting God's direct effect to one product would make Him lower than lowly causes, does your heart feel any unease? How do you reconcile God's absolute power with His choice to create through a chain of causes?
?
The first objection shows that composition implies multiplicity. Examine your own understanding of God's attributes: do you ever unintentionally think of His attributes as parts that could be separated? How does this lesson help you guard against that error?
?
Both objections challenge the idea that God's direct creation is only one thing. In your personal life, do you sometimes feel that God's actions in your life are limited or only produce one outcome? How does reflecting on God's infinite power change that perspective?

The Dilemma of Essence and Existence in the First Effect
Presents a dilemma about the essence of the first effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection against the philosophers' emanation scheme uses a dilemma about the essence of the first effect to expose a contradiction in their system.

Core Idea
If essence and existence are distinct, the first effect leads to either necessitarianism or a violation of 'from one only one'.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the philosophers' claim that the first effect has its possibility from itself and its existence from the First Principle. The objector forces a choice: if the essence is self-sufficient, it would be necessary; if dependent, then the First would cause two things. Both options undermine the philosophers' own principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and follow the literal translation. Notice the structure of the dilemma being presented.
The passage is a single continuous objection; read it as an argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The objection splits into two possibilities: the essence is either independent (ghani) or dependent (muhtaj) regarding the agent. Find where each branch is introduced.
The key phrases are 'إما أن تكون غنية عن الفاعل' and 'أو تكون محتاجة إلى الفاعل'.
3
Trace the consequences of each branch
Follow the logic: if independent, the essence would be necessarily existent by itself (impossible, since only God is necessary). If dependent, then the First Principle is the cause of both quiddity and existence, violating the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'.
Both branches lead to a contradiction of the philosophers' own claims.
4
Understand the overall aim of the objection
The objection is not merely semantic; it strikes at the core of the emanation scheme by showing that the assumed distinction between essence and existence in the first effect is incompatible with the axiom of unity of the First Principle.
It highlights a key tension in the philosophers' metaphysical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالسؤال الأول : إن قولكم : إن إمكانه حصول من ذاته ، ووجوده حصل من علته : مشعر بأن تلك الذات مغايرة للوجود ، فنقول : تلك الذات من حيث هي هي [ إما أن تكون غنية عن الفاعل ، أو تكون محتاجة إلى الفاعل . فإن كان الأول ، فالذات من حيث هي هي (۳)] واجبة التحقق لذاتها . وذلك محال . وإن كانت محتاجة إلى الفاعل ، وذلك الفاعل هو المبدأ [ الأول (٤) ] فحينئذ يكون المبدأ الأول علة لتلك الماهية ، وعلة لذلك الوجود ، فيكون قد صدر عنه أمران . وذلك يبطل أصلا (ه) كلامكم .
So the first question: Your statement that its possibility arises from itself, and its existence arises from its cause, suggests that that essence is other than existence. We say: That essence insofar as it is itself is either independent of the agent or needs the agent. If the first, then the essence insofar as it is itself is necessarily realized by itself, and that is impossible. And if it needs the agent, and that agent is the First Principle, then the First Principle would be a cause for that quiddity and a cause for that existence, so two things would have proceeded from it, and that nullifies the very foundation of your statement.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents an additional objection against the philosophers' emanation scheme. The objection focuses on the relationship between essence (dhat) and existence (wujud) in the first effect. The philosophers claim that possibility comes from the essence itself, while existence comes from the cause. This implies the essence is other than existence. The objection then considers two possibilities: either the essence is self-sufficient (ghani) or dependent (muhtaj). If self-sufficient, it would be necessary by itself, which is impossible (since only God is necessary). If dependent, then the First Principle would be the cause of both the quiddity (mahiyya) and the existence, meaning two things proceed from the First, contradicting the principle that from the One only one proceeds. Thus the philosophers' own principle is undermined.

Key Terms
ذات
وجود
ماهية
مبدأ أول
غني
محتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is just a semantic quibble about words.
Correction: It targets the core metaphysical commitment that from one simple cause only one effect can proceed, exposing a structural flaw.

Checkpoint
If the essence of the first effect is independent of the agent, what follows?
A) It is contingent
B) It is necessary by itself
C) It is non-existent
D) It is a cause of other things

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' emanation scheme cannot maintain both that the first effect's essence and existence are distinct and that from the One only one proceeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only God is self-sufficient (ghani), then in which areas of your life do you still act as if you can be independent of Him? How will you remind yourself of your constant need for Him today?
?
The philosophers' dilemma shows that treating anything other than God as self-sufficient is impossible. Is there any created thing—a person, a possession, or a status—that you secretly rely on as if it were necessary for your happiness? What step will you take to break that reliance?
?
The principle 'from one only one' led to a contradiction here. How does this challenge you to accept that God's creative power is not bound by human logical constraints? Will you submit to His absolute freedom even when it seems to defy your reasoning?

Applying the Active Intellect Argument to God
The opponent argues that the same principle for Active Intellect should apply to the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against the philosophers' emanation theory and how the author turns their own logic against them.

Core Idea
If the Active Intellect can produce multiplicity via recipients, so can God.

The opponent raises a clever objection: the philosophers claim the Active Intellect is the cause of all sublunar existence, yet it only has three aspects. The philosophers respond that existence is one and multiplicity comes from recipients. The author seizes on this: if that is allowed for the Active Intellect, why not for the Necessary Being? This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the structure: objection, response, counter-argument.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and response
Find where the opponent states the philosophers' view of the Active Intellect, and then the philosophers' defense that existence is one and multiplicity comes from recipients.
3
Analyze the author's counter-argument
Notice how the author applies the same logic to God with an 'a fortiori' (first principle) argument. Why is this effective?
This reveals an inconsistency in the philosophers' system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس: الستم قلتم: إن العقل الفعال هو المدير لما تحت فلك القمر؟ فقد حكمتم بأنه هو العلة لوجود كل ما يحدث في هذا العالم من الصور والأعراض، مع أنه لم يحصل فيه إلا الجهات الثلاث. فإن قالوا: العقل الفعال مبدأ للوجود، والوجود من حيث هو وجود معنى واحد، وإنه إنما يتكثر بحسب القوابل. فعلى هذا لا يلزم أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. فنقول: إذا جوزتم هذا فلم لا يجوز مثله في واجب الوجود، حتى نقول: الصادر عن واجب الوجود لذاته، هو الوجود فقط، وهو شيء واحد. والتكثر إنما يحصل بسبب الماهيات القابلة؟ فإن هذا الكلام إن كان مقبولاً في حق العقل الفعال، فذكره في واجب الوجود أولى.
The sixth question: Did you not say that the Active Intellect is the manager of what is beneath the sphere of the moon? You have judged that it is the cause of the existence of all forms and accidents that occur in this world, while it only contains the three aspects. If they say: The Active Intellect is the principle of existence, and existence insofar as it is existence is a single meaning, and it becomes multiple only according to the recipients. On this basis, it is not necessary that from one more than one proceeds. So we say: If you allow this, why do you not allow the same regarding the Necessary Being, such that we say: That which proceeds from the Necessary Being by virtue of Its essence is only existence, which is one thing, and multiplicity only occurs due to the receptive essences? For if this statement is acceptable regarding the Active Intellect, then mentioning it regarding the Necessary Being is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection. The opponent points out that the Active Intellect (al-‘aql al-fa‘‘al) is said to be the cause of all sublunar forms and accidents, yet it only has the three aspects (triple consideration of intellect, soul, sphere) that characterize the first intellect. The philosophers respond that the Active Intellect is the principle of existence, and existence is a single meaning that becomes multiple due to recipients. Therefore, from one (the Active Intellect) only one thing (existence) proceeds, and multiplicity comes from the recipients. The author turns this argument against them: If this is allowed for the Active Intellect, then it should be allowed for the Necessary Being (God), for He is even more deserving. Thus the principle would allow for multiplicity from God as well, undermining the emanationist restriction.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال
الوجود
القوابل
واجب الوجود
الماهيات القابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' response is consistent with their overall emanationist framework.
Correction: The author shows that the same logic leads to a conclusion the philosophers would reject: that God can produce multiplicity through recipients, violating the cherished principle that from one only one proceeds.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the philosophers' response regarding the Active Intellect should apply to the Necessary Being?
A) Because the Necessary Being is also a cause.
B) Because if multiplicity from one is allowed for the Active Intellect, it should also be allowed for the Necessary Being, who is more deserving.
C) Because the Necessary Being is the same as the Active Intellect.
D) Because existence is the same in both.

Key Takeaway
The author turns the philosophers' own logic against them by arguing that if multiplicity from one is allowed for the Active Intellect, it should be allowed for the Necessary Being, undermining the principle of emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God directly creates every detail of your life, or do you subconsciously attribute the diversity of blessings to secondary causes, as if God only produces one general effect? This lesson refutes that limited view. Will you commit to seeing God's hand in every multiplicity?

Dualistic Beliefs About God and Iblis
Presents dualistic claims about God and Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various dualistic claims that some sects make about God and Iblis as co-eternal principles.

Core Idea
Dualism claims two gods: one good, one evil.

This passage introduces the beliefs of those who say that God and Iblis are two divine principles. It presents the core claim and then shows how the proponents differ among themselves. Understanding these positions is essential before refuting them.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim of dualism
Read the opening lines that state the belief that God and Iblis are brothers, one good and one evil.
Notice the contrast between 'merciful, generous' and 'evil, vile'.
2
Identify the three subgroups
Look for the three different opinions about the nature of these two deities: both eternal, one generated from a thought, or light vs. darkness.
Each subgroup is introduced with 'منهم من قال'.
3
Reflect on the variety
The author notes these sects are numerous. Think about why it is important to know the range of dualistic views.
This sets up the need for a decisive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الناس من قال : الله تعالى وإبليس أخوان . إلا أن الله هو الإله الرحيم الكريم المحسن ، وإبليس هو الإله الشرير الخبيث القاسي . ثم إن القائلين بهذا القول اختلفوا . فمنهم من قال : كل واحد منهما قديم أزلي، واجب الوجود لذاته . وهما ليسا بجسمين ولا بجسمانيين . ومنهم من قال : إن الإله الخبيث الشرير إنما تولد من فكرة خطرت ببال الإله الكريم الرحيم . ومنهم من قال : إله العالم هو النور والظلمة . فالنور : هو الإله الرحيم، وجنده : الملائكة . والظلمة : هو إبليس، وجنده : الشياطين . وفي هذه المذاهب كثرة لا حصر لها . واعلم : أنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الدالة على توحيد الإله تعالى . والذي نذكره الآن أن نقول : الإله الخير الرحيم، إن لم يقدر على دفع الإله الشرير الخبيث فهو عاجز ، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية . وإن قدر على ...
Among people are those who said: God Almighty and Iblis are brothers. Except that God is the merciful, generous, good deity, and Iblis is the evil, vile, cruel deity. Then those who said this saying differed. Some of them said: Each of them is eternal, necessary of existence by itself. And they are neither bodies nor embodied. Some of them said: The evil, vile deity was generated from a thought that occurred to the mind of the merciful, generous deity. Some of them said: The deity of the world is light and darkness. Light is the merciful deity, and his soldiers are the angels. Darkness is Iblis, and his soldiers are the devils. These sects are numerous without limit. Know: We have mentioned the proofs indicating the oneness of God Almighty. What we now mention is that we say: The good, merciful deity, if he is not able to repel the evil, vile deity, then he is weak, and the weak is not fit for divinity. And if he is able to...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualistic beliefs that posit two divine principles. It begins by presenting the claim that God and Iblis are brothers, but then distinguishes between them as good and evil. The author then notes that the proponents of this view differ: some hold both to be eternal and self-necessary, neither corporeal; others claim the evil deity originated from a thought in the good deity's mind; yet others identify the deity with light and darkness, with angels and devils as their hosts. After stating that such views are numerous, the author invokes a proof for monotheism: if the good deity cannot prevent the evil deity, he is weak and thus unworthy of divinity; if he can prevent but does not, the implication is left incomplete in the text. The argument is a standard theological refutation of dualism, relying on the incompatibility of weakness or inaction with the concept of divinity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there are two co-eternal deities, one good and one evil.
Correction: The author presents this as a false belief that will be refuted.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the subgroups mentioned among dualists?
A) Both deities are eternal and self-necessary.
B) The evil deity was generated from a thought in the good deity.
C) The good deity is light and the evil deity is darkness.
D) The evil deity is more powerful than the good deity.

Key Takeaway
Dualistic beliefs vary, but all share the idea of two opposing divine principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you were to defend the unity of Allah against someone who says 'evil proves there must be two gods,' how would you use this passage's description of their internal disagreements to weaken their argument? Write your response as if speaking to them directly.

The Proof for Monotheism Against Dualism
Presents a logical proof for monotheism against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that refutes dualism by showing that a good deity who cannot stop an evil one would be weak and unworthy of divinity.

Core Idea
If the good god cannot stop evil, he is weak and not God.

Having presented dualistic claims, the author now offers a simple but powerful proof for monotheism. The argument focuses on the inability of a supposedly good deity to prevent an evil deity, leading to a contradiction with the concept of divinity. This logical proof undermines all forms of dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the beginning of the proof
Read from 'We have mentioned the proofs' to the end of the passage. Notice that the author sets up a conditional argument.
The proof is incomplete in the text, but the logic is clear.
2
Identify the two conditions
The argument considers two possibilities: either the good deity cannot repel the evil one, or he can. If he cannot, he is weak.
The second condition is cut off, but it likely implies if he can but does not, he is not good.
3
Apply the argument to all dualist views
Think about how this proof refutes each variant presented earlier. None can escape the dilemma.
This shows the universality of the proof.

Refutation of Dualistic Beliefs about Good and Evil Gods
Author refutes dualistic beliefs using a logical dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that refutes all dualistic beliefs positing two independent deities—one good and one evil.

Core Idea
A single good god cannot coexist with an independent evil god; logic shows the contradiction.

The author presents several dualistic views held by ancient sects, then deploys a powerful logical dilemma. By examining the options available to the 'good god' in relation to evil, he demonstrates that any belief in two rival deities collapses into absurdity. This lesson will walk you through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualistic views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the various sects described: some say God and Iblis are brothers, some say the evil god originated from a thought, some say the gods are light and darkness.
The passage begins with a summary of dualistic beliefs before the refutation.
2
Identify the dilemma's structure
The author presents three possible scenarios for the good god regarding the evil god: unable to repel, able but unwilling, or able and repelling. Find these in the text.
Each scenario leads to a contradiction or absurdity.
3
Analyze each horn of the dilemma
For each scenario, see why it disqualifies the deity from divinity: incapability, being pleased with evil, or rendering the evil god incapable while evil still exists.
Take notes on how each point refutes the dualistic claim.
4
Understand the universal application
Notice that the argument does not depend on the specific details of any particular dualist sect. It applies to any system claiming two independent deities.
This is why the refutation is powerful and comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف الناس في ذلك. فمنهم من قال: الله تعالى وإبليس أخوان. إلا أن الله هو الإله الرحيم الكريم المحسن، وإبليس هو الإله الشرير الخبيث القاسي. ثم إن القائلين بهذا القول اختلفوا. فمنهم من قال: كل واحد منهما قديم أزلي، واجب الوجود لذاته. وهما ليسا بجسمين ولا بجسمانيين. ومنهم من قال: إن الإله الخبيث الشرير إنما تولد من فكرة خطرت ببال الإله الكريم الرحيم. ومنهم من قال: إله العالم هو النور والظلمة. فالنور: هو الإله الرحيم، وجنده: الملائكة. والظلمة: هو إبليس، وجنده: الشياطين. وفي هذه المذاهب كثرة لا حصر لها. واعلم: أنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الدالة على توحيد الإله تعالى. والذي نذكره الآن أن نقول: الإله الخير الرحيم، إن لم يقدر على دفع الإله الشرير الخبيث فهو عاجز، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية. وإن قدر على دفعه ولم يدفعه كان راضياً بالشر، والراضي بالشر شرير. فالإله الخير: هذا خلف. وإن دفعه فحينئذ يكون الإله الشرير المدفوع عن فعل الشر: عاجزاً. والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية. ولأنا إذا قلنا: الشر لا يصدر إلا عن ذلك الشرير، ثم إن إله الخير دفعه دفعة واحدة عن ذلك الشر فوجب أن لا يبقى في العالم شيء من الشرور والآفات. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل.
People have differed regarding this. Some said: God Almighty and Iblis are brothers, except that God is the merciful, generous, good deity, and Iblis is the evil, wicked, harsh deity. Then those who hold this saying differed among themselves. Some said: each of them is eternal, pre-eternal, necessary of existence in itself, and they are neither bodies nor embodied. Some said: the evil, wicked deity was generated from a thought that occurred to the merciful, generous deity. Some said: the deity of the world is light and darkness. Light is the merciful deity and his army is the angels; darkness is Iblis and his army is the devils. These sects are abundant beyond enumeration. And know: we have already mentioned proofs for the oneness of God Almighty. What we will mention now is that we say: the good, merciful deity, if he is not able to repel the evil, wicked deity, then he is incapable, and the incapable is not fit for divinity. And if he is able to repel him but does not repel him, then he is pleased with evil, and the one pleased with evil is evil. So the good deity: this is a contradiction. And if he repels him, then the evil deity, repelled from doing evil, becomes incapable, and the incapable is not fit for divinity. And also if we say: evil does not proceed except from that evil one, then if the good deity repels him at once from that evil, it would necessitate that no evil or calamities remain in the world—and that is known to be false.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualistic beliefs that posit two gods—one good and one evil. The author begins by outlining various dualistic views: some say God and Iblis are brothers, with God being good and Iblis evil; some say both are co-eternal and necessary; others say the evil god originated from a thought of the good god; still others identify the gods as light and darkness. Then the author presents a logical dilemma against these views. If the good god is unable to stop the evil god, he is incapable, which disqualifies him from divinity. If he is able but does not stop evil, he is pleased with evil, and thus evil himself—a contradiction to his goodness. If he does stop the evil god, then the evil god becomes incapable, again unfit for divinity, and moreover, if the good god repels all evil at once, no evil should remain in the world, which contradicts observable reality. Thus all dualistic positions are self-defeating.

شيطان
نور
ظلمة
عاجز
راضي بالشر
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's argument only refutes one specific dualist sect.
Correction: The argument applies to all dualistic beliefs that posit two independent deities, as the logical dilemma is based on the structure of the belief, not its details.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what problem arises if the good god is able to repel the evil god but does not do so?
A) The good god would be incapable.
B) The good god would be pleased with evil, thus evil himself.
C) The evil god would become incapable.
D) No evil would remain in the world.

Key Takeaway
The existence of evil does not imply a second evil god; rather, any claim of two independent gods leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that a good God who tolerates evil is complicit in it. When you witness injustice or suffering, do you silently accuse God of being 'pleased with evil'? How must your faith in His wisdom and justice transform your reaction?

Rational Proof for the Creator's Existence
Rational division and proof for the Creator's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational division leads to the proof of a Creator who is powerful, wise, and free.

Core Idea
Rational division proves that the world's events require a free Creator.

This passage presents a rational argument for the existence of the Creator. It begins by asserting that reason itself divides all possibilities into four categories. Then, by observing the contingent events of the world, we infer the need for an originator. Additional proofs show that this originator must be a free agent, establishing a powerful and wise God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument unfolds from a general rational division to specific proofs.
The phrase 'these four divisions' refers to a previous rational classification.
2
Identify the key inference from events
The passage states that 'the events of the world indicate their need for an originator.' Ask yourself: why do contingent events require an external cause?
This is the core of the cosmological argument.
3
Notice the shift from originator to free agent
The passage references proofs that the God of the world must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar). Understand that this distinguishes the Creator from impersonal causes.
This establishes God's will and choice.
4
Conclusion: what is established?
The passage concludes that 'it is established for this world: a powerful, wise God.' Reflect on how the attributes of power and wisdom follow from the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : فهذه الأقسام الأربعة حاصلة بمقتضى تقسيم العقل . ثم الدليل دل على وجودها : أما البارى تعالى . فنقول : حوادث العالم تدل على افتقارها إلى المحدث . والدلائل التي نقلناها عن القائلين بأن إله العالم يجب أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً ، دالة على كونه فاعلا بالاختيار . فقد ثبت لهذا العالم : إله قادر حكيم
They said: These four divisions are obtained by the requirement of the division of the intellect. Then the proof indicates their existence. As for the Creator, exalted: We say: The events of the world indicate their need for an originator. And the proofs that we have transmitted from those who say that the God of the world must be a free agent indicate that He is an agent by choice. So it is established for this world: a powerful, wise God.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage first asserts that the four divisions are derived by rational division. Then it provides proof for the existence of the Creator: the contingent events of the world require a cause (al-muhdith). It also references proofs that the Creator must be a free agent (fa'il mukhtar), establishing that He is powerful and wise.

Two Arguments for the Creator as a Free Agent
The author provides two arguments for the existence of the Creator as a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves the existence of the Creator using two distinct arguments: from the contingency of the world and from the nature of a free agent.

Core Idea
The world's contingencies and the need for a free agent point to a powerful, wise Creator.

In this passage, the author presents two concise yet powerful arguments for the existence of the Creator. The first appeals to the observable contingency of the world, while the second builds on theological proofs that the Creator must act by choice. Together, they establish that the world has a God who is both powerful and wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the structure: a statement of proof, then two arguments, then a conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the first argument
Find the first argument: the contingencies of the world indicate need for an originator. This is a classic kalam argument for a creator.
Consider what 'contingencies' (حوادث) means.
3
Identify the second argument
Find the second argument: transmitted proofs that the God of the world must be a free agent indicate that He acts by choice.
This distinguishes the Creator from a blind necessary cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion combines both: the world has a God who is powerful (able to create) and wise (acting by choice).
Reflect on why 'powerful and wise' are the attributes concluded.

The Soul: Distinct from Body and Eternal
The author argues that the soul is distinct from the body and proves its eternity via an argument about the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the soul is distinct from the body and eternal, based on the nature of the free agent and the need for an ignorant precursor.

Core Idea
The soul's eternity is proven by the need for an ignorant agent to precede God's first act.

This lesson presents a unique proof for the soul's existence and eternity, drawing on the distinction between a free agent and a necessary cause. The author outlines two stations: first, that the soul is not the body, and second, that it is eternal. The key argument for eternity revolves around the impossibility of an eternal act from a free agent, requiring a prior ignorant agent—the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two stations
Read the passage and note that the proof is divided into two stations: first, proving the soul is distinct from the body; second, proving it is eternal. Understand that the first station is only briefly mentioned.
The author references well-known philosophical proofs for the first station but does not reproduce them.
2
Understand the free agent vs necessary cause
The second station's argument hinges on the distinction between a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار) and a necessary cause (موجب بالذات). A free agent acts by intention and choice, so its act cannot be eternal.
This distinction is crucial for the entire argument.
3
Trace the reasoning to an originated act
Since God is a free agent, his act must be originated (محدث) and preceded by non-existence. This implies that God became an agent at a certain time after not being an agent from eternity.
4
Grasp the role of the ignorant agent
The beginning of God's act requires a preceding ignorant agent (فاعل جاهل) who started the act in ignorance. This agent is the soul, and its necessity implies the soul's eternity.
This is the core of the proof for the soul's pre-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات النفس ففيه مقامان : الأول : إثبات أن النفس غير البدن ، ودلائل الفلاسفة في إثبات هذه النفس مشهورة . والمقام الثاني : إثبات أنها قديمة. والدليل على قدمها : أنه لما ثبت بالدليل أن إله العالم فاعل بالاختيار ، لا موجب بالذات ، امتنع أن يكون فعله أزلياً . لأن الفاعل بالاختيار هو الذي يفعل بواسطة القصد والاختيار ومن كان فعله بواسطة القصد ، فإن فعله يكون محدثاً مسبوقاً بالعدم ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فإنه لا بد وأن يصير فاعلا ، بعد أن كان غير فاعل من الأزل ، إلى ذلك الوقت. فإنه يستحيل أن يبتدىء بالفعل في ذلك الوقت ، إلا بعد أن يتقدمه فاعل جاهل ، شرع في الفعل بجهله ، وثبت أن ذلك الفاعل الجاهل هو النفس ، وهذا يقتضي أن النفس يجب أن تكون قديمة .
As for the proof of the soul, there are two stations: The first: proving that the soul is other than the body, and the proofs of the philosophers in proving this soul are well-known. The second station: proving that it is eternal. The proof of its eternity: since it has been established by proof that the God of the world is a free agent, not a necessary cause by essence, it is impossible that His act be eternal. Because the free agent is the one who acts by means of intention and choice, and whoever's act is by means of intention, then his act is originated preceded by non-existence, and everything that is such, then it must become an agent after having been non-agent from eternity until that time. For it is impossible that He begin the act at that time unless preceded by an ignorant agent who started the act in his ignorance, and it has been established that that ignorant agent is the soul, and this necessitates that the soul must be eternal.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the soul in two stations (مقامان). The first station is proving that the soul is distinct from the body (غير البدن). The author merely notes that the philosophers' proofs for this are well-known and does not elaborate. The second station is proving that the soul is eternal (قديمة). The argument proceeds as follows: It has been established that the God of the world is a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار), not a necessary cause (موجب بالذات). Therefore, His act cannot be eternal (أزلي). A free agent acts by intention (قصد) and choice (اختيار), so His act is originated (محدث) and preceded by non-existence. Thus, He becomes an agent at a certain time after not being an agent from eternity. For this to happen, there must be a preceding 'ignorant agent' (فاعل جاهل) who started the act in his ignorance (بجهله). That ignorant agent is identified as the soul. Therefore, the soul must be eternal (قديمة) to serve as the substrate for the first act. This is a unique argument for the pre-existence of the soul.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for the soul's eternity is standard Islamic theology.
Correction: This is a distinctly philosophical argument (from the philosophers) and is not widely accepted in mainstream Islamic kalam. It relies on the concept of an 'ignorant agent' which is not part of orthodox theology.

Checkpoint

B) The void

Key Takeaway
The soul must be eternal because it serves as the ignorant agent necessary for God's first originated act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The argument forces you to see your soul as eternal. How does that change your view of death—not as an end, but as a return to a pre-existent reality? What accountability does that place on your choices today?
?
You are more than your body. Your soul has a history before this life. How does that truth confront the way you live now—attached to the physical world, ignoring the eternal dimension of your own self?

The First Doubt: Impossibility of a Specific Time for Creation
Razi presents the first sophism against the possibility of a specific time for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first sophism against the possibility of God beginning creation at a specific time, and the dilemma it poses regarding the property of the chosen time.

Core Idea
God's choice of a specific creation time seems arbitrary unless the time has a unique property.

Razi presents an objection from opponents of the Kalam position. They argue that if God is a free agent who began creating at a specific moment after infinite inaction, that choice appears arbitrary. To press this point, they introduce a sophism focusing on whether the chosen time has a special property. This lesson unpacks that sophism and its implications, showing the dilemma that the Kalam theologian must address.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premises
Read the first part of the passage that establishes two premises: God is a free agent, and a free agent’s action is originated. This leads to the conclusion that God was inactive from eternity until the time of creation.
These premises are foundational to the Kalam cosmological argument.
2
Identify the dilemma
The sophism presents a dichotomy: either the specific time of creation has a special property that makes it suitable, or it does not. The opponent argues that both options lead to problems.
3
Analyze the first horn
If the time does have a property, the opponent then asks: does that property belong to the time by its essence, by a necessary concomitant, or by neither? The first two are rejected because they would imply that all times have that property, leading to creation at every time.
This is a crucial step—understand why essence and concomitant are rejected.
4
Consider the third possibility
The passage cuts off before fully explaining the third option (property not due to essence or concomitant). Reflect on why that option might also be problematic (e.g., leads to infinite regress or arbitrary choice).
This sets up the need for the Kalam theologian to provide an alternative explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : قد ثبت بالدليل : أن إله العالم ليس علة موجبة بالذات ، بـل فاعل بالاختيار ، وثبت : أنه متى كان فاعلاً مختاراً ، واجب أن يكون فعله محدثاً . وإذا ثبت أن فعله محدث، لزم أن يقال : إنه كان تاركاً للفعل من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا يجوز أن يبتدىء الإله الحكيم بالفعل ، بعد أن كان تاركاً له مدة غير متناهية . والذي يدل عليه وجوه من الشبهات : الشبهة الأولى : إنه إما أن يقال : بأن هذا الوقت اختص بخاصية ، لأجلها استحق أن يخصه الفاعل الحكيم بالشروع في التكوين والتخليق فيه وإما أن يقال : إنه لم توجد هذه الخاصية . والأول باطل . لان اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية ، إما أن يكون لذات ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازمه ، أو لا لذاته ، ولا لشيء من لوازم ذاته . والأول والثاني باطلان إذا لو جاز أن يكون ذلك الوقت المعين، موجباً لتلك الخاصية المعينة ، جاز
They said: It has been established by proof that the God of the world is not a necessary efficient cause by essence, but a free agent. And it is established that whenever He is a free agent, it is necessary that His action be originated. And if it is established that His action is originated, then it necessarily follows that He was refraining from action from eternity until that time. And if this is established, then we say: It is not permissible for the Wise God to begin action after having been refraining from it for an infinite duration. And that which indicates this are several aspects of sophisms: the first sophism: Either it is said that this time was distinguished by a property, due to which the Wise Agent deserved to specify it for commencing formation and creation, or it is said that this property did not exist. The first is false, because the specification of that time with that property is either due to the essence of that time, or due to something from its necessary concomitants, or not due to its essence and not due to something from its necessary concomitants. The first and second are false, because if it were permissible that that particular time necessitate that particular property, then it would be permissible
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from opponents of the Kalam position, structured as a syllogism followed by a doubt. It begins by stating two established premises: (1) God is not a necessary cause by essence but a free agent, and (2) a free agent necessarily produces originated actions. From this it follows that God's action is originated, meaning He was inactive from eternity until the time of creation. The opponents then claim it is impermissible for the Wise God to begin acting after infinite inaction. To support this claim, they introduce several sophisms; only the first is given here. The first sophism argues that the specific time of creation must either have a special property that makes it suitable for creation, or not. If it has no property, no reason exists for choosing that time. If it has a property, that property must belong to the time either by its essence, by a necessary concomitant, or by neither. The first two options are rejected: if the time had the property by essence, then every other time would have the same property, leading to creation in all times; if by a necessary concomitant, the same consequence follows. The third option is left unfinished in the text, but the implication is that it would lead to an infinite regress or arbitrary choice. The passage thus sets up a dilemma that the Kalam theologian must address.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار (free agent)
فعل محدث (originated action)
شبهة (sophism)
خاصية (property)
لازم (necessary concomitant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This sophism proves that God cannot create at any specific time.
Correction: The sophism is an objection that the Kalam theologian refutes; it does not itself prove the impossibility but rather challenges the reasonableness of the choice.

Checkpoint
According to the first sophism, why is the first option (the time having a special property) considered false?
A) Because God does not need a property to choose a time.
B) Because the property, if due to the time's essence or its necessary concomitant, would necessitate that all times have that property.
C) Because the property cannot be known by reason.
D) Because the property would make the time eternal.

Key Takeaway
The sophism challenges the reasonableness of God's choice of a specific creation time by arguing that any property of the time leads to absurdity or arbitrariness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God could have created the universe at any point in eternity, does it trouble you that He chose this specific moment for your existence? How does that make you reflect on your own purpose in time?
?
The doubt claims that God's choice of time seems arbitrary without a property. In your life, do you often demand a reason for God's decrees, or do you submit to His will even when you see no external cause? What would it take for you to trust His wisdom fully?
?
If the opponent's logic forces an infinite regress, then any explanation for God's action would lead to an endless chain. How does this challenge your own attempts to fully understand God's decisions? Are you willing to accept that some aspects of divine wisdom are beyond human reasoning?
